1

A Real Perception of the Pinnacle of Jain
Philosophy
Dr. Kumarpal Desai

The book "Anekānta Syādvāda" is as fascinating as the
life story and intellectual world of its author, Shri
Chandulal Shakarchand Shah. A creator’s work, in one
way or another, often reflects their life. Born in 1907
in Vahelal, a village near Ahmedabad, Chandubhai
Shah studied at C.N. Vidyavihar School in Ahmedabad,
which was deeply influenced by nationalist
sentiments. As a result, he naturally imbibed Mahatma
Gandhi’s ideology. He joined the Mazdoor Mahajan
Sangh, an institution founded by Mahatma Gandhi.
This organization is unique in the world, as it brings
together both workers and owners to resolve their
issues through dialogue. Perhaps it was this very idea
that later evolved into his deep philosophical
contemplation on Anekāntavāda - the Jain principle of
pluralism, growing like a tree of spirituality!
In 1930, Shri Chandulal Shah actively participated in
Gandhiji’s famous Dandi March and endured two years
of imprisonment. On the other hand, he also
contributed to the freedom movement by
joining Janmabhoomi as a journalist, writer, and
reporter. Over time, he went on to work for prominent
newspapers such as Free Press Journal and Jai Bharat.
A journalist closely experiences the pulse of the
masses and writes in a style that resonates with the
people. This accessible and engaging style is also
evident in the book Anekānta Syādvāda. Furthermore,
his travels across America and Europe provided him
i

with deep insights into global affairs and scientific
advancements. These experiences are reflected in the
essence of this book.
In 1957, Shri Chandulal Shah's life took a significant
turn. He always had a deep inclination toward
spirituality due to his past influences and association
with saints and sages. However, alongside his religious
tendencies, he was also highly skilled in business and
innovation, earning admiration from everyone around
him. Despite his remarkable success, his mother did
not seem particularly delighted by his achievements.
This thought constantly occupied Chandubhai’s
mind—why was his mother not truly happy, even
though he was making great progress, traveling across
the world, and attaining new accomplishments in
business and research.
One day, when he got the opportunity, Chandubhai
asked his mother, "Mother, aren’t you happy with my
progress?"
His mother replied, "Which mother wouldn’t be happy
with her son’s success? But how can this truly be called
development? How long will the achievements and
wealth you have gained last? All of it is temporary.
Instead, engage in a study that leads to the upliftment
of your soul. If you think about both this life and the
afterlife, ensuring that both are going to be
enlightened, then I will truly be happy."
Chandubhai asked, "Mother, I have no special
knowledge of Sanskrit or Prakrit. How can I read
religious scriptures?"

ii

His mother said, "Go to the Upashraya and ask Maharaj
Saheb. He will guide you to a teacher who can help you
learn."
Chandubhai received the address he was seeking. At
that time, Panyas Pujya Shri Bhadrankarvijayji
Maharaj was residing in the village of Lunava,
Rajasthan. Chandubhai humbly sat at his feet and said,
"My mother has sent me here to gain knowledge that
will illuminate both this life and the next. Please guide
me." Chandubhai possessed a sharp intellect, a deep
capacity to internalize spiritual values and a vast
reservoir of life experiences. At the same time, he
displayed immense humility. Pleased by his devotion,
Guru Panyas Bhadrankarvijayji began teaching him
the sacred Namaskar Mantra Vidya, which filled his
heart with spiritual fervour. It was as if a hidden spring
had emerged from the depths of his soul, bringing forth
an outpouring of devotion and spirituality. Following
his guru’s guidance, Chandubhai diligently studied
Sanskrit and Prakrit scriptures. During his study
of Arihant Vandanavali, a revered Prakrit text, he was
so deeply moved that his heart overflowed with
emotion. Placing the scripture on his head, he danced
in devotion, expressing profound gratitude toward the
monk who had composed it. Like a stream bursting
forth from the earth, his poetic inspiration began to
flow, and he composed verses that continue to inspire
many devotees today. His hymn "Je Chauda
Mahasvapna Thaki Nij Mata Ne Harakhavta"
(Describing the fourteen great dreams that bring joy to
a mother) became a part of Stuti Kalp-lata, drawing
countless faithful into deep devotion for the Divine.
iii

The Gujarati rendition of Arihant Vandanavali,
originally composed by Chirantanacharya (Unknown),
was gifted to us by Chandubhai. The credit for this
undoubtedly goes to his mother, whose guidance led
him toward this spiritual path. Following this, he also
wrote several novels and short stories, some of which
were published in Kalyan magazine from Surendranagar, Dharmachakra periodical through Maftalal
Sanghvi, and by Gandiv Sahitya Mandir, Surat.
Additionally, he translated the Bhaktamar Stotra into
Gujarati and contributed several other literary works.
Shri Chandulal Shah's pen name was "Chandra", and
just like the moon, his writings exude a soothing
serenity that brings indescribable joy to the soul.
In Panasar, in the divine presence of and refuge in
Bhagwan Mahavir, Shri Chandulal Shah observed an
extended period of silence (Maun Vrat) for over a
month. Immersed in deep meditation and constantly
chanting the profoundly powerful Namaskar
Mahamantra, he peacefully departed from this world
on January 24, 1962, at the age of 54. However, his
literary and spiritual contributions continue to guide
society even today. His children have carried forward
his religious devotion, keeping his spiritual legacy
alive. I have personally witnessed this in his daughter,
Smt. Pallaviben Kobawala, and son-in-law, Shri
Ravindra Kobawala. Shri Chandulal Shah, known by
his pen name "Chandra", also facilitated the
republication of his heartfelt Gujarati poetic
translation of the Sanskrit Bhaktamar Stotra, ensuring
that its essence and devotion reach generations to
come.

iv

In Jain literature, especially on philosophical subjects,
it is rare to find books written in a simple,
contemporary language that can be easily understood
by non-Jains. Moreover, books that scientifically
explain complex concepts using relatable analogies are
even rarer. However, in this book, Shri Chandulal Shah
has adopted a unique approach. Instead of directly
presenting Jain's principles, he first introduces
opposing viewpoints, creating curiosity and
engagement. Then, through analogies, real-life
experiences
and
logical
explanations,
he
systematically clarifies the concepts, making the book
accessible and relatable to everyone.
The book begins with a striking statement: "Your
Anekāntavāda is nothing but a big bundle of
contradictions! How can opposite qualities exist in the
same entity?" This bold assertion sets the stage for a
thought-provoking discussion. Shri Chandulal Shah
skilfully
weaves together
his
international
experiences,
scientific
experiments,
diverse
philosophical perspectives, personal growth, and
engaging conversational style, making the book highly
captivating. While discussing Anekāntavāda, he also
explains fundamental Jain concepts, including:
 The five Anuvratas (small vows)
 The five causes
 The five types of knowledge
 The six substances (Dravyas)
 The seven viewpoints (Nayas)
 The eight types of Karma
 The Nine Fundamental Principles (Tattvas)
v

 The power of the Shri Namaskar Mahamantra
At times, he employs humorous analogies, at others,
fictional narratives, and sometimes, logical debates to
clarify his points. Through personal, familial, and
social examples, he demonstrates how Jain's
philosophy can be applied in daily life, making it
beneficial to the entire world. Just as Albert Einstein
introduced the Theory of Relativity to explain the
physical universe, Bhagwan Mahavir, 2,550 years ago,
introduced Anekāntavāda to help us understand
reality and knowledge in our daily lives. By having the
translation of Shri Chandulal Shah’s book we can
visualize the simple yet profound pathway for the
world to grasp real Indian philosophy at its finest.
Acharya Jinbhadrasuri explains the concept of
Anekānta in the following words — 'the disputes and
contradictions remain intact between different
opinions as long as one does not look to the truth
through assimilation and coordination. The dispute
between various opinions is the product of one's own
faults and weaknesses.'
Anekāntavāda and Syādvāda are considered the
pinnacle of Jain philosophy, and this book serves as an
invaluable resource for their study. Having already
been published in Hindi and Marathi, it is now being
made available in English, ensuring that this profound
wisdom reaches a wider audience. This book will act
as a guide not only for scholars but also for ordinary
individuals who may not have direct access to
philosophical texts. With its English translation, Jain
philosophy - especially the concept of Anekāntavāda will become accessible worldwide, allowing more
individuals to benefit from its universal wisdom and
applicability.
********************
vi

Editor’s Note
Muni Muktimandan Vijay

Anekāntavāda
One of the most important principles of Jainism,
Anekāntavāda, lies at the heart of this philosophical
exploration. It teaches that truth is multifaceted and
complex, indicating no single perspective can claim
absolute authority over reality. Instead, truth must be
approached from multiple viewpoints to attain a
comprehensive understanding. This principle is both
intellectually stimulating and profoundly practical. In
a world often divided by rigid opinions and ideological
conflicts, Anekāntavāda serves as a reminder of the
importance of tolerance and open-mindedness. It
encourages the acceptance of diverse viewpoints,
fostering harmony and respect within society. By
embracing Anekāntavāda, we cultivate wisdom,
patience, and a deeper appreciation for diverse
perspectives.

Origin of This Work
The inspiration for this work arose from an
enlightening experience. One day, Gurumaharaj
entrusted me with a book titled ‘Anekānta Syādavāda’
written in Gujarati by Mr. Chandulal S. Shah. He
advised me to write a review for a column in the
esteemed magazine ‘Ṡruta Samvibhāga’. Immersed in
the text, I was deeply moved by the author's profound
knowledge and exceptional presentation. The book
conveyed profound wisdom in a manner that was both
compelling and intellectually stimulating.

vii

A thought emerged: if this valuable knowledge could
be made accessible in English, it would serve as a
beacon of wisdom for contemporary generations. Jain
philosophy, with its logical and scientific approach,
holds great relevance in modern times. However,
linguistic barriers often restrict access to these
profound teachings. I shared this vision with
Gurumaharaj, who, recognizing its significance,
wholeheartedly granted their consent. Thus, the
journey of translating and publishing this work
commenced.
The process presented numerous challenges. Adapting
such a deeply philosophical text while preserving its
essence and authenticity demanded immense
dedication.
Nevertheless,
with
unwavering
determination and the support of well-wishers, the
project steadily progressed. Today, we are honoured
to present this work to a global audience, hoping it will
enlighten and inspire readers as the original text did
for me.

The Aim of This Publication
The primary aim of this publication is to disseminate
the philosophy of Jainism, one of the most scientific
and rational belief systems in human history. Rooted
in non-violence, truth, and the pursuit of knowledge,
Jain teachings offer invaluable insights for cultivating
a life of balance, awareness, and compassion.
This book transcends mere translation; it serves as a
bridge between ancient wisdom and modern
understanding. In an era dominated by material
pursuits and scientific advancements, the need for
ethical guidance and spiritual depth is paramount. Jain
viii

philosophy, with its emphasis on self-discipline, nonattachment, and responsible living, provides a
framework for navigating life's complexities with
clarity and mindfulness.
Furthermore, by presenting these teachings in English,
we aspire to extend their reach beyond linguistic and
cultural boundaries. This work is not merely an
academic endeavor but a sincere effort to illuminate
minds and foster a sense of purpose among readers.
This book highlights this essential principle, urging
readers to apply it in their daily lives, whether in
interpersonal relationships, professional settings, or
personal growth. For gaining in-depth understanding
of Anekāntavāda, the author has also covered other
philosophical aspects like Karma, Guṇ asthānaka etc,
resulting in a comprehensive insight into Jainism.
While preserving the essence of the original text, I have
shortened the explanations whenever deemed
appropriate for the convenience of the readers.

Acknowledgments
The successful completion of this book would not have
been possible without the contributions and blessings
of several individuals. Their unwavering support,
guidance, and dedication played a crucial role in
bringing this vision to life.
Blessings: I extend my heartfelt gratitude to Acharya
Shree Vijay Vimalkirti Suriji, whose blessings have
been a guiding light throughout this journey, and to
Pandit Shree Trailokyamandan Vijayaji, whose
wisdom and encouragement provided the foundation
upon which this work was built.

In addition to these key contributors, I would also like

would like to thank Dr. Kumarpal Desai, who has

Furthermore, I wish to acknowledge the unwavering

the late author, for granting us permission for this
publication. It is your interest and enthusiasm that
make efforts like these meaningful. May this book
inspire reflection, encourage dialogue, and contribute
to a greater understanding of Jain philosophy in the
modern world.
As we present this book to you, we sincerely hope it
serves as a beacon of knowledge, guiding readers
toward deeper understanding and appreciation of Jain
philosophy. The teachings enclosed within these pages
are timeless, offering wisdom that transcends
generations. May this book inspire not only intellectual
curiosity but also a commitment to self-growth, ethical
living, and universal compassion.

Translator’s Note
Ms. Nimisha Vora

Change manifests in many ways, often gently,
sometimes profoundly; and when it leads us toward
growth, it becomes a journey worth remembering. For
me, that journey began with Anekānta Syādvāda.
As someone unfamiliar with the many nuanced layers
of Jainism, I began this project as an amateur. But
through the process of reading and translating this
book, I found myself not only connecting more deeply
with the faith, but also with my own self, and with the
people who walked this path with me.
At its core, this book is about perspective. In writing
about it, I also ended up shaping my own. It allowed
me to look at things more mindfully, to reflect more
openly, and to begin understanding not only the
profound values of Jainism, but also the principles I
hold within myself.
I am deeply grateful to the Late Mr. Chandulal S. Shah,
not only for bringing this powerful work of literature
to life, but for creating something so meaningful and
impactful. My heartfelt thanks to Acharya Shree Vijay
Vimalkirti Suri and Pandit Shree Trailokyamandan
Vijay for their blessings and for guiding me through
some of the more intricate concepts, offering both
insight and inspiration throughout.
To Muni Shree Muktimandan Vijay, I owe my deepest
gratitude, for his unwavering faith, guidance, and
constant encouragement. And to my family and
everyone who stood by me during this journey, thank
xi

you, this would not have been possible without your
support.
Translating Anekānta Syādvāda has been more than a
project, it has been a personal transformation. One
that has helped me grow, reflect, and move closer to
the person I aspire to become.
********************

xiii

CHAPTER 1

The Quest for Truth
"This Anekāntvāda of yours is completely and
absolutely fake! If you are talking about one thing in
particular, how can there be a reciprocal of mutual
and opposite properties? This is only the luxury of the
intellectual. There cannot be a coexistence between
poison and nectar; this fact is understood even by a
small child. Then, how do you plan on unloading your
Anekāntvāda on the learned and intelligent men?"
While meeting at one of the luxurious hotels in New
York City, I heard these remarks from my scholar
friend.
Lines of joy and sorrow appeared on my face while
hearing these outbursts of criticism that were part of
my conversation.
Both of us were discussing the topic of philosophy.
This friend of mine was extremely intelligent and
curious. While discussing this topic, I mentioned Jain
philosophy. As soon as I mentioned it, he spoke those
few words that made me stop talking.
"As per the Hindu mythology, through the union of
deities and demons, both poison and nectar were
brought forth. I am sure you would know about this."
I asked.
"Yes." He answered.

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

"You can't disagree that both poison and nectar were
in the sea at the same time." I said.
Hearing this, my friend fell into a trance of thoughts
and started thinking. Letting him dwell further on
these thoughts, I asked him a second question:
"You are regarded as a scholar. People also consider
you a knowledgeable man as well as a pandit, is that
true?"
"Of course. That is true. I have acquired my
knowledge and scholarship after years of study.
There is nothing unrealistic or untrue about people
believing me to be a scholar." He replied.
"Okay well, what if someone says you don't have any
shred of knowledge, you are completely dumb, how
would you count that statement as?" I asked again.
"Absurd - Ridiculous - False." He answered
immediately.
Hearing his answer, I smiled again. Then, slowly I told
him:
"That is not absurd. What if I can prove it right now?"
"You are most welcome to. I invite you to prove it."
"Good. See, you are a professor of Sanskrit. Your
knowledge of Sanskrit is so rich that today, in any of
its topics, you can hold your own stand against
anyone. But you haven't studied the Latin Language.
If you go to any Latin-speaking country, even though
there is water under the bed, about which you are
unaware, would you or would you not have to die of
thirst? From that perspective, as far as the Latin
language is concerned, you are completely
2

The Quest for Truth

uneducated. Thus, would you be considered dumb or
not? Would the same stand true for languages like
French, German, Russian, etc., or not?"
Upon hearing this, he fell into his thoughts. He
thought for a moment and then answered:
"Yes, if seen from this perspective, then what you do
say stands to be true."
"Then, you cannot call Anekāntvāda a scam or
something fake, you are one and only, but, from one
perspective you are a scholar, and, from another, you
are a dumb illiterate person. Will you admit the
contradictory statements that you are both a scholar
and not one at the same time?"
It seemed to me that my learned friend had gained
some insight on this matter. He left after promising to
stay in touch and keep up correspondence. While
leaving, he assured me that he would read and
contemplate even more on this topic.
Another such event took place in New York City. This
time, I was gifted with a brilliant student. As soon as
the topic came up, he said:
"In this age known as the twentieth century, if one
doesn't accept rationalism and science, no one will
accept them. In today's world, what comes without
proof from the lab, what is not understood by
intelligence, hardly anyone would be willing to accept
that. Old myths and ideas that are incomprehensible
are useless to discuss about and hold no meaning."
This gentleman was a native of Gujarat, India. After
completing his studies abroad, he came to America
3

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

for further education in the sciences. He then began
to give me a detailed understanding of rationalism.
"Go on, give me some examples." I encouraged him to
take his conversation forward.
"Look, back then, some people used to say that the
earth wasn't round like the orange but rather like a
plate. On top of that, they say that the earth is
stationary and does not move. Now, the earth is
round like an orange and revolves, that fact is proved
by scientists today. What do you have to say about
this?"
Instead of answering this question, I removed a silver
dollar coin from my pocket. I put the silver dollar in
his hand and asked:
"What kind of a round is this?"
"This can be called a plate-shaped round." He replied.
I then took the coin from his hand. On the table, with
the first finger of my left hand, I pressed the coin and
held it upright. Then, with the first finger of my right
hand, I thrust the back of the coin off the table, lifted
my left hand, and left the coin rotating on the table.
The dollar started spinning on the table by itself. At a
good pace, the coin started spinning like a top.
Showing that I asked him:
"Tell me, which round does this dollar resemble?
Does it look like an orange now?"
"This is an illusion." He replied.
"Then what is the guarantee that science has not used
the means of illusion to conclude whether the earth is
4

The Quest for Truth

round like that of an orange or that of a plate?" I
asked.
My friend fell into his thoughts. Letting him linger in
his thoughts for a while, I again asked him another
question:
"You have traveled a lot by train. When the train is
moving at a certain speed, and we look out at the
roads, trees, etc. they all appear to be running along
as well. When in reality, everything is stationary and
fixed in its position. Yet, it looks as though it is
dynamic and running. Is what I said true or not?"
"Yes, it seems so."
"Similarly, if two trains stand parallel to each other at
a station and when the train behind starts moving, it
looks like the train we are sitting in is the one that is
moving forward. When we look at the opposite side
and notice that everything is standing still and as it is,
it is then that we realize the other train is the one
moving and not ours. At that point, we are convinced
that no, it is the train behind us that is moving and not
ours. You might have experienced this."
"But, all of this is an illusion. Not a reality." He
answered.
"It was later that we realized that it was an illusion.
Can you say for sure that everything that science has
said or shown us is not an illusion or based on it?" I
asked.
"How can you say this? In the past, the findings that
were then considered true have been proven false
today. Moreover, what was once considered an
impossible idea has today become a possibility. There
5

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

is constant research and exploration that is going on
today. What was wrong then is still proved to be
wrong today but, through precise rational and
experimental proportions. New discoveries today are
also the result of intelligence and experimentation."
He answered.
"Is intelligence complete or infinite?" I asked in
return.
"How can that be said?"
"And if you rely only on incomplete human intellect,
then it is nothing, but refusal born out of egoism."
Hearing this, he fell into a trance. Seeing him
confused, I explained further, intending to clarify.
"It's not that I don't believe in intelligence or the
revolution that has occurred in science. I don't mean
to say that this is useless. I do believe that science has
developed drastically, and it continues to progress
each day. There are concepts and fundamentals given
by omniscient Tīrthaṅkaras that are treasured in our
Jain Āgamas. Yet, today's science has not advanced to
the extent that it can be challenged. On the contrary,
the roots of all important discoveries and innovations
in the field of science lie in the narration of the
Tīrthaṅkaras. What I truly mean to say is that human
beings today have the intellect to use for the further
development of their wisdom. If that happens, the
evolving intellect will arrive close to perfection at
some point. But, on the journey toward the
destination, a lot of things are not accepted by the
intellect. Instead of denying it, forgoing it, or shoving

6

The Quest for Truth

it away, even if we choose to take refuge in faith there;
there's absolutely nothing wrong with that."
"I do somewhat understand what you are trying to
say." He said.
"There lies a risk in accepting what is understood and
in that what is seen. Because it is not impossible that
what we understand and what is seen is based on
illusions rather than the truth. Hence, what we can do
is simply follow the statements of the omniscient
Tīrthaṅkaras for the matters that we understand. And
for what we don't understand, we can simply stick to,
'This is not understood by me.' Accepting it is the
safest way here. Saying, 'This is wrong because it is
not intelligible; because science has not stamped on
this; this is useless' is not the desirable thing to do so."
I spoke.
"What you are talking about is spiritual. Science on
the other hand is experimenting with materialistic
things. Why would you mix science with spirituality
and combine them both?" My friend gave birth to a
new argument.
"A very few people would believe today that spiritual
and material things are independent of each other. If
you look at it from a certain point of view, both of
them differ. Science claims that it exists for the benefit
of mankind. The spiritual gurus of the world
themselves speak while keeping in view the interest
and welfare of mankind. The difference lies in its
imagination and understanding. In the roots, welfare
lies in both spiritual science and material science." I
replied.
7

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

"But, if we only think about the material things,
wouldn't that differ from the way we think about
spiritual things?" He raised his doubts.
"No. Both are one. Even so, if we consider only
material things, the evolution of science is in its
primary stages. Prof. Einstein had put forward the
theory of relativity and the entire world has been
amazed. But this said theory of relativity had already
been explained in the Jain philosophy thousands of
years ago. Why does no one pay attention to that?"
"Is this relativity in the Jain philosophy?" He asked.
"The philosophy of Jainism is based on relativity." I
replied to him with an answer.
"Now I have to read about your philosophy." He said.
"Don't say it as mine but as ours. Have you forgotten
that you're a Jain by birth too?"
Being a little embarrassed, he expressed his gratitude
and left.
Like the above-mentioned events, the author
experienced a similar third event while traveling in
America. This time, he had a conversation with an
American friend of his. He was extremely interested
in Religion and Philosophy.
After discussing it for a while, he told me:
"You talk in a way that suggests Jainism and the Jain
philosophy are complete, absolute, and allcomprehensive."
"No doubt. I do believe so." I answered.

The Quest for Truth

"12-15 lakhs." I replied.

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

"Religion and the number of adherents of religion are

Where will I find this list? It was thanks to this

The Quest for Truth

The world outside has an ignorance towards the Jain
religion and philosophy. With a heavy heart, I
returned to India. This matter stayed as it was and
years passed by. Coming back to my homeland, I see
that ignorance in the matters of Jain Philosophy still
prevailed, and that in itself was no wonder. What was
surprising was that the people belonging to the Jain
community themselves were unaware of the matters
of their own philosophies. When I tried to tell them
that the Jain religion doesn't believe that our God is
the creator and did this, I was laughed at and called
an 'atheist' multiple times. This left me shocked and
overwhelmed.
However, I experienced a deeper sadness and shock
when I realized my own inferiority.
I used to talk about such big things about Jain religion
and Jain philosophy at different places. Like a
minister of a deserted village, while spreading
information about Jain philosophy at various places, I
stunned a lot of people. I believed that I held immense
knowledge and information on this topic.
After meeting with Jain monks, Panyāsaji Mahārāja,
and Ācārya Mahārāja, I engaged in deep philosophical
discussions with them. During these conversations, I
gathered special insights, which made me realize that
my understanding of the subject was like a single
drop in the vast ocean of knowledge. I had been so
captivated by that small droplet, but then it struck me
that much like a queen's graceful walk can deceive
with its charm, I too was deceiving people by the
fascination of it. This realization left me standing
there, speechless and in awe.

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

In my quest for search, I came across a 'Gurudeva.' I

have had the courage to write this.
None of the books I read were in Sanskrit or Prakrit.

I kept receiving continuous guidance from Guruji

Though there were certain texts written in modern

When I was in Indore, he sent a book for me to read.
In the same context, he had shared a letter with me.
An extract from it was:
'…This is the third edition of the book I've sent you.
Once you go through it and read the section on
Syādvāda, you'll notice how unclear, vague, and
confusing it is for the reader. However, there are
scattered truths about Syādvāda within the text. Your
task is to find these truths and create content that

The Quest for Truth

presents them clearly and accurately. Make sure the

It was during my trips to America and Europe that I

clear way that was understood by the readers. I had a
growing spirit in my heart to do this. Pujya Gurudeva

Coincidentally after that, I wasn't in touch with

CHAPTER 2

Perspectives and Paradoxes
Before discussing Jain Philosophy further, let us
ponder upon a few basic topics.
In today's world, there are contradictions around all
four sides of us: in what we see, what we know, and
what we learn about, isn't that so? Are these
contradictions a lie or an illusion?
What one sees and understands is different from
what is seen and understood from another person's
perspective. Hence, are the ones who see and those
who don't, the ones who understand and those who
don't, wrong? Only after indulging in a conversation
with them, will we realize that every person
considers themselves right and the other one wrong.
Years ago, Pandit Jawaharlal Nehru had met the
Prime Minister of the Commonwealth in London,
where he said:
"If every citizen of the world is favoring one side of
the argument in a democracy, if all of their opinions
are the same, then there will be nothing like
democracy that exists in this world."
This is something that holds a lot of meaning and
holds great importance for understanding it. A
realistic assessment of the basic nature lies in the
same.
14

Perspectives and Paradoxes

Spiritual Philosophy in itself is about something
similar. Through this, we get to see multiple
ideologies. As long as the world exists, the differences
will remain. Since time immemorial, these differences
have been existent and until eternity, the differences
will stay. The existence of the world has stayed
dynamic because of the differences and debates that
surround it. Say if the differences come to an end,
then the progress pulls a break there. Debates make
life enjoyable and worth living. Since time
immemorial, a lot of great personalities have been
trying to put an end to these debates and come to a
common conclusion, which is a matter of pride. Yet,
we see that instead of reaching a low scale of
disagreements, they have only increased.
But there is no need to feel distressed about it. Just as
controversies and debates are eternal, so is man's
curiosity to know "What is the truth?". Human
curiosity has been preserved for eternity and will live
forever. Until the curiosity of seeking the truth
doesn't disappear from the human mind, its
development won't stop.
All the philosophies that exist in the land of the world
today, have been derived as a result of the continuous
development of human curiosity. By expressing our
gratitude and thanking the growing curiosity of the
human mind to be on a constant search to explore
something new, see new things, and learn about
unknown topics, if we move ahead, the first seed of
thought that gets planted is to know that out of
everything discovered through the human curiosity,
how much of it is actually true?

15

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

This question is very complicated. For all the effort
put behind satisfying curiosity, we wonder who made
these efforts, how much effort did they invest, how
did they do it, how much time was dedicated to this?
We must think about all of these aspects. While
contemplating this, we will definitely come across
thoughts that point out the imperfections, limitations,
and the egotistical behavior of the human intellect.
In the pursuit of scientific discovery, when a man
finds a solution, he swells with pride and inflates his
ego. His friends, colleagues, and he himself will start
beating drums loudly to celebrate the discovery. The
same thing has been happening in the spiritual realm
for a long time. Oftentimes, few seekers seek the path
of maṅtras for self-realization, chanting, yoga,
meditation, etc. They opt for that way and sit down on
that path.
As soon as someone even sees a ray of twinkling light,
they label it as 'self-realization' and walk away from
their paths after it. As soon as someone's mental
power or concentration leads to something even
remotely miraculous, they assume their research has
come to an end and celebrate that victory.
In our country, there are two very visible divisions
seen in the spiritual realm. One side is clinging to the
faith they were born with, the inbuilt tradition and
culture that sticks to them even as they are moving
ahead. The opposite side belongs to the ones who
have attained intellectual and modern education.
Upon deeper analysis, we'll realize that there aren't
any major differences between both categories.
Like the difference of opinions that lie when in the
context of the inbuilt faith in a man, the same opinions
16

Perspectives and Paradoxes

lie in firmness for the men who are civilized,
educated, and intelligent. Meeting the trained
followers of Swami Ramakrishna Paramahansa, Shri
Raman Maharshi, Shri Arvind Ghosh, Nityananda
Swami, Swami Ramdas, and many other great men in
different ways, and each of them appeared to believe
strictly and strongly that, "The truth is only in their
pockets and nowhere else." Believing in this is not
enough. They also spread the message that "Everyone
else should accept and follow their beliefs and only then
will they be able to grow else they'll end up in the pits
of hell."
A Persian poet once wrote about the view of the Taj
Mahal from Agra fort. He expressed:
“If there is anything heaven on this earth,
That's it, here it is, here it is.”
We are surrounded by poetry similar to this all
around us. Meet any gentleman who has taken an
interest in spiritual matters. Most people who are
followers of the same opinion keep glorifying their
beliefs.
Naturally, when such a situation appears around us,
our instinct arises and puts forth a question for us,
"What should we believe in?"
In terms of belief, a common opinion is that whatever
we understand or believe in should be vigilant,
logically considerate, rhetorical, and backed up by
arguments. If something is not supported by logic, it
should be considered false and untrue, leading it to be
thrown away.

17

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

In this kind of belief, a very crucial thing is forgotten.
What is considered logic is the same thing that is
considered 'pure' as well as 'perfect', right?
In modern universities, when logical reasoning is
taught, it includes a portion called 'Fallacy of Logic'
which is related to false logic. Logical Reasoning is a
very tricky and confusing subject. If the writer,
teacher, and learner have no conscience, if the mind
is full of prejudice, and not free, and if the three
actions (writing, teaching, and learning) run under
the shadow of egoism, then what are the
consequences?
Let us consider the logic of mathematics. A river was
filled with water up to a depth of 2.5 times the height
of a man. To cross the river, five men asked how much
water each of them would need to encounter. A
mathematics expert calculated that each man would
encounter water up to half his height - reaching their
waists.
Now if the five men had accepted this calculation and
went ahead to walk in the river and cross it, what
would the result have been? All of them would have
drowned. From the viewpoint of mathematics, this
was logic without error. But, in practice, it can be
fatal. This is false logic. It is irrational if it is followed.
Let's take another example.
The walls of one random building are painted in
white. In it, green light bulbs are placed. When the
lights come on, the green light is reflected on the
walls. Now, the white walls appear green at that time.
There is bound to be a difference of opinion between
people who see these walls during the day and then
during the night. One would say that the color of the
18

Perspectives and Paradoxes

walls is white. Another one would disagree and say
that the walls are green in color. Both these opinions
are considered true as well as false at the same time.
The third person who knows this, the occupant of the
house, reveals this only when they meet. The result is
that both are right, concerning different times of the
day, despite their contradictory beliefs. The
realization of that happens only when both look at the
walls both during the day and at night. Until then, is
there any surprise in both remaining firm in their
beliefs?
Let's talk about what is visible to the naked eye. There
are four people standing on top of a mountain. One
person has a cataract in his eye and is not wearing
glasses. The next one too has a cataract in his eye but
he is wearing glasses. The third eye is free from any
kind of disease. The fourth eye is good but in addition,
he has a pair of binoculars.
When all four of them look at the horizon, will all of
them come in one line? Will everyone be able to see
the same thing?
During that time, when we ask them to describe what
they are seeing, we'll be getting different
explanations from each of them.
The same is true for logic. The understanding,
intelligence, knowledge, experience, and power of the
different people will always overlap and differ. So,
what can be considered pure logic?
We are still exploring this logic in anticipation of
global understanding and human intelligence. We
have not yet come to the logic of philosophy. One
must be patient till we reach there.

CHAPTER 3

Beyond Judgement
By now, we recognize the necessity of using logic to
gain a deeper understanding. In this process, we've
also acknowledged that misunderstandings and
misconceptions can arise. Now, let's take a step
forward.
Let's imagine the setup of a court. The Judge, Plaintiff,
Defendant, Plaintiff's Counsel, Defendant's counsel,
the legislators, the members of the jury, and the
people who came to hear the case with interest- the
audience, are the seven types of people that are
gathered here. The case is ongoing, witnesses of both
sides have been examined, arguments have been
presented by both the parties and the judge directs
the jury to present the opinion they have unitedly
reached at. After that, the final decision is made by the
judge.
How does the judge deliver their judgment? They sit
at the threshold of justice, embodying knowledge of
the law, experience, and a deep respect for dignity
and integrity. When legal proceedings commence, the
judge occupies an impartial, unbiased, pure, and
pious throne.
He is quite neutral, unprejudiced, uncorrupted, and
considers every argument and evidence presented in
the court, remains within the limits of the established
law, and makes decisions based on the assumptions
20

Beyond Judgement

that lead to the conclusion of what is right. We walk
on the belief that this judge is completely just and fair
and dedicated to his service and duty.
Even so, is every opinion given fair and just? When an
appeal is made in the higher courts, we've seen many
judgments changing their course and taking a reverse
from the original opinion. Does it always happen that
a judgment turned around as such would still be fair
and just? We often get to hear that the ones who seek
justice do not receive it. Have we not seen scenarios
where the innocent are punished and the guilty
released?
The doors of a court are knocked upon in the wake of
a dispute. There are many reasons behind these
quarrels. But if we analyse we'll be able to see the
main reason as ignorance, false knowledge
(misunderstanding), prejudice, egoism, possessiveness, blindness, selfishness, irrationality, slavery of
lusts and intolerance, etc.
The greatest misfortune is that few of us are willing
to acknowledge that these qualities or at least one of
them exists within us. Confession seems distant, and
many hardly pause to reflect on it. We often hear
people claim they have been misunderstood or
misjudged. Yet, when we ourselves misunderstand or
misinterpret someone else, how many of us are ready
to admit it or even consider it?
Do we really need to look far to see animosity,
conflict, strife, and quarrels among friends, business
associates, or within families - between husbands and
wives, siblings, mothers-in-law and daughters-in-law,
fathers and sons, or even neighbors?
21

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Ignorance of the truth, misunderstanding, having an
opinion beforehand i.e a prejudice, an egoism which
makes you state that you are better and different than
everyone else, inconsiderate selfishness, lack of
understanding which leads to indiscretion while
thinking about pure and lawful logic, the helplessness
because of the lack of control over our senses, the
stubbornness i.e an attitude to never bend and the
intolerance born from it - between all of this, the
human society has sunk and lost itself.
Generally, a man works better with logic at his front.
Here, logic does the work of a horse. He leads thought
and action by staying ahead. But, in many places, logic
takes the appearance of a carriage rather than a
horse.
In such moments, a person often acts impulsively,
driven by passion, selfishness, and desire. To justify
these actions, they then construct arguments and
conclusions. Here, the decision and action come first,
and logic is used merely to validate them. In this
context, logic becomes the carriage rather than the
horse. Just as a carriage cannot run without a horse,
the logic employed afterward is not true reasoning
but rather a guise for selfishness and mere
argumentation.
Sometimes, we've seen and experienced that when
two people are in a discussion, sharing their own
opinions and having a debate based on it, they're just
talking with their ears closed and are unresponsive to
the opposite person's opinions. When one person is
putting forth his point, the other one, instead of
listening to it, is thinking of new points to support his
22

Beyond Judgement

argument. At such times, there is a lot of irrelevant
discussion happening.
You will easily understand that this is not the path to
finding and gathering the truth. Then, where do we
look for the truth? And how?
The way Jain philosophy has answered these
questions, nobody else has understood it or answered
it in the same way. The answer to these questions is
found through Anekāntvāda and Syādvāda. To my
knowledge, no other philosophy could impart to us
the knowledge like the lessons given by Anekāntvāda
and Syādvāda in Jain philosophy.
The core principle of this philosophy is to avoid
viewing things from a single perspective and instead
recognize that there are multiple angles to consider.
Many have mentioned the importance of looking at
the other side of the shield; however, that perspective
is often limited by our imagination and can only
present two sides. Nonetheless, the previously
mentioned perspectives remain relevant and
important in this broader understanding.
Hence, the Jain philosophers have always insisted
that despite a sharp intellect, as long as neutrality
doesn't manifest itself, the complete truth is not
achieved until then. That's why they say that the
cultivation of neutrality is the first necessity.
To cultivate neutrality, true knowledge should be
acquired
by
removing
ignorance.
The
misunderstanding which is falsehood should be
thrown away. All types of prejudice-born opinions
must be locked away. You have to be free from your
ego. You should be far away from blind selfishness,
which has materialistic interests and is directly
23

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

linked to your senses. Complete freedom must be
obtained from the slavery of instincts and sensual
lusts. Discretion must be fully awakened, and you
must cultivate a feeling of compassion and friendship
towards the world, the supreme flame of tolerance
must be kindled.
That's a big statement made, isn't it?
The way to understand, find and live the absolute
truth, has been shown by the Jain Tīrthaṅkaras, and
that fact has been accepted by even the scholars of
Eastern and Western philosophies. The way that has
been shown to us by the almighty Tīrthaṅkaras, no
one can stop us from seeking refuge in it.
In scientific laboratories, we observe that various
types of experiments are conducted simultaneously.
Once research on one aspect is completed, additional
studies build upon that foundation, leading to
ongoing investigations and discoveries. Now, one of
the most important things to be understood here is
that it is not mere fiction or a baseless imagination
behind all the experiments conducted at the scientific
laboratories. Behind all of this, there's something,
something even more special than what we know,
and such 'faith' strengthens itself and stands strong.
Where does such faith of 'there is something'
originate from?
This is something to be thought deeply about. It is a
definite thing, no matter where it comes from. The
implication of this is that 'What is not seen and
understood still definitely exists.' This faith in its
existence is the root of all the research. You cannot
help but accept it.
24

Beyond Judgement

When researchers begin their experiments on the
power of faith in the laboratory, they must rely on
logic. Any experiment based on faulty reasoning is
likely to fail, prompting researchers to revisit and
refine their logic. Ultimately, when they arrive at
sound reasoning, they find that true achievement and
success follow.
This principle extends beyond material concerns; in
every aspect of life, individuals must rely on pure
logic. Not everyone can access pure logic in the
laboratory of their minds. When they do, they can
move forward, overcoming their sins and reconciling
both material and spiritual aspects of existence. This
understanding can be revealed through Omniscience,
and we must seek refuge in it. Regardless of the truth
of divine teachings, the curiosity to explore and
understand them can guide us further along our path.
When a small kid first goes to school, he doesn't know
how 14 x 3 = 42. The uneducated parents rely their
faith on the school and the math department.
Whatever the teacher teaches there, the child learns.
15 x 3 = 45, is what he was taught yet, he did not have
a procedural understanding of it. Then, when he's
developed more understanding of the concept, and
he's able to do addition and subtraction, he's then
able to calculate 3 x 5 = 15 by himself and
understands what he learned earlier, and puts it to
application.
When it comes to philosophy, we are like this child
too. If we don't learn about the beginning faithfully,
how will we get the chance to experience reality as it
is?
25

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Someone would question that mathematics is the
only one in the world. How is it the same for
philosophy?
Agreed that there are many philosophies in the world.
Then why kneel to one of Jain's philosophies? Why
not start with someone else? A question like this
would also be asked.
The answers to this deserve careful consideration.
Learned philosophers of Jain philosophy assert that it
is the only philosophy where various perspectives
converge, much like different streams merging into a
river. No other philosophy has made such a claim. So,
what's stopping us from starting from this point?
The Jain philosophers are so convinced and believe
completely that once curiosity is awakened to
develop and gain an understanding of this supreme
philosophy, the beauty and power of this philosophy
will automatically present itself to us.
To fully understand and experience this, there's no
insistence on abandoning vices and faults
immediately. The idea is that once one begins to grasp
this philosophy, those vices and faults will eventually
fade away on their own. The philosophers believe
that as understanding deepens, the negative traits
will naturally diminish over time.
If we want to truly benefit from this philosophy, we
must adhere to one important condition - neutrality.
It's essential to approach the teachings with an open
mind and a neutral perspective. Don't abandon the
journey after just a few steps or leave it unfinished.
Having only this expectation, let's go further.

26

CHAPTER 4

Jainism: A First Look
We talked about neutrality in the last few pages, and
since we've assumed that you already are in a neutral
state, let us now focus on what more we can
understand. Now, here's a question:
Is there a system or method in existence that allows a
person to gain understanding and knowledge,
ultimately leading them to discover and embrace the
absolute truth?
As per the Jain philosophers, the answer to this
question is, yes. They claim that the omniscient
Tīrthaṅkaras, have guided us just so that we could
reach this path of understanding, and that in itself is
unprecedented, wonderful, and complete.
Now, if this is to be understood correctly, we must
take a look at the list of important aspects of Jainism
as a religion and philosophy. We have to quickly
examine the important things and get acquainted
with them. Because, by being briefly introduced to the
method, the process, and the calculations that we
have to see and understand, we'll have better
flexibility in understanding the core aspect of it, the
foundation on which the principles and conducts are
laid down.
Let's understand more about this.

27

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The word 'Jain' means followers of Jina. 'Jina' means
having attained the position of Tīrthaṅkara by
attaining victory over your mind, speech and body.
Tīrthaṅkara means a person who has established a
group viz. Sangha consists of Sādhu, Sādhvī, Śrāvaka
and Śrāvikā. Tīrthaṅkaras are also named Arhat,
Arihaṅta, the all-knowing and, kēvalajñānī. Those
who attain complete knowledge and, before
achieving salvation through the atonement of all their
sins, spread the teachings of this dharma to the world
are revered as 'Jina.' Those who follow their path are
known as 'Jains.'
The word 'Jain' does not indicate or identify any caste
or community. It is an indicator of 'religion.' Jainism
makes no distinction based on caste or community.
Anyone who follows the practices and believes in the
principles of Jainism, regardless of their caste,
community, or country, is recognized and known as a
'Jain.'
Jainism is a completely democratic religion in the way
that the establishment of the Sangha by the
Tīrthaṅkara is a collective power. The Sangha,
comprising its main four elements sādhu, sādhvī,
śrāvaka, and śrāvikā, is so vast that its power is
considered supreme. When the Tīrthaṅkara sits
down to give a sermon (Deṡ anā ), they first bow to this
fourfold Sangha. In Jain Dharma, democracy is fully
embraced, with no discrimination or differences
made between men and women.
Jainism and Jain Philosophy, as the western
philosophers believe, have not even been around for
just a few thousand years. Jain philosophy hasn't
28

Jainism: A First Look

existed since the time of the last Tīrthaṅkara of this
age, Ṡri Mahāvira. In the ongoing period, countless
years have passed since the time of the first
Tīrthaṅkara, Ṡri Ṛ ṡ abhadeva.
The Jain philosophers have divided the entire period
of this into two main sections. One is called Avasarpiṇī
and the other one goes by the name Utsarpiṇī.
Together, they form and finish the circle of time. This
circle of time has been happening since time
immemorial and it will continue to do so until
eternity. There have been countless time circles and
that will keep happening. There is no end to that. We
are in the middle of the current and ongoing
Avasarpiṇī period of the time cycle. These periods of
the time cycle are divided into six sections each and
are termed ārā. We are in the fifth ārā of the current
Avasarpiṇī period.
During the infinite time cycles of the past, and in each
Utsarpiṇī and Avasarpiṇī period of the cycle, it is
believed that there have been 24 Tīrthaṅkara and,
that order is considered eternal. Thus, for Jains, their
religion is believed to be infinite, eternal, and
unchanging. A time cycle is infinite; similarly, Jainism
is eternal and will last forever.
The Jain Philosophy does not believe in the origin or
the end of this universe. The world is considered to be
eternal. In the same way, Jain philosophy does not
consider the god or the brahmatattva as a doer and
maker of this world. As the cycle of time is eternal, so
is the world. If we consider the start and believe
Brahmatattva to be the doer or maker, questions
arise, such as what existed before the start and how
29

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

the doer came into existence. No other philosophy has
been able to give a satisfactory answer for this. This
belief of Jainism is vigilant and united.
Jain philosophy mentions six substances (dravya)
that support the world. These 6 substances are
mentioned below:
i.

ii.

iii.

iv.

Dharmāstikāya: Here, the word 'dharma'
associates its meaning by helping the state of
motion. In this universe, thanks to this Dravya,
things can move from one place to another.
Adharmāstikāya: Here, Adharma means being
still. One needs assistance to be in the state of
motion, similarly one needs something to help
them stay still. Like dharmāstikāya assists in the
state of motion similarly, Adharmāstikāya helps
in being still. Both living and nonliving things are
by themselves as independent doers when it
comes to staying stationary or being in motion.
Yet, even modern scientists believe that they
must have assistance in staying stationary or in
movement. Thus, since time immemorial, Jain
philosophers have presented these two
substances namely, 'dharma' and 'adharma.'
Ākāṡāstikāya: Covering all the other substances,
giving space to them, if there's any one substance
holding the strength for all of that, it is Ākāṡa. This
substance is universally accepted and
acknowledged. The inclusion and unity of all
directions also happen in Ākāṡa. This substance
consists of two parts i.e.
a) lokākāśa and b) alokākāśa.
Pudgalāstīkāya: Pūr and Gam - the unity of these
two terms has led to the formation of the word
30

Jainism: A First Look

v.

vi.

Pudgala. Pūrana and Galana mean meeting and
parting, forming together and falling apart. This
work of Pudgala is of two types - to form and to
part. Out of the existence of all things in the
universe, the creation of every inanimate object
has been happening and will keep happening
because of Pudgala.
Jīvāstikāya: What is not an inanimate object is
believed to be conscious. This nature of
consciousness itself is a substance form. We can
also acknowledge this term by calling it
ātmadravya.
Kāla: Time-period is the 6th substance. Time also
exists independently. The Jain philosophers have
considered this to be an inanimate object.

The entire universe is composed of these six
substances. A living being (Jīvāstikāya) united with
the body (Pudgalāstīkāya) at a given space
(Ākāṡāstikāya) with the help of motion and stillness
(Dharmāstikāya and Adharmāstikāya) does all
activities.
This, according to Jain philosophy, is the structure of
the universe. Here, it is necessary to clearly say that
along with dharma, adharma, ākāśa, Pudgala and jīva,
the word astikāya has been added after every word
intentionally. Asti stands for particle (pradēśa), and
kāya means mass or a group (samūha). All 5 of these
substances are believed to have innumerable
particles and groups. Thus, the word astikāya has
been added to them. Because kāladravya doesn't have
any particles, the word astikāya has not been added
to it.
31

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The Jain philosophers have structured 9 elements
that are based on the above-mentioned 6 substances,
that are useful on the road to salvation. These 9
elements have been explained below:
Jīva: The consciousness of life. This is based on
jīvadravya.
II. Ajīva: Inanimate objects. This includes dharma,
adharma, ākāśa, pudagala and kāla.
III. Puṇya: Good and auspicious deeds.
IV. Pāpa: Bad and sinful deeds.
V. Āṡrava: A path responsible for the karma getting
attached to our soul.
VI. Saṁvara: The action through which we can block
the inflow of karma into our soul.
VII. Nirjarā: Destruction of the karma. This is of two
types:
a. Sakāmanirjarā means that through penance,
meditation, and several other ways we
diminish our karma.
b. Akāmanirjarā means that karma gets destroyed
on its own at reaching the expiration period of
suffering.
VIII. Baṅdha: The bond that is formed with the soul like
the bond that milk forms when absorbed with
water.
IX. Mōkṣa: The soul's liberation after being the karma
has been completely diminished.
I.

Like the 6 substances, these 9 elements too have been
closely observed by the Jain philosophers. Here, only
a brief description of it has been mentioned. If you
have found this topic interesting and something you
are engrossed in, you can learn about it, and get an
understanding of it from the experts on this subject.
32

Jainism: A First Look

With the combination and process of the abovementioned 6 substances and 9 elements, the world
has been functioning since eternity. For any event to
take place, there are 5 rationales described by the Jain
philosophers and these are mentioned below:
i. Kāla: A rationale that defines maturity as well as
the immaturity period of a thing or work. This time
is to be understood as a cause.
ii. Svabhāva: Here, the origin of svabhāva is such that
each thing has its own nature. We can also
recognize it by natural merit or a feature.
iii. Bhavitavyatā: The other name for this is termed
'Niyati' (destiny). It is one of the eternal, infinite,
and independent fundamentals.
iv. Prārbdha: This has a second name as Karma.
What is formed by the actions of the individual
and the community is known as prārbdha.
v. Puruṣārtha: For this, udyama (efforts) is another
term. The efforts made by a living being is
puruṣārtha.
In ancient Jain philosophy, five rationales are
considered essential for completing a task. The
author intends to discuss these rationales in greater
detail in the coming chapters, and this is merely a
brief overview of their significance.
To understand what has been stated above and
certain other things, proper knowledge is required
and the Jain philosophers have divided it into 5 parts.
The 5 different parts are:
i. Matijñāna
ii. Śrutajñāna
iii. Avadhijñāna
33

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

iv. Mānahparyavajñāna
v. Kēvalajñāna
The general and specific understanding of these five
pieces of knowledge will be explained in detail in the
following pages.
To determine if the content stated above is pure and
real knowledge and not fake information, Jain
scriptures have laid out two types of testimonials i.e.
Pratyakṣa and Parokṣa. Testimony means basis,
and evidence. The first testimony has two sections:
Sāmvyavahārika (the practical) and Pārmārthika (the
transcendental). Whereas, there are three sections of
the second type: Anumāna (conjecture), Upamāna
(analogy) and Āgama (scriptures).
To conclude the testimonials stated above, Jain
philosophers have mentioned about 4 instruments
which are known as Dravya (matter), Kṣetra (space),
Kāla (time) and Bhāva (characteristic). We can also
call them four bases. This is known by the term
Catuṣtaya. We'll gather a more in-depth
understanding of this in the further chapters.
The understanding of the above and other matters
that have been given in Jain philosophy is often
recognized by the terms 'Anekāntavāda,' 'Syādvāda,'
and 'Sāpēkṣavāda.' This philosophy has been given to
us by the omniscient Tīrthaṅkaras.
The method that was devised to understand the
philosophy of Syādvāda was recognized by the term
'Naya'. We can understand this as Relative
Knowledge. There are two main divisions of Naya:

34

Jainism: A First Look

i. Dravyārthīka (related to object): It means the one
which explains the general form of the object
ii. Paryāyārthika (related to form): That is, the one
which explains the special form of the object
The names of 7 Naya are mentioned below:
i. Naigama
ii. Saṅgraha
iii. Vyavahāra
iv. Ṛjusūtra
v. Śabda
vi. Samabhirūdha
vii. Evambhūta
Out of the seven, the first three fall into Dravyārthīka
(related to object) category, and the last four are of
the Paryāyārthika (related to form) category. We will
go into further detail in the coming chapters. But the
philosophy of these 7 Nayas is something that has to
be taken into understanding. For the practice of
religion, Jain philosophers have divided it into two
sections.
i. Niścaya Naya (related to ultimate goal)
ii. Vyavahāra Naya (related to a path of ultimate
goal)
In the seven Nayas mentioned above, both the general
and specific aspects of object and form are
emphasized. Jain philosophers have placed special
importance on remembering and understanding
these teachings, which are as follows:
"In this world, there is nothing special without the
ordinary and there is nothing ordinary without it
being special."
35

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

This subject is very interesting and we will receive
more understanding about it in the following pages.
The Jain philosophers have prepared a chart to better
understand everything that has been mentioned
above and all the other things of importance. We have
given it the name of Saptabhaṅgī. The core of
Syādvāda's philosophy lies in the Saptabhaṅgī. A lot
of people get agitated after seeing Saptabhaṅgī.
Because at first glance, it appears to be containing a
lot of contradictory statements. The ones who do not
have a conceptual understanding or thought of the
science of things often are confused and in a dilemma.
To properly grasp this, Jain philosophers have said
something that is of extreme importance. If we keep
this as our guiding principle, we won't face any
difficulties in understanding the deeper meaning that
Saptabhaṅgī provides.
Everything is multi-dimensional. This, multidimensional defines itself as each and everything has
not one but multiple different sides to it. In this again,
there is something of much more importance that
must be considered when it comes to multiple
dimensions, they each have contradictory properties.
We will get a further understanding of this matter
later. For now, let us go into a little more depth about
Saptabhaṅgī:
i. Asti means it is.
ii. Nāsti means it isn't.
iii. Astināsti means it is and isn't.
iv. Avaktavyaṁ means that which cannot be
described in words.
v. Asti avaktavyaṁ means it is but indescribable.
36

Jainism: A First Look

vi. Nāsti avaktavyaṁ means it is not and cannot be
described.
vii. Asti nāsti avaktavyaṁ means it is there and isn't
there but at the same time is indescribable.
In the seven terms that have been mentioned above,
in each of them, Syāt (with respect to a specific angle)
and Eva (certainly) added over there. A special
mention will be given to them in the following
chapters at the right time. Here, there must be no
confusion or ambiguity. They all certainly have a clear
and definite meaning.
In the eyes of Vedanta and other philosophies, the
worldview is divided into 3 independent sections
namely, Utpatti (Origin), Sthiti (State), and Laya
(Destruction). Instead of this, Jain philosophers have
talked about 3 interconnected combinations namely,
Utpāda (Creation), Vyaya (Dissolution), and Dhrauvya
(Stationariness). We'll get to an understanding of that
later.
Jīva or Ātmā has been given an independent
personality in the eyes of Jainism. Each soul is eternal
and has a unique existence unlike any other. But only
when the soul becomes free from all karma does it
attain Mokṣa. Hence, it has been believed that even in
Mokṣa, its independent existence will continue to
remain. This existence is pure, unadulterated,
enlightened, and free from reincarnation.
Among the legislative powers of the world, Jain
philosophy has considered Karma to be the highest
power. Though there is a greater spiritual power
against it, it is still believed to be tainted and impure
because of the karma they are bound to. This conflict
37

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

between Ātmaśakti and Karmaśakti has been going on
since time immemorial, and in this battle, Ātmaśakti
fights to break the dominance of Karmaśakti by
destroying all the karma attached to it and emerge in
its true form that is pure and original.
What has been stated above implies that Jain
philosophy is on its path to achieving that goal and
that is conveyed in the phrase called
'Jñānakriyābhyāṁ Mokṣah.' This sentence means Achieving Mokṣa through the wise rituals with
knowledge. This is an incomparable and unique
phrase. There are two types of rituals mentioned. One
is known as 'Dravyakriyā' and the other 'Bhāvakriyā.'
These rituals are not independent of each other but
rather complement each other.
The foundation of this ritual is structured and based
on 'non-violence' which has vast meanings and holds
special importance. Thus, it defines a very significant
part of the religion which says that non-violence is the
ultimate religion.
In the cycle of birth and death, the Jain philosophers
insist that the goal of this application of knowledge by
the soul is to progress toward the path of salvation.
By misbehaving and going in the wrong direction
while following our conduct, we lead ourselves
towards fateful consequences that affect us in having
a rather negative next life. But, if we rightfully follow
our conduct towards our religion, we allow ourselves
to have a positive and rather successful next life and
this fact is known by the entire universe. For the sake
of upliftment of the soul and attainment of salvation,
the good conducts that have been shown for a being
to follow are divided into two sections:
38

Jainism: A First Look

i. Sarvaviratī sādhudharma (Acceptance of
Monkhood)
ii. Deśaviratī gṛhasthadharma (Acceptance of partial
monkhood)
Both these paths of conduct have been minutely and
deeply explained by the Jain Philosophers. For a
householder, they have used the word 'Śrāvaka' and
both these conducts have a special position and
importance of their own.
The entire Jain community worships a maṅtra which
is known as the 'Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra' with
utmost fervour. It is considered to be the essence of
all the scriptures and it also holds the power to
manifest unimaginable results when it comes to
devotion, meditation, and development of one's soul.
A small index has been given above which is about the
special and important aspects of Jain philosophy and
religion. Having got such a brief introduction now, it
will be much easier to understand what we want and
think in the future.
In all aspects of life, every big and small question, the
manner, and method in which we get the right
understanding is often known by the term 'Syādvāda
method'. Before going deeper into it, we'll discuss
Religion and Philosophy. This will help gain a better
understanding.

CHAPTER 5

Religion and Philosophy
What is religion? What is philosophy? Is there a
difference between the two, and if so, what is it?
Where did they originate, and how long have they
existed? How have they impacted humanity, and to
what extent? What kind of relationship do they share?
Are they mutually supportive, or do they exist
independently of each other?
If we sit down and contemplate religion and
philosophy, the questions which have been
mentioned above are bound to arise in our minds.
To start with, let us first get a general definition for
this.
"Religion shows conduct. And philosophy ties a
relation with thoughts. Yet, both of them are
interrelated. Both of them hold the same and equal
importance."
After having covered a concise definition of religion
and philosophy, let us move further into the chapter.
One thing is for sure; good conduct doesn't happen
without having good thoughts. Likewise, we won't
have good thoughts if we don't have good conduct.
Do not assume that thoughts are more important than
conduct. Just as good thoughts can improve one's
behaviour, good behaviour can elevate one's thoughts
to the highest level. Similarly, bad thoughts can spoil
40

Religion and Philosophy

behaviour, and bad behaviour can degrade one's
thoughts to the lowest level. Therefore, the
interrelationship between thoughts and behaviour is
equally important, as both influence each other.
Now, think of a man who is thinking of pleasing a
deity. His purpose is to attain worldly material
happiness. And, someone has said that "Some deities
are pleased with the sacrifices of humans and give
them what they desire." A man, blinded by his selfish
motives, follows these thoughts and pushes away any
good thought that interrupts him. The fact that this
happens even in today's so-called alert, awakened,
and cultured age is not completely ruled out but
rather likely it could happen. We still read about it in
newspapers and hear about it from courtrooms that
parents desiring to have a child, trouble and bully
other children or even kill them while practicing
certain cult activities.
Now, let's consider another type of man. He fully
believes in non-violence, but due to a lack of moral
strength, he struggles to uphold this principle.
However, with the support of his good thoughts, he
remains constantly aware of his shortcomings. Even
if he commits acts of violence, he will feel regret and
sorrow for his actions.
Now, another point to consider: When we talk about
good thoughts and good behaviour, what does 'good'
really mean? Is it simply what we like or what feels
good? No. We often feel good about things that satisfy
our instincts and please our senses. But this is
something we must understand that if we start

41

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

following only what we like and what feels good, we
risk falling into a dangerous trap.
Here, we arrive at the conclusion that 'good' means
what is 'true.' But this raises another question: what
is 'true'? If we attempt to define truth solely in our
minds, we risk falling into another trap. Our
selfishness, incomplete understanding, and limited
intelligence will inevitably become obstacles in
discerning the true nature of things.
It is thus that the need for philosophy presents itself.
The need and utility of resorting to one definite
philosophy is something that will not be left not
understood by us.
Then, the question arises as to which philosophy to
resort to.
All the philosophers of the world provide valuable
insights and ideas that promote good behaviour. This
naturally raises the question: which philosophy
should we adopt in a given situation?
We go to the market to buy a shoe, a saree, or a dhoti.
We enter a shop that is bright and lit up with tube
lights. The shopkeeper shows us shoes and a saree.
Everything the merchant shows us in the glittering
bright lights looks good. Is all of that really good? Do
we get startled and buy the items? Not at all. We will
check the quality of the fabric, we will check if the
colour of the cloth is dull or bright, we will look at the
details clearly, the neatness of it, we will check where
the cloth came from, we will contemplate the value of
the clothing and then, in the end, we'll decide whether
the seller is commercially trustworthy. It is only after
42

Religion and Philosophy

going through all 7 of these questions that we finally
conclude whether or not to buy the goods we wish to.
At the same time, if our mind, while reflecting on
these seven concepts, understands a philosophy that
includes the seven nayas and the seven categories
(Saptabhaṅgī), how much joy would it bring to grasp
these seven different ways of thinking?
Jain philosophers have given a gift to the world in the
form of such a great philosophy. It is known by the
name 'Anekāntavāda.'
All that glitters is not gold. All of us are aware that
something that looks shiny and good from afar isn't
necessarily something that is a good product.
Isn't it necessary for us to think similarly about
philosophy? Why should we be oblivious to the choice
of that philosophy that upholds our life, the
development of life, the basis of happiness, and
contentment in life?
We are all aware of the current state of religion and
philosophy in the Western and Eastern nations
surrounding India. The Western nations are largely
materialistic and focused on the pursuit of material
happiness and development. Their religion is shaped
by their prophets, and they have also developed their
own philosophies. However, these are primarily
centered around materialistic and worldly pleasures.
A synchronization between humans and harmony,
between good will and good behaviour, has been
considered necessary even by people of the West. But
the purpose has only been for worldly pleasures. No
one in the West has noticed or considered it
43

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

necessary for self-development. Thus, they have
remained away from the shadow of the complete
development of philosophy.
In contrast, in India, philosophy is viewed as a tool for
self-development and has evolved with that
perspective. Philosophy and self-growth have
become complementary to one another. This
interconnectedness is symbolic, as the origins of
nearly all major religions trace back to Asia.
Hinduism, Jainism, Buddhism, Christianity, and Islam
are the five major religions of the world today. Of
these, Jainism, Buddhism, and Hinduism developed in
India, while Islam and Christianity also originated in
Asia. Islam, Christianity, and Buddhism have all
emerged within the last two thousand years, and the
chronological order of their origins is welldocumented, leaving no room for doubt or
controversy about their beginnings.
Hinduism and Jainism are two religions whose
followers claim that their religion has existed since
time immemorial. However, a religion cannot be
considered superior merely because of its age. Upon
investigation, it has been found that Hindu scriptures,
considered ancient and authoritative, such as the
Upanishads and the Bhagavad Gita, reference the first
Tīrthaṅkara, Ṡri Ṛ ṡ abhadeva. This suggests that
Jainism predates these texts, making it older and
more ancient than Hinduism. Although some dismiss
this fact due to bias or claim that Jainism originated
after Vedic traditions, historical evidence shows that
Jainism is one of the oldest religions in the world.
Accredited scholars, both from India and the West,
agree on its ancient origins.
44

Religion and Philosophy

As far as philosophy is concerned, there are a lot of
divisions in Hinduism. It is a well-known fact that
there is a difference of opinion among Hindu
philosophers who are on a higher level. The various
branches of Hindu Philosophy include Vedānta,
Naiyāyika, Vaiśeṣika, Sāṅkhya, Yōga, Mīmānsaka,
Vaiyākaraṇa, and Cārvāka, etc. (Though there is a
controversy over whether Cārvāka Philosophy is a
branch of Hinduism or not).
Against all this, Jain philosophy is the only philosophy
where branches or divisions don't exist. When it
comes to the practice of religion, there are
Śvetāmbara, Digambar, Sthānakavāsī, and Terāpanthi
sect. But when it comes to philosophy, all four of these
branches are the same and one. There can be small
and minor differences here and there but, there are
no major or significant differences when it comes to
the basic foundation of this philosophy.
This is the only thing that assures the solidity of Jain
Philosophy and proves its foundational strength. The
undivided principles that have been shown to the
world by the Jain Tīrthaṅkara have not lost even a
brick from its fort and nobody has been able to
disrupt it either.
If this is the case, and there is no doubt about it, then
there must be a significant reason for it. The reason is
the uniqueness of Jain philosophy.
From an antiquity perspective, Jain philosophers
assert that Jainism is eternal, immortal, and
unchanging. However, its significance doesn't rest
solely on its ancient origins. What truly makes Jainism
45

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

remarkable and special is the uniqueness and
excellence of its philosophy.
It has a unique and special place among all the
philosophies of the world and a victorious philosophy
like this is known by the Anekāntavāda which has
been mentioned before. Particular importance is
given to logic in this special philosophy. The logic is
so perfect that it cannot lead to a finger being pointed
from any angle, and once scholars from all over the
world are introduced to it, they are amazed by it. Even
Western scholars like Mr. Hermann Jacoby, Dr.
Stinkono, Dr. Parold, and Bernard Shaw have been
fascinated by this elemental knowledge.
At first glance, Anekāntavāda may seem complex and
confusing, but it is deeply rooted in the everyday
thought processes of ordinary people. For instance,
when we buy clothes, the decision-making process
involves considering various perspectives and
preferences, reflecting the essence of Anekāntavāda.
If this isn't an example of Anekāntavāda in action,
then what is?
If we split the word Anekāntavāda, we see a
combination of two words: 'Aneka' and 'Anta.' In
simple words, it means the one who has multiple
endings. There is no statement to be made that says
that Jain Philosophy has many edges. The precise
meaning is that many edges of a subject have been
examined and checked from all different angles and
perspectives and the solid truth of it is what
philosophy shows us today.
Jain philosophers have not restricted their
philosophical verification to Anekāntavāda alone.
46

Religion and Philosophy

They have studied the prominent philosophies of the
world using their unique method and concluded that
these philosophies are often one-sided. According to
Jain philosophers, most other philosophies consider
only a single perspective, rather than examining an
issue from multiple points of view as Anekāntavāda
does.
In Jain Philosophy, according to the Āgama, it has
been believed that for a part of it, the 7 Nayas are
something that must be known and understood. To
understand these 7 Nayas, we can also recognize
them as 7 ends. From that point of view, Jain
philosophers have proven that Jainism is the only one
that is based on the intersection of all these 7 ends. All
the other philosophies are only based on a single
point of view or a single end.
According to the proven opinion of Jain philosophers,
they have given different situations on which the
