various philosophical viewpoints are based:
Advaita Vedānta and Sāṅkhya, are formed based on
Saṅgraha Naya.
Naiyā yika and Vaiś eṣ ika philosophies are formed in
the opinion of Naigama Naya.
Cārvāka's opinion depends only on Vyavahāra Naya.
The Buddhist view follows the Ṛjusūtra Naya.
Mímāṅasaka view is based on Ṡabda Naya.
Vaiyākaraṇa philosophy is based on Samabhirūdha
Naya.
Apart from this, all the various Extremist
philosophies are based on the Evamabhūta Naya.
47

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Whereas, Jain Philosophy, along with the group of all
7 of these Nayas represents a majestic ocean. An
understanding of each of these will come in the next
chapter. No special justification is needed for the
statements made above. Elaborating on them would
require filling thousands of pages and creating a
remarkably comprehensive scripture. Therefore, we
simply encourage anyone who is curious and
captivated by the ideas presented to explore further.
There is much more to learn, understand, and
discover.
If anyone has a suspicion about all that is mentioned
above, or if they feel that whatever has been
mentioned is false and untrue, or to prove that their
thinking is true, if they dig deep and find out about it,
they will reach the conclusion that will make them
believe for themselves that all of this philosophy
(Anekāntavāda) is true. Once all the conclusions have
been mentioned, there can be no doubt about the
veracity of the decisions made.
So, let's dive a little deeper and understand
Anekāntavāda a little more.

48

CHAPTER 6

Anekāntavāda
We have received a general understanding of
Anekāntavāda from the previous pages. We have
understood that it is a combination of two words i.e.
Anēka+Anta. Here, it is also a combination of 3 words
rather than just 2: An+Eka+Anta which means the one
whose end is not one, therefore, has multiple ends is
known as Anekānta. That's what makes this a
beautiful philosophy.
To make any decision, we must consider its various
aspects from multiple perspectives. By now, we have
clearly understood this approach.
Let us take the example of a tree and a mountain.
The trunk of the tree is round. Half of it is visible from
the front. The other half is visible when we go around
and behind it. But, apart from the two sides of the
circle, there's a lot more about the tree. The cavity of
the trunk, the roots of the tree, the land and soil on
which the tree grows, the soil that is hidden inside,
the water that nourishes the roots that are inside the
soil, the head of the tree, its branches, its leaves, its
fruit, and the taste of the fruit and so much more, are
all associated with the word 'tree'?
Similarly, the word 'mountain' is only made of 8
letters. Yet, if we explore it then we will find out about
a lot more than three thousand things. In addition to
the many variations, there are also contradictions
49

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

that we will find out. By stimulating our thinking and
by giving it a chance to develop, we will be
experiencing a kind of supreme joy like no other.
Now, let us say we want to buy a table, and it is right
in front of us. We must consider its cost. Will we
simply pay the price the seller asks for? No, we will
think about various factors: Is the table made of iron
or wood? If wood, what kind? Is it new or old? If it is
old, how long has it been used, and was it wellmaintained or used roughly? We will also consider its
height, length, width, overall appearance, the
craftsman, or factory where it was made, and even the
merchant selling it. Won't we think about all these
aspects before deciding?
Let us consider the four dimensions Jain philosophy
teaches: matter, space, time, and characteristic. The
matter of the table refers to the type of wood. Space
refers to whether the wood is domestic or imported.
Time indicates when the table was made - is it new or
old? Characteristics refers to the condition of the
table when we decide to buy it, including its
appearance and strength. By examining these four
aspects, we form a complete picture of the table. This
thorough understanding makes it easier to determine
its price, considering our needs, the urgency of the
purchase, and other relevant factors.
This is the matter of the inanimate object, now let us
consider an example of a single human being having
a consciousness. How much can we know about a
person based on their face?
We will first decide if they are male, female, or
transgender. Now from this, let us talk about males.
50

Anekāntavāda

Who is he? Where is he from? Which and what kind of
community was he born in? Is he rich? Is he a
gentleman? What is his color, body structure, what
condition is his body in, culture, his education, his
understanding, reputation, and so many more
countless things that won't come to our mind? In
addition to its external features, will we also get to
know about what kind of a person he is from the
inside?
Everything is contradictory, infinite, multi-faceted,
and full of variations. An example of this is when we
try to understand any one person completely. After
considering any one, two, or four things about this
man, will we be able to give a clear and definite
opinion on this man? No, obviously not.
From this, it is clear that we cannot pass an opinion of
anything by just looking at it from one end.
Anekāntavāda teaches us an invaluable lesson:
'Before concluding any matter, consider all
perspectives from every angle.'
Isn't this such nice advice? Now, let us talk a little bit
about the higher role here.
From Anekāntavāda's point of view, Jain philosophers
say that, "The object which is in essence, is also
not, that which is true is also false, that which is
one is also numerous, that which is eternal is also
impermanent; like this, everything is filled with
contradictory properties."
Had this been explained at the very beginning, we
might have dismissed it, perhaps even stopped
reading. However, with the prior explanations
51

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

already given, we now understand the concept more
deeply. As a result, we are less likely to disregard this
topic altogether.
Yet, a lot of questions would still arise naturally. 'If
something is considered true, how can it be
considered false?' This seems like such a
contradictory subject. It is such a unique and
intriguing subject that even a lazy person would rise
with full interest.
Let us set aside discussions of common sense, which
most people grasp easily. Even those who are
extremely intelligent and knowledgeable often
dismiss certain extraordinary concepts as impossible.
However, this is not the case here.
If the decision is to be made only from one end, then
this matter seems like one to be thrown away. But
Jain philosophers have taken the perspective of
Anekāntavāda after considering multiple viewpoints
and thoughts, and only after taking all of it together,
have they come to this decision and that shouldn't be
forgotten. Only after taking along with the four
requirements of matter, space, time and
characteristic, comparing the 7 Nayas, and, after
unlocking the box of Saptabhaṅgī, did Jain Scientists
call this strange-looking concept an 'absolute truth.'
Let us take an example of a small transaction. It is an
accepted and known fact that a medicine that is
prescribed for one sick person won't be helpful for a
person with a different sickness. Then, is it possible
to deny that this particular medicine is 'useful and
useless?'
52

Anekāntavāda

Jain philosophers face a major objection from those
who follow other philosophies, particularly regarding
Anekāntavāda. The question often raised is "How can
something be both true and false? How can something
be both temporary and permanent?" The idea that a
single entity can possess such contradictory
properties seems, to many, an out-of-the-world
concept.
The reason for this is that they have seen things only
from one side, in one pattern, and have not bothered
or tried to look at things from a different angle. How
can there be a lie in truth? This is a very
straightforward question. To find a simple answer to
this, we will discover a lot if we bring together a
memory box of our daily experiences. Here, an
important thing to remember is that Jain
philosophers have taken into view the entire form of
the thing and then talked about this, not just by
viewing one side of things.
We call one man 'white' but while talking about the
entire mankind, we recognize them with many colors
together.
A student named Param ranked first in his class.
Referring to him as the '#1 Ranker' is true from the
perspective of his class. However, when considering
all the other classes, calling him 'first' would be
untrue.
In the same way, the existence of truth depends on
the basis of untruth. Think about this carefully. If
there was no untruth, what would be the need for
truth? If there were no lies, would anyone even value
the truth? In this universe, truth exists because of lies;
53

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

there are lies because of the truth. If you take away
one, the other will disappear automatically. In the
absence of one, the other becomes futile. From this, it
is clear that both truth and lies live together, in one.
Taking Anekāntavāda as the base, if we go deeper and
search for more answers, it will be clear to us that
truth and lies are not two separate elements. The
existence of one is due to the existence of another. If
one is destroyed the other becomes useless; its
necessity, usefulness, or existence does not last.
With the utmost seriousness and depth of this talk, if
we think about this by taking Anekāntavāda as the
base, we will immediately understand that truth and
lies are not as different as they seem to be. There is no
possibility of the truth without a lie and lies cannot
exist without the truth. That is, these seemingly
contradictory elements are mutually supportive and
both are two different forms of the same element.
From the perspective of Anekānta, these two are
different and the same.
Similarly, 'Nitya-Anitya' (Permanent-Impermanent),
'Dharma-Adharma' (Merit-Demerit), 'Eka-Aneka'
(Single-Multiple), etc., even though they have
opposite properties, they are one and the same. From
these three pairs, if you remove one of the two pairs,
the other one will automatically disappear. By
accepting this exactly as it is, we will solve a lot of
confusion that comes our way.
Let us consider an example. There's a man whose
name we assume to be Mr. Jones. Now, let us examine
Mr. Jones.
54

Anekāntavāda

The man is one but there's both good and bad to him.
He is kind but brutal too. He is generous but also
stingy. He is forgiving and angry. He is both violent
and non-violent. He is an honest man but also a liar.
He is both tall as well as short. He is talkative but also
silent. He is wise as well as ignorant.
If someone says such contradictory things about a
single man, then what among them is true? But, Mr.
Jones possesses all of these contradictory features.
There will be no difficulty in proving this.
In various situations and contexts, Mr. Jones displays
different behaviors with different people. For
example, he may show love towards his wife while
being cruel to his servant. We can find many such
instances of Mr. Jones exhibiting these contradictory
qualities. Likewise, the elements of matter, space,
time, and characteristic can lead to similar
contradictory behaviors in many individuals.
In the same way, when it comes to talking about each
of the qualities differently, to describe all the different
behaviors of Mr. Jones while defining, "Mr. Jones is a
good man," "Mr. Jones is a bad man," we will talk
about all these different things.
When Syādvāda and Saptabhaṅgī will be mentioned
hereafter, the illustration of Mr. Jones will be very
useful in understanding these subjects.
Jain philosophers highlight four dimensions: Matter,
Space, Time, and Characteristic. By considering these
dimensions, we can gain clarity on complex concepts.
In the upcoming chapters, we will explore each of

55

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

these dimensions independently, which will enhance
our perspective and strengthen our thinking.
Marking the line from the perspective of
Anekāntavāda, there are a lot of such things that we
will understand easily. How it is wrong to take up one
thing and only think about it like in the previously
given example of Mr. Jones. Similarly, in philosophical
thinking, it is wrong to take into consideration one
single thing or form and think about it.
Let us think a little more.
The existence of those we are thinking about, won't
always remain absolute or forever in the same state.
If we accept this, then with changing the state, the
same thing as it appears today, the form does not
appear after that, and nor does it remain in the same
form afterward. We also have to admit this. Thus,
when and in which state we are accepting the
existence of the thing, that acceptance becomes
conditional. That acceptance does not stay
unconditional ever.
For example, after chopping a gourd and making it
into a vegetable, while eating, we do not say "pass the
gourd," we say, "Give me the vegetable."
After making the trousers from the cloth, when we
have taken them to the laundry worker to wash them,
we don't tell them, "The clothes have to be washed,"
we tell them, "My pants have to be washed." Many
such illustrations can be given. After the son is named
Praveen, the father doesn't say "Where did my son
go?" rather, he says, "Where did Praveen go?" When
shoes are made of leather, the leather does not fade;
56

Anekāntavāda

Instead of saying "Where is the leather?" we ask,
"Where are the shoes?"
Now it is easily understood how the situation flips
once, the condition and nature of things change. In the
same way, when a form does not exist, even its nonexistence does not remain unaffected. It becomes
conditional, and no one can say that there's no gourd
in the vegetable, no cloth in the coat or trouser, no son
in Praveen, and no leather in the shoes. When the
situation or nature changes, when primarily one form
comes into our focus, the former form, which is
secondary to it, does remain over there.
As the state changes and time passes by, let us see the
changes that happen in things. In other philosophies,
three states namely, 'Origin, State and Destruction'
have been said. Contrary to that, Jain philosophers
have shown these three states as 'Creation,
Dissolution and Stationariness.'
The original matter is eternal, so the question of its
origin does not remain, but when a new state has
been revealed from another state, instead of 'origin'
calling it 'Utpāda' (creation) would be a cautious and
rational choice. There are constant changes in the
state of a matter that appears. Hence, it is called
'Vyaya' (Dissolution). If we examine our life, despite
its condition, at every moment, it keeps getting used
and spent. And hence, it would be more appropriate
to call it 'Vyaya' (Dissolution) instead of 'Laya'
(Destruction). If everything's the way it is forever,
how does it flow? The flow that appears is just a form
of a state of existence. In fact, it disappears just to take
another form. Instead of 'Sthiti' (Remain), Jain
57

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

philosophers
use
the
word
'Dhrauvya'
(Stationariness). This is also something that can be
naturally understood. When the state of an object is
changed, its original form stays the way it is. The
smoke that erupts from the fore can be seen
disappearing in the sky, but it is not destroyed. It goes
into the sky, separates, and generates a different form
as a cloud.
We recognize the original matter as 'Dhrauvya,' which
appears consistent with reasoning.
If we observe, when the original object is one, its
various forms are identified by different names.
These
different
forms
inherently
possess
contradictory qualities. Iron is one object. When it is
shaped into a shield, sword, knife, spear, or stick, all
are identified by different names, yet they perform
contradictory functions. A sword cuts while a shield
does not allow cutting. A knife can pierce, but a needle
sews.
Poison is one, yet it acts differently in quantity
and state. It is called poison when killing a man
and medicine when giving life. This is the
inherent contradictory nature of a single thing.
When a gun is in our hands, we believe it to protect
us. When it goes into the enemy's hands, the same gun
threatens our lives. Here, there is a difference in the
space of the pistol. In the case of poison, there was a
difference in characteristics.
Humans also have various stages of existence, such as
childhood, adolescence, youth, middle age, old age,
and the final stage. When the body and name are the
58

Anekāntavāda

same, how many forms are there due to the
dimension of time? Behind them, there are
contradictory qualities, too. A person's nature also
shows contradictory qualities.
From the perspective of matter, it is one body, but it
appears with softness, strength, and weakness,
without beard and mustache, with beard and
mustache, bald, curly hair, rough, soft like cotton, and
rough like a thorn, etc., which are contradictory
qualities.
In terms of space, it is recognized by different names
like English, American, European, African, Bengali,
Gujarati, etc.
In terms of characteristics, the same person is seen as
gentle, fierce, calm, restless, stable, panicked, restless,
foolish, serious, beautiful, and ugly. Even though the
human body is one, when it is in different forms due
to
the
dimensions
of
matter-space-timecharacteristics, it appears different due to different
dimensions.
All this proves that, without a doubt, contrasting
properties in the same object do exist. There will be
no ambiguity in accepting this now, no difficulty in it.
By resorting to Anekāntavāda, Jain philosophers have
explained many such things very clearly.
Modern psychologists say that in every human being
opposing characters like Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde, and
instincts that are as distant as the North Pole and the
South Pole exist. Hence, no worldly man can be
known as completely good or as absolutely bad.

59

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

A gentleman donated Rs. 5 lacs to build a public clinic
in his own name but refused to give Rs. 1 lac to one of
his clerks who urgently needed the money for a lifesaving operation. Unfortunately, the clerk could not
receive the necessary treatment in time and passed
away. This is based on a true incident.
What would we say about this gentleman? Kind?
Generous? Cruel? Vile? There is no need to answer.
This is an easily understandable concept.
Many such scenarios can be found in different fields.
All of this will easily make us understand that 'There
is only one thing and there is no such thing,' when Jain
philosophers say this, they say it from the point of
Anekāntavāda, and when they say that, they are
completely right.
Such concepts of Anekāntavāda are something that all
of us must understand. If we do understand it
properly, the solutions to all our problems in life will
arrive to us easily.
If we think more about it while keeping Anekāntavāda
in our mind, then, it won't be difficult to understand
that existence and non-existence, permanence and
impermanence, and similarly, individuality and
plurality, etc, all exist at the same time, and to see and
understand all of it, we will have to resort to
Anekāntavāda.
In today's rapidly advancing field of scientific and
atomic research, it is easy to understand that a
singular entity and multiple entities can coexist
simultaneously.

60

Anekāntavāda

It is also easy to understand the permanence and
impermanence of a thing. Everything carries in itself
the nature to change, and that fact is accepted by all.
In the dimensions of matter, space, time, and
characteristics, one single thing undergoes many
changes. Since it is changeable, it is temporary.
Yet, its original matter, even in different forms,
remains forever. Hence, it is also permanent. Like it is
false to say that it is only temporary, it is also wrong
to say that it is only permanent.
Even this change does not happen suddenly. It takes
its time. Cloth doesn't get dirty automatically, raw
rice doesn't suddenly become edible rice, wheat
doesn't suddenly turn into bread and a child does not
grow very old in a quick second. There is a chronology
that all of these things follow. Despite all these
changes, the original matter doesn't get completely
destroyed.
Once a particular form of an object is vanished,
another form of it makes an appearance. The original
matter of it is not completely destroyed. When water
comes in contact with fire, it burns and then
evaporates. Yet, when the steam is transferred into a
particular machine, it can be brought back to its liquid
form i.e., water, even if it is later identified as 'distilled
water.' Even the steam contained its original form of
it, which was water. The two types of elements that
are in the water, H2O, still remain in one or another of
their forms while going through changes.
When a pot is made from clay, the pot still has its
original matter which is clay. When the pot is broken,
61

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

another form of it still contains the original matter
which is clay.
It is utterly untrue to call anything absolutely true or
untrue or to believe it utterly eternal or utterly
impermanent. If the same elements are to remain the
same, and if they do not have opposite properties, it
is not in their nature to change, then their existence
just becomes useless.
If a stone remains unchanged over time, both in the
present and the future, it indicates that it cannot
function or act. Therefore, why should we expect it to
do anything?
If Brahma is believed to be a singular entity with a
stable and unchanging existence, then what is its
significance due to lack of function?
If the world is viewed as entirely an illusion, how can
it genuinely be related to Brahma, the only reality?
Similarly, since non-living and living are considered
fundamentally different from each other, how can one
expect that one affects the other?
If the world is changeable, then what Vedic
philosophers believe and show that even Brahma
should be changeable, and if that isn't the case then,
from an eternal and unchanging Brahma, how does a
changing world arise?
The independent origin of the everlasting eternity
from the impermanence or of the only impermanence
from the everlasting eternity cannot be possible. Jain
philosophers have said this with a strong emphasis
and unequivocally. This is something that holds a lot
of importance and should be understood. From
62

Anekāntavāda

Dvaita (Duality), Advaita (Non-duality), and all of its
sections as well, and from the philosophies which are
stuck only to one end, we do not receive such an
understanding. Because all that has been depicted
there is from a single perspective and has been
formed by the solitary (Ekāntika) decisions. In front
of all of them, stands the Jain Philosophy's concept of
Anekāntavāda like a mighty ocean roaring its
knowledge. The understanding of Jain Philosophy is
the only right and true understanding.
Let us clarify this a little more. Instead of using truth
and false, let us use the words Satva (existence) and
Asatva (non-existence). These both have opposing
properties. But here, if we bring in the four
dimensions as mentioned earlier, we will learn that
there is the existence of a thing with respect to one's
own matter, space, time and characteristic, at the
same time, it is non-existent with respect to some
other thing's matter, space, time and characteristic.
In these dimensions of matter, space, time, and
characteristic, what is Sva (self) and what is Para
(non-self)? We will be going in-depth on this over the
chapters to come. Since we cannot be in confusion
regarding this, let us go over it in brief. Where it is
about our own self, it is ours and where it is not about
our own, it is non-self. Before reaching the in-depth
section of this topic, if we give it a little thought
beforehand, it'll be easier for us to understand later
on.
In this way, non-existence and existence,
impermanence and permanence, plurality, and
individuality, etc., if we look at all these things with
63

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

mutually exclusive properties from different sides
then, we will see that all of it is not solitary
(Ekāntātmaka)
but
multidimensional
(Anekāntātmaka).
Before concluding this chapter, one very important
point must be made. Non-Jain philosophers have
accepted that a single thing can have multiple
properties. The ones who have not understood
Anekāntavāda raise questions like, "This is in us too.
What new did the Jain philosophers have to say?"
Here, the uniqueness of Jainism is seen. Jain
philosophers have not talked about Anekāntavāda by
just showing that each thing has multiple properties.
They have proven that topic, that very concept.
Moreover, Jain philosophy says that in each thing, the
mutually opposite properties co-exist and things are
not just multidimensional but mutually contradicting
and multi-dimensional. These opposite properties
are not visible from a single perspective. It can only
be understood when seen from multiple perspectives.
This is the peculiarity of Jain philosophy, of
Anekāntavāda. This isn't a small or insignificant thing.
It is a great achievement in the subject of philosophy.
It is for this that Anekāntavāda believed to be a gem
among philosophies.
Something can be true, eternal, and one while also
possessing multiple features. However, being multifeatured does not necessarily mean it is multidimensional. It can be true and false, temporary, and
eternal, one and multiple, all at once. These mutually
contradictory properties exist simultaneously within
itself, which is why it is considered multi64

Anekāntavāda

dimensional. This point requires careful and
thorough understanding.
From this, it will be clear that mutually opposite
properties are there in the same thing, and when this
has to be proved, when it has to be discovered, when
it has to be understood, only at that time, will the need
for Anekāntavāda arise. A clearer thing to say would
be that, if we do not resort to Anekāntavāda then, we
will never be able to reach the right decisions.
After giving such a detailed explanation, if we had to
describe Anekāntavāda in brief, then it would be
stated as:
"The one who can expose the mutually opposed
properties and elements contained in the same
thing is known as Anekāntavāda. On the contrary,
if we do not base any of our decisions on
Anekāntavāda then, that part of the decision just
cannot be right."
After going through such a clear understanding of
Anekāntavāda, and before arriving on the topic of
Syādvāda, one thing has to be revealed, in this
chapter, certain things have been said more than
once, in a repetition; but it has been done deliberately
to deliver a more clear and correct understanding.
Now, let us move on.

65

CHAPTER 7

Syādvāda
After reading and thinking about what was written
regarding Anekāntavāda in the previous pages, there
would be no problem in believing that we now have a
basic understanding of this unique philosophy.
We now clearly understand that everything has
infinite, mutually opposing properties. We also have
to look at things from multiple perspectives to
understand elements with mutually opposing
properties.
If there were any mathematical calculation or method
to help us clearly understand this phenomenon, it
would be very instrumental. To approach this
complexity, Jain philosophers have provided a
method known as Syādvāda.
Syādvāda is also known as Anekāntavāda as well as
Apēkṣāvāda. From a general point of view,
Anekāntavāda and Syādvāda look almost the same,
but if we understand both of them clearly, we will
know that to present the philosophy of
Anekāntavāda, Syādvāda is a method to do so.
The
relationship
between
Syādvāda
and
Anekāntavāda is like the 'speaker and a subject to be
spoken to' or like that of 'goal and the instrument to
achieve it.' Here, if we want to give an analogy then,
we can compare Anekāntavāda with gold and,
Syādvāda to a special stone that checks the purity of
66

Syādvāda

gold, or, Anekāntavāda to a fort and, Syādvāda to a
map showing the various routes leading to the fort.
The word 'Syādvāda' is a combination of two words:
'Syāt' and 'Vāda.' Let us properly understand the
meaning of the word 'Syāt'. Further, when the section
of Saptabhaṅgī is described, the word 'Syāt' plays a
very important role. Therefore, let us properly
understand the meaning of this word beforehand.
The abbreviated meaning of the word 'Syāt' is with
respect to. Here, when mentioned 'with some
respect,' it refers to a state, condition, position, or
situation.
Many people get confused while trying to understand
the meaning of the word 'Syāt'. Some assume its
meaning to be 'doubt' while some consider it to be
'possibility.' Some also call it a 'probability.'
Those who have begun to understand and grasp the
concept of Syādvāda are on the path to truly
comprehending it. When a lot of non-Jain scholars
have neutrally observed Jain philosophy, it is then
they have accepted its importance. The famous
professor of Gujarat, the Late Mr. Anandshankar
Dhruv, in one of his lectures, expressed his opinion on
Syādvāda. He said:
"Syādvāda presents before us the vision of
integration. The allegations that Shankaracharya has
made on Syādvāda do not relate to the original
context. It is for sure that anything cannot be
understood completely without observing it from
different points of view. For this, Syādvāda is useful
and valuable. The Syādvāda shown in Mahavir's
67

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

theory is considered skeptical by many people, but I
do not believe that. Syādvāda is not skeptical, rather,
it gives us a perspective, it teaches us how to observe
the world."
Many other scholars like the Late Professor Mr.
Anandshankar Dhruv, including the scholars of the
West, have expressed similar great opinions for
Syādvāda. The ones who have truly understood the
real context in which Jain philosophers have used the
word 'Syāt' have no feeling of confusion in their
minds. The meaning of that has been derived by
linking it with a definite state of matter, space, time,
and characteristic. Hence, the meaning of the word
'Syāt' is not 'doubt,' 'possibility,' or 'probability.' It is
'definitely.'
In Saptabhaṅgī, Eva (certainly) is used along with the
word 'Syāt' and is applied to indicate the certainty
that a statement possesses. From this, it is clear that
Syādvāda is not a probability or skepticism but rather,
certainty.
One would naturally question here that if something
is certain and that presents only one kind of a state,
then what is the point of adding the word 'Syāt'? Why
not call it as Niṡcitavāda (related to certainty) instead
of Syādvāda?
This question is not baseless. Why not use the word
'Niṡ cita' (certain) instead of 'Syāt'?
But the essence of this philosophy presented by the
Jain philosophers holds a specialty in the word 'Syāt'.
That word has been used deliberately, on purpose,
and with complete surety. The term has been used
68

Syādvāda

not for the sake of use, but because it is necessary. If
this word hadn't been used, the philosophy of
Anekāntavāda would have fallen like the collapsing of
a house of cards.
Let us begin by recalling a fact again. Everything is
endowed with multiple qualities that are mutually
opposite. If not, there would have been no need to use
the word 'Syāt'. But this is not the case. Hence, the use
of the word 'Syāt' is necessary and indispensable.
To understand this in detail, let us take one part of
Saptabhaṅgī.
स्यात् अस्स्त एव - Syāt + asti + eva = with some respect (it)
certainly exists. Here, let us add the word 'pencil' to
it. So now it means: with some respect, pencil
certainly exists.
Let us proceed with a clear understanding as we
explore this further. As I write, there is a table in the
corner of my room with a sheet of paper on it, and in
my hand, I hold a pencil. The pencil is made of goodquality wood, and at this moment, it is 3 o'clock in the
afternoon.
The wood of the pencil is the matter here, my fingers
are the space, 3 p.m. is the time, and good quality is
the characteristic, keep this in mind.
Now, if I simply say, "There is a pencil," my friend
Veenu, who is sitting beside me, might immediately
respond, "There is no pencil." When I glance at him,
he might add, "Even if you have a pencil in your hands,
I don't have one in mine." Can what he said be
considered wrong? No. Yet, at the same time, was my
statement wrong? No, that was true as well.
69

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

While talking about this pencil, there were
automatically two mutually contradictory statements
that came up:
There is a pencil.
There is no pencil.
In this situation, when I say, "There is a pencil," I am
correct; however, my friend Veenu is also right when
he claims, "There is no pencil." Both statements, while
seemingly contradictory, highlight the importance of
dimensions. From one angle, my assertion that there
is a pencil is a fact, yet Veenu's statement also holds
true from his viewpoint. This indicates that both the
answers could be wrong if we change the view-points.
My statement is valid from my perspective, while it is
false from his. This illustrates the need for the concept
of Syāt, which acknowledges that truth can vary
depending on individual perspectives.
Here, it will become a definite statement once the
word 'Syāt' is used. Then, there will be no room for
any disputes or objections to arise. By using that
word, there will be an indication of a specific viewpoint that there is a pencil with respect to my hand.
Even when my friend Veenu used the word 'Syāt' he
confirmed that there was no pencil with respect to his
hand. The conversation will be clearer after that.
Here, the use of the word 'Syāt' has ensured a definite
and assured state of circumstance.
What I have, others do not have, and what I do not
have, others have - to explain this clearly to myself,
my friends, and everybody reading this, I have used
the word 'Syāt' here. The special and specific meaning
70

Syādvāda

of it is that when we use the word 'Syāt' besides
saying that the pencil is there with me, and we also
give an indirectly clear suggestion that the pencil is
only in my hand and not in my friend's.
From this, it is clear that 'Syāt' is used to give clarity
on a particular thing's state and situation. By
referring to the thing's particular circumstance, it
gives a clear and definite meaning to it. But, when
considering and confirming the thing's definite
circumstance, it is also necessary to consider other
conditions, positions, and forms of it and it is then
that the word 'Syāt' is used.
If the word 'Syāt' was not used here then without the
word, the decision to be made would have been from
a single perspective instead of multiple ones. And
because of this, we may not bother to consider other
view-points. Consequently, our decision, on the one
hand, is solitary, and then it becomes false and on the
other hand, we are deprived of knowledge of other
states or forms of things.
From this, it will be clear that the word 'Syāt' is not
used in vain or as a doubtful one but rather, as a clear
and a solid one.
Before concluding anything, we have to consider the
four bases - matter, space, time, and characteristic. If
our thinking is not subject to these four dimensions
then, even our judgments and conclusions become
ridiculous.
Thus, we must recognize that the word 'Syāt', with its
various nuances, encapsulates the essence of
Anekānta and serves as the foundation of the entire
71

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

philosophy. Without this term, our thinking risks
becoming constrained to a singular perspective and a
fixed understanding of reality. The primary reason
Anekāntavāda is also known as Syādvāda is its
inherent emphasis on multiple viewpoints. If we
advance without embracing this concept, our journey
may resemble that of a blind traveler lost in a dark
wilderness, unable to find a way out.
Do you now realize the necessity of the word 'Syāt'?
Let us consider the diverse skin colors observed
among humans across various continents and
subcontinents. Indians typically have a wheat
(brown) skin tone, while Chinese individuals often
have a yellowish hue. Africans are commonly
associated with black skin, Europeans and Americans
generally have white skin, and Native Americans are
often characterized by a red skin tone. These five
major skin colors represent the rich diversity of
human beings worldwide.
If someone asks, "What is the skin color of humans?"
How should we respond? While there are five
primary skin colors—brown, yellow, black, white,
and red—these colors vary significantly by region.
For instance, describing Indians as having a brown
skin tone is accurate, but this characterization would
not apply to individuals from America. Thus, the
answer depends on the specific context and
population being discussed.
When we describe the skin color of Native Africans as
black, there is no doubt or hesitation. By combining
the term 'Syāt' with 'black,' we affirm that this
description is definitive. The term 'black' is certain,
72

Syādvāda

and when paired with 'Syāt,' it underscores that while
black is the skin color of people from one region,
individuals from other regions possess different skin
colors. This example illustrates that the use of 'Syāt'
does not introduce ambiguity or uncertainty; rather,
it emphasizes determinism and the specific context of
the statement.
Those who have not grasped Syādvāda properly often
mistake it for a mere combination or compromise.
This perspective is entirely misguided. First,
combination only occurs between entities with
similar properties; mutually opposing qualities
cannot be combined. Furthermore, there is no
compromise involved here. A compromise
necessitates a degree of letting go and making
adjustments, which can lead to outcomes that are not
entirely fair, reasonable, or rational. Syādvāda does
not simply compromise by overlooking flaws or
making concessions. Instead, it serves to unravel
complexities and clarify understanding, moving
forward with greater insight.
If you understand it clearly then, Syādvāda is a
platform of neutralism and one that practices the
principle of impartiality. It does not look equally at
both truth and false. It does not hold biases or love for
one side and hatred towards the other. It is only
biased towards absolute justice. It is an ardent
opponent of injustice or untruth.
From the eyes of Syādvāda, untruth is solitary (which
looks at only one side). It considers truth to be multidimensional.

73

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The 7 Nayas (relative knowledge) we are going to talk
about later, that 7 bits of knowledge are only a small
fraction of the great form of Syādvāda. Thus, Syādvāda
is known as an ocean and Naya is known as a stream.
"Why isn't it called a combination of multiple
streams?" This question was again asked. The usage
of the word 'combination' was briefly explained
above. When qualities like virtuousness, fortune,
generousness, forgiving, temperance, and similar
ones are combined, it is then that the word
'combination' is used. When negative characteristic
traits like that of a sinner, wicked, unfaithful,
drunkard, thief, wrathful, and similar ones come
together, the word 'combination' can be used even
then. Qualities or demerits of different emotions can
be combined separately. However, mutually opposite
qualities and demerits cannot be combined together.
A single thing is seen to have several properties which
are mutually opposite, and that is known as
Anekāntavāda, and, the ones that are not brought or
collected from outside in the future, but the ones that
are present in its original and natural state in the
present.
If this is remembered correctly, we will not fall into
the confusion of combination and reconciliation.
This yet again proves that Syādvāda, is not something
difficult, shady, or suspicious, but something that is
clear, complete, unequivocal, and definite philosophy.
The epitome of Anekāntavāda shows the
disentanglement of mutually opposite properties and
in that philosophy, Syādvāda is a special section. It is
not graspable by the means of external senses but by
the application of the mind. If this is understood
74

Syādvāda

clearly, we can immediately say that when in the
word 'Syāt', the words and sentences are pronounced
with the proper understanding, and the meaning
derived is the right knowledge, the true knowledge.
Any knowledge that is known apart from that is
recognized as Mithyātva (False knowledge).
We have now understood the meaning of Syāt. After
understanding its meaning, we should now have a
good idea of the word 'Syādvāda' as well. In the field
of religion too, Syādvāda holds a marvelous base. It
gives us this wonderful master key to removing all the
paradoxes of the world. It would be very interesting
and useful to know how Syādvāda helps us in our
lifestyle. It is certain that in our daily lives, Syādvāda
is extremely useful and instructive. That is, from the
physical value of life's usefulness, there is no doubt
that we all would love to think about it.
Before, only after understanding the four dimensions,
five reasons, seven Naya and Saptabhaṅgī, we can
make a decision in life, discuss and understand the
daily usability of Syādvāda more easily, flexibly,
clearly and more quickly.
Even in this chapter, anything that has been
repeatedly said was intended to get those things
rightfully understood in our minds. Now, let us try to
understand the four dimensions of matter, space,
time, and characteristic. For a simpler understanding,
instead of using the word 'dimensions,' we will use
the word 'base' from here onwards.

75

CHAPTER 8

Four Bases
Previously, we have talked repeatedly about the four
bases. Those four words namely, 'matter, space, time,
characteristic,' are no longer unknown to us. The
things we will now talk about will include the
mention of these words again and again. Let us now
properly understand why these words are so
essential and are considered the pillars when
deciding anything important and what is the
usefulness of these words.
The first base is Matter.
When we talk about matter, we generally refer to the
countless objects and things that we observe around
us. These external forms, though varied, share a
foundational material that remains constant, which is
what we mean by 'matter' here. It is the enduring
matter that underlies all these different forms and
states. Even as the forms change, the essential
material persists, eternal and unchanging. This is the
true essence of matter. For example, gold remains the
fundamental matter in various ornaments, wood in
furniture, and clay in pots.
Among the six substances mentioned in the previous
introduction chapter, time has not been included in
the category of matter, as it is a unique substance.
Time cannot be divided into parts. Day, night, hour,
minute, second, etc., are divisions that we have
76

Four Bases

created for convenience, but they are not actual
divisions of time itself. These names have been
assigned through human intelligence and
imagination to make daily activities more
manageable. Terms like morning and evening are not
truly time but rather the result of other factors, such
as light. Time, unlike other matters, is a regulatory
entity. Thus, when discussing material matters, time
will be excluded from this category. However, time
remains a crucial element and one of the six
substances, just not under the matter base here.
Let us take an example of a few things and
understand the matter it includes:
Thing

Matter

Basic Material of Sword

Iron

Basic Material of Table

Wood

Basic Material of Ring

Gold

Basic Material of bread Wheat
(roti)
Basic Material of Shoes

Leather

In this way, if the object is to be thought in
anticipation of matter, as stated above, we will surely
get a wise idea.
The second base is Space.

77

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The word 'kṣetra' means space where material
resides. There is a subtle difference between the
general understanding and the philosophical
interpretation of this term. In a simple sense, space
refers to the area where or on which an object rests.
However, to fully grasp this concept, we must
recognize that the foundation or basis of a thing
cannot simply be considered as space.
For example, consider a vessel filled with milk.
Typically, we would understand that the vessel is the
place where the milk resides. From a practical
standpoint, this assumption is acceptable. However, if
we aim to comprehend the concept of space as a
foundational element in its true sense, we would find
ourselves limited by this kind of simplistic belief.
The space in the vessel where the milk resides is
separate from the vessel. When we place the vessel on
the wooden plank, at that time, the space where the
vessel resides is not the wooden plank but only the
amount of space the vessel is covering on the wooden
plank.
Similarly, the space of the milk in the vessel is
separate from the space which the vessel resides in.
Generally, we say 'The milk is in the vessel.' But
actually, the milk is not in the vessel but only in the
space inside the vessel. So, when we use the word
'space,' it is to be understood that it is used without
the reference to the place. We must make judgments
using our discretion as necessary.
The vessel is made of stainless steel. When we fill the
milk in the vessel, the stainless steel remains where it
is and the milk stays where it belongs i.e., in the space
78

Four Bases

inside the vessel. So, the vessel may have supported
the milk to stay in one place, but both have different
spaces in their own field.
When we talk about the basis of space, it is important
to keep this in mind. For example, 'I am sitting on a
chair,' when someone says this, the area he is sitting
in and the area of the chair itself, the space of both is
different, not one.
Let's take some simple and straightforward examples
to understand this.
1. The space of the pencil in my hand while writing is
my hand (to the extent an area is occupied by it).
2. The space of the paper on which it is written is the
table (to the extent an area occupied by it).
3. The jurisdiction of the Prime Minister of India is the
country of India.
4. The field of the orator giving the speech is the
lecture hall.
5. The space of the airplanes is the sky (to the extent
an area occupied by it).
Thus, the space of a matter is the area it resides in.
The third base is Time.
Here, the meaning of time is the time of existence of a
particular form of a thing. The duration when each
form has gone through a change, is its own time.
This may seem a little confusing. But here when we
are talking about time base, we are not talking about
time itself. The time must be mentioned with respect

79

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

to that thing. We are talking about the duration of that
thing itself.
Let us clarify this further. Consider mangoes and
bananas, both of which are fruits. We place them in
two separate baskets, and on May 5th, 2017, at 7 a.m.,
when we open the baskets, we notice that both fruits
are ripe and ready to eat. The time at which both
fruits become ripe and edible was precisely 7 a.m.
that day.
Now, these mango pods were brought and kept ten
days ago; while the bananas were brought the
previous evening and kept. Both were raw when they
were brought. Both fruits were ripe and ready to eat
in the morning on the above-mentioned day at seven
o'clock. The special thing to understand here is the
ripening time of both fruits. It is not seven o'clock in
the morning. It is a question of the ripeness of that
fruit. With proper understanding and calculation, we
will say that for mangoes - the (ripening) time is ten
days and the (ripening) time for bananas is twelve or
sixteen hours. From this point of view, both of them
are transformed from raw to ripe but their time is
different.
Let us take a few examples for simple understanding.
1. The period after the nirvāṇa (a state of liberation)
of Tīrthaṅkara Ṛṣabhadevā - countless of years.
2. The period of rainfall - Monsoon (Similar to this,
understand the period of all seasons).
3. Tīrthaṅkara Mahāvīra's life span - 72 years of that
time.

80

Four Bases

4. Time of nirvāṇa of Tīrthaṅkara Mahāvīra - the time
when he attained nirvāṇa.
5. Soaking time of clay – time taken to mix the two i.e.
water and sand.
Thus, when we talk about the basis of time for
anything, the origin of the thing, the duration during
which a change occurred, and the action time are to
be understood depending on the situation and the
circumstance.
The fourth base is Characteristic.
This base is related to characteristics of a thing. For
example, shape, form, taste, smell, touch, function,
etc. All of this comes under the base of characteristics.
In short, the values, power, and effect of a thing are
called characteristics. These properties, qualities,
types, caste, class, etc., keep changing from time to
time.
Everything has its own characteristic and nature and
they are ever-changing. When we have to make a
decision based on the characteristics, we have to
consider the properties of the things we are thinking
about.
Let us take some examples to understand this
properly.
1. Black or red colour, is the characteristic of the pot.
2. Sweetness is the characteristic of sugar.
3. Form or beauty is the external characteristic of a
human being.

81

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

4. Selfish or charitable, is the inner characteristic of
human beings.
5. Heat is that of fire, and coldness of water are their
characteristics.
Thus, the idea that each thing has its own properties
and nature, etc should be kept in mind while deciding
the basis of characteristics. In this too, careful use of
wisdom is essential.
Let us take a unified paradigm to understand all four
bases correctly. Let us see how a decision should be
made with different expectations regarding one
thing.
Consider an airplane.
Matter: Aluminum, iron, wood, etc. materials would
be used to make the airplane so let us consider them
as matter.
Space: It has two sectors namely production and
action. It was prepared in London, which means its
area of manufacture is London or the space where it
was made in London. The space where the factory is
located is its field. Where it lies in the field or when it
flies in and away from the field would be considered
as the space. In the former case, the airport becomes
the space. In the latter case, the sky becomes space.
Time: In this too there are two types of time production and action. The time of its making is the
time of its production and when it is ascending in the
sky, that is the time of action.
Characteristic: Its colour, appearance, figure, and
function are the characteristics of the aircraft. Its
82

Four Bases

function is usually to fly in the sky. This means, flying
is the main characteristic of the aircraft.
Jain philosophers have used pot as an example. It
includes soil as its matter, where it is made or placed
as its space, when it is made or placed as its time, and
if its colour is black or red as its characteristic.
Now, from all these four bases, we must also see the
important aspects of each of them. These bases are
divided into self and non-self. This self and non-self
are two different values. Let us understand the words.
In the first two clauses of Saptabhaṅgī 'is' and 'is not,'
two such statements are made. These statements are
made with respect to 'self' and 'non-self.'
'Self' means one's own and 'non-self' means 'that
which is not one's own.' So, when we talk about bases,
it happens in two ways.
One way is self-matter, self-time, self-space, and selfcharacteristic. And another way is non-self matter,
non-self time, non-self space and non-self
characteristic.
Thus, there are a total of 8 bases that have to be
considered when deciding about a thing.
When the word 'self' is added to the bases of matter,
space, time, and, characteristic of a thing, it means
that the thing is there. When the word 'non-self' is
added, it means it is not.
Hence, when these 4 bases come in the form of 'self',
we mean that it is there and when it comes with 'nonself', it means that it is not there.

83

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

For all these four bases the term 'Catuṣṭaya'
(quadrilateral) is used. This word is used when all
four bases are mentioned together at once.
The words 'self' and 'non-self' are added to the word
'Catuṣṭaya' (quadrilateral) and then called Svacatuṣṭaya (self-quadrilateral) and Para-catuṣṭaya
(non-self quadrilateral).
We will discuss more about its four self-forms and
four non-self forms in the chapter of Saptabhaṅgī.
After that, there is also a chapter based on the
comprehensive understanding of the phrase 'with
respect to'. Hence it is explained in brief here. If we
think and understand what has been written above,
we will find it very easy to understand the following
chapters.
Now let us understand a little about the five reasons.

84

CHAPTER 9

Five Reasons
Whatever happens in this world, there must be some
reason behind it. Some causes are visible, some are
invisible; some are known, some unknown.
The sun rises and sets. The night passes and the day
arrives. A man is born, then he dies. From happiness,
sorrow, poverty, wealth, health, illness, etc., to the
origin of atomic and hydrogen bombs to space flights,
from rockets to all events up to the orbits of launched
satellites around the Earth and the Moon, we see all
of it happening before our eyes.
Many such deeds have been taking place in this world
since time immemorial, and they are happening; they
will continue to happen forever. Some of these
reasons are understood by us, many reasons are not
understood by us. Due to the limitation of knowledge,
we do not understand many of the reasons and causes
of such events.
However, behind all these actions, there is always a
reason. Let us all agree on that.
Different theorists have provided various
explanations for this phenomenon. Jain philosophers,
however, have identified five specific causes behind
it. While other philosophies may accept only one or a
few of these causes, Jain philosophy asserts that
unless all five causes come together, an action cannot
occur; it simply does not take place.
85

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Let us then examine those five reasons. The five
reasons, in order, are as follows:
1. Time
2. Nature
3. Destiny
4. Karma
5. Puruṣ ārtha
Different opinions prevail in this matter. Some people
only consider time as the cause of action. People who
call themselves naturalists hold nature responsible
for the action. People who believe in predestination
refuse to accept anything other than destiny as the
cause of every action. The fourth view is of the people
who believe in fortune created by past karma
(actions). Those people do not accept anything other
than karma as the cause.
Whereas, individuals who believe in Puruṣārtha
(efforts) do not accept any reason other than the
pursuit of all the actions that take place in this world.
Jain philosophers say, "The five causes give impetus
behind every outcome, as the guiding force. Unless
those five factors come together, nothing usually gets
done". Let us listen to the mind of philosophers.
Time: Those who consider time as the sole cause
argue that everything arises and ends according to its
timing. A child is born from the womb, curd forms
from milk, bread is made from wheat, a tree grows
from a seed, and fruits come from the tree - all
according to their appointed times. They believe that
phenomena like the birth of a Cakravartī, the rise of a
86

Five Reasons

Tīrthaṅkara, stages of life like childhood, youth, old
age, birth, and death are all outcomes of time. Nothing
functions without time, and they regard time itself as
the driving force behind all actions.
Nature: It is important to clarify here that when we
speak of nature, we are not referring to individuals
like Mr. Tom or Henry, who may be labeled as goodnatured or bad-natured. Instead, the term 'nature' is
used to denote the inherent characteristics, qualities,
and self-expressive tendencies of everything.
Naturalists believe that nature is the driving cause
behind the world's phenomena. They argue that "a
barren woman never gives birth to a son, no hair
grows on the palms of hands, soles of feet, or on a
woman's face; mangoes never grow from a neem tree,
nor does a plantain or coconut emerge from a mango
seed. Peacock feathers grow only on peacocks, the
color of the evening sky, the thorns of a cactus,
different types of fruits, the stability of mountains, the
fickleness of air, and the intense heat of fire are all
results of nature itself, not merely outcomes of time.
Therefore, they arise from nature's inherent
characteristics.
Fish and tentacles float in water, while stones sink. A
single grain does not ripen on its own. Eating dry
ginger powder relieves gas, and certain medicines
cause diarrhea. A crow cannot produce milk, and a
conch does not grow from bones. The sun's heat, the
moon's coolness, and sugarcane's sweetness—all of
these things act according to their own nature, not
because of time. So, the cause of these actions lies in
their inherent qualities, not time."
87

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Destiny: People who consider destiny to be the cause,
defy time and nature. They believe the saying that
"Whatever is not supposed to happen, does not
happen. It happens as destiny would have it, not
otherwise. According to destiny, wherever a man's
mind is supposed to move, he is instinctively drawn
towards it. If destiny is favorable, then action done
even without thought bears the fruits.
Millions of blossoms grow on each branch of the
mango in the spring. Out of all of them, some fall,
some turn sour, some turn into mango, and some turn
into curries. Not all who are born live. This is an
indicator of its destiny."
They also share funny illustrations:
"A bird is sitting on the tree, chirping. A little far from
there, a hunter comes with arrows and clubs to hunt
the bird. With the bow in the arrow, he is ready to
shoot the bird. On the other hand, an eagle flying in
the sky catches sight of that bird. To take in its beak
and get the prey, the eagle is flying down to catch the
bird. Hunter's arrow from one side and strike of the
eagle from the other from either of these two
situations, the death of the bird seems final.
A hunter, ready to release his arrow at a bird, is
suddenly bitten by a snake on his foot. The arrow
slips from his grasp, missing its intended target.
Instead of hitting the bird, it strikes an eagle flying
nearby. The bird escapes unharmed, while the eagle,
believing it is about to catch its prey, is killed instead.
The hunter, who thought he had the bird in his sights,
becomes the target, and the bird that seemed doomed
to die, survives against all odds."
88

Five Reasons

The believer in destiny argues, using the example of a
threefold event, "What other explanation is there
besides destiny?" They go on to say, "Men return alive
from dense forests and bloody battlefields, while
others die peacefully at home in their beds. For all
events, what else could be the cause but destiny?"
According to this view, destiny is believed to be the
sole reason behind everything, responsible for all
actions and events.
Here, we have to understand the exact meaning of the
word 'destiny.'
Common people have two meanings of this word:
1. Man's destiny is shaped by his karma.
2. God's grace or God's will. People say things like, "I
said so. I did this." But, all of this is mere illusion.
Without the wish of God, not even a single leaf can be
moved.
Both interpretations of destiny are incorrect. In this
context, the term 'destiny' is not used in either sense.
Jain philosophers do not accept the existence of a God
as the doer of actions. They argue that the will to act
is a human tendency, and since instinctive behavior
cannot be attributed to God, they reject the idea of
divine intervention in actions.
In Hinduism, two forms of God were imagined,
embodied, and form-less. They have considered both
these forms as active agents. Jain philosophy has not
accepted this concept. It is possible that we would
dwell on an entirely different topic if we discuss this.
Rather, let us conclude this with what the Jain
philosophers have mentioned here and move ahead.
89

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

For the Jain philosophers, destiny means resolved or
determined. There are two divisions of time,
Utsarpiṇī and Avasarpiṇī; In each of those sections,
there is the fixed order of twelve Cakravartí (the great
emperor) and twenty-four Tīrthaṅkaras, and hence
they believe that there is a reason for this, which is
named as destiny. Along with four other reasons, this
cause works together. They do not believe that there
is only one reason behind all actions.
This opinion of philosophers is understandable.
According to this opinion, behind every action lies
destiny as the fifth inevitable cause.
In the formation of the universe and in the events of
the world, there are many such actions, behind which
there is something unfathomable cause beyond time,
nature, karma, and efforts. When those four reasons
fall short, in addition to bringing all those four factors
together, a fifth factor is also at work. The two
divisions mentioned in Jain scriptures with respect to
time, during which some work is done gradually and
steadily. During the phase of Utsarpiṇī, form, taste,
smell, body, lifespan, strength, and other attributes
get gradually elevated from the start of the phase till
the very end. Whereas in Avasarpiṇī, these attributes
gradually descend and diminish, right from the
beginning till the end. This sequence is fixed in each
division of the time cycle. Behind all this, the main
reason is destiny. Here, it is important to note that
Jain philosophers have said that every action does not
solely rely on destiny.
Another important point to consider is that when the
four factors combined fail to accomplish a task,
destiny does not come into play. Instead, in every
90

Five Reasons

action, all five factors work together simultaneously.
Each action involves multiple factors, with one
primary cause and four secondary causes. Therefore,
destiny is not the sole primary cause but rather one
of the secondary causes, serving as the fifth factor in
the process.
The group of people who believe that for every action,
there is one and only reason behind it i.e. a will of God,
accept such nature of destiny. On the other hand, Jain
philosophers consider the fifth factor, destiny, as one
of the five causes for outcome. This understanding
can be summarized as follows: if destiny is
considered the sole cause of all actions, then the
concept of karma and Puruṣārtha will get eliminated.
Referring back to the main point, we can clearly
understand that here the mention of destiny is
different from fate or karma. This fate is considered
the fourth cause i.e., our past actions, which we refer
to as karma.
Karma: Karma, also known as fortune, is a term that
signifies the result of past actions. An ardent believer
of this cause doesn't consider time, nature and
destiny as a cause of any outcome. They say:
"Whatever one does in the world, that becomes their
karma. Through karma, a living being can become an
insect, animal, human, or deity. Due to karma, Rama
underwent exile in the forest; due to karma, Sita faced
accusations and had to undergo trial by fire; only
through the power of karma, the Rā mā yana, the
Mahā bhā rata, and the battles of Panipat were fought;
the two world wars and Hitler's downfall happened
due to karma; the destruction of Ravana, the demise
91

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

of Duryodhana, the killing of Kansa; even Gandhi's
death by a bullet, all occurred due to the effects of
karma; through karma, one can become a king or a
beggar. A restless person wanders, while another
person reaps the fruits of their past deeds, without
any present efforts.
Due to karma, Tīrthaṅkara Ṛṡabhadeva did not get
food for a year and karma was the cause that
Tīrthaṅkara Mahāvira had to go through the pain of
getting nails hammered in his ear. After all, it was
karma that made Napoleon the king and the same
reason he died as a prisoner in jail."
While the believers in destiny gave an example of a
bird, the proponents of karma provide another
entertaining illustration:
"There was a basket in a locked condition lying
somewhere. At that time, a hungry mouse who had
been starving for many days saw that basket.
Knowing that there might be something to eat inside
the basket, it eagerly entered. The mouse started
nibbling the basket and eventually made a hole in it.
Someone kept a snake inside the basket. The hungry
snake had been starving for many days. As soon as the
mouse entered the hole, it landed straight in the
mouth of the snake and the snake devoured the
mouse, and with the meal fulfilled, it left the basket
from the same hole the mouse had created and went
into the forest.
Here, the mouse made the effort, but it died in the
process. However, the trapped snake inside fulfills its
hunger by consuming the mouse. Tell us, in this case,
did karma gain strength or something else?" Based on
92

Five Reasons

this example, the proponents of karma say, "In this
world, the cause of all actions is only one - karma."
Regarding understanding the meaning of the word
'karma,' no confusion must arise and hence, it should
be made clear that when we say endeavour or effort,
it should not be used to signify or mean karma.
Similarly, the actions performed in the present are
also referred to as karma. Instead, the word used here
signifies past deeds and their consequences.
Puruṣārtha: The meaning of this word means the
endeavour or effort put into any task. These
individuals believe that endeavour alone is the cause
of all outcomes, and let us also hear what they have to
share:
"Time, nature, destiny, or karma are powerless; only
endeavour has the capability. With endeavour, Lord
Rama built a bridge to Lanka, and with endeavour, he
attained victory and bestowed the kingdom upon
Vibhishana. It was through an endeavour that the
Pandavas defeated the Kauravas."
"Without effort, grains don't grow in the fields, and oil
doesn't extract itself from seeds. It is through hard
work that the crops are harvested; without it, there
would be no yield. Likewise, ingredients in a wellstocked kitchen do not become delicious dishes on
their own. If a task fails in the first attempt, one must
try a second, third, or even more times. With
consistent effort, success will eventually follow."
These individuals specifically mention "karma is the
son, and endeavour is the father of karma." It is
endeavour that has done the work and it is through
effort that one reaches accomplishment. By killing,
93

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Dṛḍhaprahāri had earned his karma, however,
through six months of hard work, he tackled and
erased all his karma.
Proponents of the endeavour claim: "Endeavour
alone is an exceptional power. It fills the pond drop
by drop, it builds a fence by placing one stone after
another, it makes a mountain like a majestic fort by
placing one brick after another. Effort alone is the
cause for earning money, fulfilling desire, doing good
deeds and attaining liberation. Even art and skill
become successful through the endeavour."
They say, "Effort is the only cause in this world and all
the actions of the world are subject only to this
impulse." On the other hand, they consider all the
other four causes as useless, fruitless, meaningless,
and impotent without endeavour.
Thus, these five causes differ in different ways, and
exclusively for each function, we have learned the
arguments of gentlemen who believe in their
respective causes.
Hence, some groups accept one cause while there are
the ones who accept all the five causes as a group.
Some believe fate and effort to be the cause of any
outcome. While some group destiny and endeavour
as one, others believe fate, efforts, and nature as a trigroup to be the cause.
However, in this matter, after thorough analysis,
gathering all evidence, and examining all foundations
and characteristics, and through the statements of
omniscient divine beings, Jain philosophers combine
these five causes into one group. It is said, "Generally,
until these five causes are not found together as one,
94

Five Reasons

nothing becomes possible." This statement should be
understood as completely true and equal. Now, let us
focus on the opinions of Jain philosophers.
The Doctrine of Five Causes: We have observed that
the way of Jain philosophers to test the nature of the
qualities and cause-effect relationships of every
object is unique and unparalleled. They do not
consider any object as one in an ultimate sense, as one
in form, or as one in exclusivity. Their perspective is
Anekānta, which means they say, "Nothing is
completely what it seems to be until it is viewed from
multiple perspectives. Exclusivity is falsehood, and
inclusivity is truth."
Regarding cause and effect, they say:
"When the five fingers of both hands work together,
only then can an action be performed. Without hands,
one cannot grasp anything, and without legs, one
cannot walk. Without clapping, there is no applause,
and without desire, no object or cause gains
significance.
While the supreme commander is credited with a
victory in war, success cannot be achieved by the
commander alone. It requires the strategic skill of the
leader, the strength and discipline of the soldiers, the
proper allocation of weapons, sufficient equipment,
efficient logistics, the support of the people, and,
above all, a just cause for which the war is fought.
The cloth is woven from yarn, but before that, there
are many factors at play - the sowing and growth of
cotton, the processing of cotton into thread, the
quality of the fibre, the weaver's efforts, the timing,
and even the fate of the mill owner. All these elements
95

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

must align. Yet, even after all this, if the producer or
consumer is not fortunate, circumstances may render
the cloth unusable. Ultimately, only if destiny permits
does the cloth become ready to be worn."
Jain philosophers have beautifully explained how
these five causes work together:
"Destiny brings the soul forth from the womb and out
of the Nigoda (a highly unconscious state). Through
karma, it attains human birth and gains the elements
necessary for spiritual progress. At the right moment,
the soul becomes inspired. If it possesses a divine
nature, it moves toward the path of liberation
through supreme efforts, ultimately reaching its final
destiny."
Today, this analogy can be interpreted in another way
as well:
"Due to destiny, the soul emerges from the Nigoda; it
becomes upward-moving by nature and time and
reaches to caramāvarta through karma, it acquires
the necessary elements useful for spiritual progress;
through endeavour, it advances on the path of
liberation." Here, if you think about this carefully, you
may notice that to get liberation, all 5 causes are
required. You cannot bypass or eliminate any of these
causes.
In the above passage, we also come across the terms
'Nigoda' and 'Caramāvarta', which we will briefly
understand here.
According to the belief of Jain philosophers, after
traversing infinite cycles of time, there comes a
Pudgala parāvartakāla, the final round of time. This is
96

Five Reasons

the soul's last time-bound transmigration in the cycle
of worldly existence. It is known as Caramāvarta. The
soul that emerges from the Nigoda and enters the
Caramāvarta experiences the passage of infinite time.
A detailed understanding of this aspect can be
obtained from the learned personalities.
The word 'Nigoda' is both significant and easy to
comprehend. To fully grasp its meaning, one must
thoroughly study the scriptures and explore various
texts. Although this may sometimes lead us away
from the main topic, let's briefly understand it.
According to Jain philosophy, all beings in this world,
from single-sensed organisms to beings with five
senses, come from Nigoda. Nigoda means "an infinite
and subtle body of an individual single-sensed being."
This Nigoda is countless in this realm. Countless
individual beings dwell in each one of them.
The beings residing in the Nigoda are devoid of
significant consciousness and any specific materials
or special efforts for attaining particular goals.
However, to get out of Nigoda, they are influenced by
the inevitability of destiny.
When beings attain liberation by the cessation of all
karmas, these beings emerge from the Nigoda. This is
the law of the cycle of the universe. The number of
beings who dwell in a particular Nigoda are 'n'
number of times higher than the beings who have
attained, are attaining, or will attain liberation
altogether. Due to this, the path to liberation is
constantly active, and this world, at any given time,
will never get emptied.

97

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The detailed exposition on the subject of the soul, as
done by Jain philosophers, is not found in any other
philosophical system. Especially, the description
related to Nigoda is unique and cannot be found in
any other knowledge. Modern scientists also support
this notion. They also believe that every hole is filled
with subtle organisms. They have stated, "If we take
the tip of a needle, without making it congested, the
tip of the needle would comfortably hold more than
one lakh of such tiny organisms."
This clearly indicates that Jain philosophers'
discussion of beings residing in the Nigoda is
authentic and evidence-based. The subject of
'Jívavícāra' (the contemplation about a living being) is
quite fascinating. By studying it, one can gain special
insights.
Regarding beings like Sthāvara (stationary), trasa
(mobile), sukṣama (subtle), and bādara (visible
organisms), as well as others, one can acquire
literature on this topic and read it or establish contact
with experts to explore further.
Now, let us return to the original topic. When five
factors come together to accomplish a task, we can
understand it based on Nigoda's examples mentioned
earlier. However, this is a complex matter. Here is a
simple and understandable analogy.
To establish a new fabric mill, several essential
resources are needed - initial capital, a well-planned
strategy developed through effort, and the coordinated skills of technicians and laborers. Fate,
effort, and nature are the critical factors initiating the
process. However, fabric production cannot be
98

Five Reasons

rushed; it requires the maturity of time, and only then
can the task be completed. From setting up each
component to advancing through various processes,
and considering the time required for development,
every step is crucial. Aspects like capital planning,
construction, installation, administration, execution,
processing, and finishing are all essential. Mastery in
each of these areas is required to successfully
complete the transformation.
However, if the cooperation of these favorable
conditions is not achieved, the game may be ruined
even after creating and assembling everything.
Thus, until the cooperation of the five causes is
obtained, cloth from the cotton, milk from the grass,
bread from the wheat, rice from the paddy, sugar
from the sugarcane or ornaments from the gilded
stone of the mine, cannot be made.
Therefore, the five factors collectively contribute to
the completion of any task. No single factor alone can
accomplish the entire task. One factor is primarily
emphasized or seen as the dominant component
among them. However, until all five come together, no
task can be completed. Here, it is important to
consider that the importance may vary depending on
the circumstances and context.
For example, in the process of turning milk into curd,
the act of adding yogurt to the milk is the primary
step. However, curd cannot form through effort
alone; if it could, curd would never spoil. This
highlights the importance of recognizing that
multiple factors are involved in completing any task.

99

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

In the view of Jain philosophers, from a practical
standpoint, true application of Anekāntavāda (the
doctrine of multiple perspectives) occurs when we
consider a cause from various angles, fostering effort,
and finding a balanced solution with a clear and open
mind. On the other hand, clinging to a single cause
while rejecting the value of other perspectives is
rooted in ignorance, stubbornness, and misguided
desires. This narrow thinking is known as Mithyātva
(falsehood), Ekāntavāda (one-sidedness), which
fosters attachment and aversion, leading to fallacy.
Anekāntavāda is 'samyaktva' (right belief). It
strengthens non-attachment and equality. It is the
true path of life. It is validated by experience. Above
all, these five factors, known as the doctrine of five
causes, are an integral part of Anekāntavāda.
Regarding the different arguments of proponents of
various causes related to the five factors, much can be
elaborated and written in opposition or in favor of
this doctrine. However, with the wisdom and
understanding ability of readers, and placing trust in
them, only a concise discussion has been carried out
here.
Now, let us move forward towards the contemplation
of Saptabhaṅgī (seven-fold answer), 'pramāṇa'
(evidence), and 'Nikṣepa' (application).

100

CHAPTER 10

Various aspects of Knowledge
Before beginning the conversation on 'Naya,' the first
step is to understand the meaning of the word
correctly. This word is a technical term used in
Jainism. Its common meaning is knowledge. However,
here, it is not used in its general sense but in a specific
context.
To know or comprehend anything, one requires some
form of knowledge. In schools, when various subjects
are taught, each subject represents a particular form
of knowledge.
Jain scholars have given an explanation of this term.
They say:
"A type of knowledge which explains the quality,
attribute or nature of an object, is called Naya."
Earlier, we saw that objects have many attributes.
Each attribute, quality, or virtue of an object is
understood and recognized using a different Naya. To
understand all the different qualities in different
ways, the Jain scholars have defined seven different
Nayas. Hence, in the following text, wherever Naya is
mentioned, it refers to all seven different types of
Naya.
There are two uses for Naya. One is for selfunderstanding, and the other is for explaining to
others.
101

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

We must first understand the relationship of this
Naya with Syādvāda. By giving an analogy in
figurative language, when we understand this matter
as a river and Syādvāda as the sea, everything else will
fall into place. Like all the rivers merge and meet
together in the sea, all these Naya merge into the vast
ocean of Syādvāda. Through this Naya, we can achieve
a complete comprehension of Syādvāda.
These seven Naya, represent different perspectives
for each object. While these 7 Naya may have
contradictory viewpoints among themselves,
together, they collectively serve the purpose of
Syādvāda. To understand this concept clearly, we can
take the analogy of two separate bank accounts of a
particular state. One account is used for income, and
the other for expenses.
The income account is solely for income, and the
