expense account is solely for expenses. Though there
are contradictory transactions in both accounts,
accounts serve that state only.
Go to any bank you want. The receiving cashier and
the paying cashier are both carrying out different
actions yet are serving the same bank.
In the same way, those who hold different opinions
within the 7 Naya can still serve the principle of
Syādvāda when they work together. The officials of
any kingdom will have varied opinions and diverse
contradictions yet will be working for the same
kingdom.
Now, this is the Naya we are referring to. It gives us
knowledge of various aspects and qualities of an
102

Various aspects of Knowledge

object. Jain philosophers have given us a concise
sentence here:
'Pramāṇanayairadhigamah' which means that
knowledge of any object is acquired in two ways through Pramāṇa and Naya.
According to scholars, Naya is considered as one part
of Pramāṇa. Let us clarify the concept of Pramāṇa.
Pramāṇa means 'evidence' or 'proof.' By using
Pramāṇa, one can unquestionably and accurately
know and understand an object.
Jain philosophy defines five types of knowledge
known as Matijñāna, Śrutajñāna, Avadhijñāna,
Mānahparyavajñāna, and Kēvalajñāna. Knowledge
itself is considered as Pramāṇa. These 5 types of
knowledge will be mentioned in the further texts.
In the science of logic, there are four dimensions. In
this context, 'direct' and 'indirect' are two primary
forms of Pramāṇa. Three divisions within indirect
evidence are outlined. The meanings of the terms
'direct' and 'indirect' are self-explanatory.
All these are presented sequentially as follows: (1) Pratyakṣa (Direct) Pramāṇa
(2) Anumāna (Conjecture) Pramāṇa
(3) Upamāna (Analogy) Pramāṇa
(4) Āgama (Scriptures) Pramāṇa
Let us understand these four types of Pramāṇa step
by step:
Pratyakṣa Pramāṇa: Our five senses are eyes, ears,
nose, tongue, and skin. Through these five senses and
the mind, we comprehend an object that is in contact
103

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

with them. This knowledge that is obtained through
the 5 senses and the mind is known as direct
evidence. For example, if we hold a flower in our
hand, we can perceive its form, fragrance, color,
shape, etc. This is an example of Pratyakṣa Pramāṇa.
Even if the flower is not physically present in our
hand, we can still smell it from a distance, we can
experience its fragrance through the nose. This
knowledge is also known as Pratyakṣa Pramāṇa.
Here, the objects that are in contact with our senses
are experienced by us directly. In this way, the
knowledge that is obtained through the five senses
and the mind is termed Sāmvyavahārika Pratyakṣa by
the Jain philosophers. It also has four distinctions. For
these four distinctions, in Jain philosophy, there are
four technical terms: Avagraha, Īhā, Apāya, and
Dhāraṇā. We can understand these four names by
unclear
perception,
blurred
perception,
determination, and remembrance.
From a distance, when we see something and
speculate, it is 'Avagraha' or an unclear perception.
When we get closer to it and start recognizing it, it
becomes 'Īhā' or blurred perception. Afterward,
when we determine the object, it is 'Apāya' or
determination. And later, when the memory of that
object gets stored in our mind, it is 'Dhāraṇā' or
remembrance.
For example, imagine a scenario where you see a
human-like figure from a distance, it is Avagraha. As
the figure approaches, you start to doubt whether it
is a man or a woman, it is Īhā. Apāya comes into play
when you determine that the figure is male and not
female. Store an image in one's mind is known as
104

Various aspects of Knowledge

Dhāraṇā or remembrance. It is essential to remember
that as long as the image of the male remains fixed in
your mind, it comes under Pratyakṣa Pramāṇa.
However, in the future, when we see and remember
him through our memory, that moment of recalling
comes under Parōkṣa Pramāṇa.
Anumāna (conjecture) Pramāṇa: The knowledge of
one thing based on another is Anumāna Pramāṇa
(conjecture). It is when we infer knowledge of one
object based on the knowledge of another object.
When we see smoke coming from a house and arrive
to an conclusion that it is due to fire, it means our
Anumāna is the reason for the conclusion. When we
see a man carrying a fire extinguisher, ringing the bell
incessantly, and going hurriedly, even then we must
consider that a fire must be burning somewhere
nearby. When we hear the voice of music somewhere,
we assume that a festival or a celebration is
happening nearby. All of this is considered Anumāna
Pramāṇa. This is very helpful for us when deciding
about something happening around us or far from us.
There are two types of 'far.' One is 'far from a certain
time' which implies a relation with the past or future,
and the other is 'far from a certain place,' which
means far from our place of residence.
In this way, knowledge of subtle things can also be
inferred from Anumāna Pramāṇa.
Upamāna (analogy) Pramāṇa: The knowledge of
the object based on the knowledge of another object
which looks similar to the former is called Upamāna
Pramāṇa. For example, we have a guest coming over.
He is observing a cow tied in our courtyard. Seeing
the cow, the brother says to us that the animal that
105

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

looks like a cow in his region is called 'Roz'. Later we
go to that brother's place, and see an animal that is
not a cow but is similar to a cow. That is when we
remember and recognize that this animal is Roz. This
is known as Upamāna Pramāṇa.
Āgama Pramāṇa: The knowledge that we gain from
the speech, statement, or writing of a trustworthy
person is called Āgama Pramāṇa. Generally, based on
words the knowledge that is gained is known as
Āgama Pramāṇa.
In this Āgama Pramāṇa, we often need to apply our
faith. In the scriptures, the topics that are shown by
words which we accept are only based on Āgama
Pramāṇa. One important thing in the matter of
scriptures is that there are no such matters included
in it that are contradictory to the outcomes of
Pratyakṣa Pramāṇa, Anumāna Pramāṇa, etc. It gives
the correct knowledge of self-development and casts
light on its path.
Jain scholars have considered Āgama Pramāṇa as
valid evidence. Because the knowledge that these
scholars have given is endowed by omniscient beings.
After attaining complete knowledge and omniscience,
they have given this knowledge and what they have
given is itself a big evidence. After eradicating
attachment, aversion, and ignorance, there is no
scope left for saying false. So, whatever he has said
has to be accepted. Therefore, what Tīrthaṅkara has
shared with us, whatever they have said, has been
said for only the good of the world.
Before we move forward, let us remember that,
among all the four, the first that comes is Pratyakṣa
106

Various aspects of Knowledge

Pramāṇa where the knowledge is presented to us
through the senses and the mind, whereas the other
three types - second, third, and fourth, are Parōkṣa
Pramāṇa and are only conveyed to us through the
mind and other mediums, and we must understand
this correctly.
In the case of Parōkṣa Pramāṇa, apart from these 3
types, 5 types have also been mentioned: Smaraṇa,
Pratyabhijñāna, Tarka, Anumāna and Āgama. We will
not delve into the details of these five here; however,
those who have a special interest in this subject
should seek the contact of knowledgeable persons.
Here, we must remember that the Naya does not
encompass the entire subject of Pramāṇa but just a
part of it has been incorporated into the Naya. A brief
analysis is carried out regarding Naya. Looking at
this, it is possible for a doubt to arise in someone's
mind, that, if each form of Naya speaks of the only one
form of things, then why isn't it called Ekānta
(solitariness) or Mithyātva (falsehood)?
The answer to this question, which has already been
given earlier, is simple. Right now, for obtaining the
answer to this question and understanding the
subject, let us move forward. Then, no place will be
left for any doubt or fallacious reasoning to arise
later.
When do we call it Ekānta? When we determine a
conclusion through a single point of view, denying the
acceptance of the second aspect of a thing, is what is
known as Ekānta. This is not applicable in the case of
Naya. When one Naya considers only one form of
thing, at that time, it does not deny the other forms,
107

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

despite being contradictory in its nature. For
example, according to Saṅgraha Naya, let us say that
the form of a thing is said to be 'some' which is
contradictory as compared to Naigama Naya. So, it is
the opposite situation. However, Saṅgraha Naya does
not deny or reject the aspect of Naigam Naya. In all
seven Nayas, remaining different characteristics of
the object are acknowledged as secondary ones in
their respective Naya. This means that knowledge of
Naya does not lead to falsehood.
Another important point to remember is that all these
Naya are parts of Syādvāda. The word 'syāt' in the
doctrine of Syādvāda serves its purpose. This use of
the word 'syāt' is not only for indicating the absence
of absolute assertion but also for indicating relative
perspectives. It is used to accept the contradictory
aspects of a particular thing and to prove the
acceptance of these as valid. And that is why Syādvāda
is considered a necessity and lies there as its
specialty, which we have already understood earlier.
Here we need to realize that an object can have both
a general and a specific meaning. Without considering
the general or specific context, if we talk about
something in the wrong way, it can lead to
misunderstandings. For instance, if someone tells us
that "The road ahead to the right is closed." This is a
general meaning. "Repairs are going on and the road
is closed for vehicular traffic," becomes a specific
meaning. It is possible that if we do not understand
the specific context, we might turn back or delay our
work unnecessarily.
If one does not understand the equivalence between
the general and specific meanings of objects and
108

Various aspects of Knowledge

words, then in many matters, especially in
philosophical discussions that involve intricate
concepts, there is a higher possibility of
misunderstandings.
Returning to the main subject, now pay attention to
this matter. In the introduction chapter, it is shown
that the Seven Naya is divided into two categories.
(1) Dravyārthika: Here, the term 'Dravya' is used to
denote the meaning 'general,' for example, when we
use the words 'Man' or 'Animal,' it has the general
meaning. Naigama, Saṅgraha, and Vyavahāra help in
understanding the common meaning, but it is
essential to remember that this common meaning can
also have different and contradictory implications.
(2) Paryāyārthīka: Here, the word 'paryāya' carries
the meaning 'specific.' We recognize substances as
some object, and we identify 'paryāya' as different
forms or appearances of the substance. When
distinctions in forms are made within the original
substance, then from there, specific meanings
emerge. For instance, the word 'human' might
generally refer to all humans, but in a particular
conference where he gives a lecture, it could take on
the specific meaning of a speaker. Ṛjusūtra, śabda,
Samabhirūdha, and evambhūta are the four Naya that
exhibit specific meanings for this purpose. These
specific terms identify substances with a unique form.
These specific forms can also be contradictory to each
other, so remember that.
With this perspective, the first three Naya, namely
naigama, saṅgraha, and vyavahāra are explained as
Naya with general meanings. The last four Naya,
109

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

namely Ṛujusūtra, śabda, Samabhirūdha and
evambhūta, are explained as Naya with specific
meanings.
Now, it's also important to understand the second
aspect here. Previously, we discussed the Fourfold
Basis: Matter (Dravya), Space (Kṣetra), Time (Kāla),
and Characteristic (Bhāva). Similarly, here, while
thinking about Naya, we also need to understand
another fourfold aspect.
This aspect has been presented under the concept of
Nikṣepa. This Nikṣepa has four divisions as follows:
Nāma Nikṣepa
Sthāpanā Nikṣepa
Dravya Nikṣepa
Bhāva Nikṣepa
Since the word Nikṣepa is a technical word, let us
understand it properly. Nikṣepa means division. Any
one word has four divisions to it. One is a noun or the
name; the second is a form; the third is an attribute
related to past or future and the fourth is a present
attribute. When we associate a certain word with a
particular object or when we associate an object with
a certain word, we say that we have done Nikṣepa.
Each of these divisions corresponds to an aspect of an
object. Nāma represents the name, Sthāpanā
represents its form, Dravya represents an attribute
related to the past or future, and Bhāva represents
present attributes. Nikṣepa helps us clearly
understand and categorize different aspects of an
object.
110

Various aspects of Knowledge

When we associate certain meanings with a word
through its relationship, that process is referred to as
Nikṣepa (Classification). What is especially
interesting here is that irrespective of the number of
meanings we assign to a word, the meaning of that
word can be understood and comprehended using
these four classifications.
If we can understand its meaning accurately and
appropriately, then instead of using lengthy phrases,
we can simply use the word ‘Nikṣepa’ to convey the
same idea. This makes our communication more
efficient and precise.
Now let us sequentially understand these four
divisions of Nikṣepa:
1. Nāma Nikṣepa: To understand and control
interactions and our own comprehension, we provide
a specific and controlled identification, commonly
known as a name. Through this, any object can be
recognized for comprehension. By assigning a certain
name or label to it, we distinctly categorize and
comprehend things. For instance, when identifying
each of Mr. X's five sons, each is designated with a
separate name to distinguish them.
The act of assigning a name is referred to as Nāma
Nikṣepa. This projection involves giving a name to an
object. There is a connection between the name and
the object, but the name does not necessarily carry
any specific meaning or essence. When a particular
name is assigned to an object, substance, or
individual, it does not necessarily reflect a significant
meaning or essence related to it. However, for
identification, when a name is bestowed upon an
111

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

individual, it becomes a part of their identity, which is
what Nāma Nikṣepa implies.
For instance, Hanumanji's alternate name is
'Bajrangbali.' This second name reflects the distinct
qualities present in Hanumanji and is used to denote
those attributes. This second name signifies an
attribute; and will come under Bhāva Nikṣepa.
Whereas the name 'Hanumanji' will fall under Nāma
Nikṣepa.
2. Sthāpanā Nikṣepa: The process of Sthāpanā
Nikṣepa involves assuming or attributing one entity
within another. In this case, the name of the
established entity is used to identify that object. In
this context, there are two distinctions: Tadākāra
Sthāpanā and Atadākāra Sthāpanā.
When we sculpt an idol of a deity or a human, we
assign it the name of that deity or a human as an act
of devotion. If the object is made of stone, then it may
have the shape of a deity or an individual. In this case,
we associate an entity, whether divine or human, with
the created form. This Tadākāra Sthāpanā reveals the
physical manifestation.
On the other hand, in the context of leisure activities
like crafting using wood or plastic in the game of
chess, we assign names such as king, queen, knight,
rook, bishop, and others. Here, the act of attributing
those names to the pieces made from wood or plastic
is equivalent to the concept of Sthāpanā Nikṣepa.
However, in the case of pieces like those used in the
game of chess, where there is no specific shape
associated with the names like king, queen, knight,

112

Various aspects of Knowledge

rook, or bishop, and the act of assigning names to
shapes is known as Atadākāra Sthāpanā.
In the context of cinema, the portrayal of the original
individual's
characteristics
in
characters,
photographs, sculptures, and the like, is termed
Tadākāra Sthāpanā. In games like chess, where there
is no specific shape associated, the act of
characterizing pieces is known as Atadākāra
Sthāpanā.
In conclusion, any object or entity that has been
distinctly named, whether it is given a specific form
or not, is a case of Sthāpanā Nikṣepa.
In Nāma Nikṣepa both name and the individual come
together, whereas in Sthāpanā Nikṣepa, form and
personality arrive together.
3. Dravya Nikṣepa: Relating to the past and future
times, to express the essential nature of an object or
individual by its name in the present, is known as
Dravya Nikṣepa.
After gaining independence in India, the integration
of all native states into Indian took place. Those who
were once the kings of these states are no longer
called 'kings.' At present times, we refer to these great
personalities as Kings of such-and-such states when
addressing, identifying, and acknowledging them.
This is an example of Dravya Nikṣepa.
Similarly, in the future, if someone were to inherit a
fortune worth lakhs of rupees, we may even use the
term 'millionaire' for them in contemporary times.
There can be an arrangement wherein a person gets
a fixed amount of number after reaching a certain age.
113

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

However, today that person may not possess such
wealth. In the current scenario, we use the term
'millionaire' for them at present times. This is an
example of Dravya Nikṣepa.
Clearly, now it will be understood that a word used in
the present time to attribute a certain object or
individual to its past or future event is termed Dravya
Nikṣepa.
4. Bhāva Nikṣepa: To address any object or
individual considering its present condition or
qualities as per the attributes present in the present,
is termed as Bhāva Nikṣepa. The phrase 'Narendra
Modi' explains Nāma Nikṣepa. If we give a name to an
old photograph of them, it is Sthāpanā Nikṣepa. Even
after they have stepped down, we still refer to them
as the former Prime Minister which is Dravya
Nikṣepa. In the present, where they are still holding
the office of Prime Minister, referring to them as
India's Prime Minister is Bhāva Nikṣepa.
Similarly, the act of donating makes the giver 'donor,'
ruling makes the ruler 'king,' wrestling makes the
person 'wrestler,' writing poetry makes the person
'poet,' removing an association makes the person
'disassociated,' and taking the lead in an organization
makes the person 'leader.' Recognizing them through
these words and in this manner via their actions is
Bhāva Nikṣepa.
In these four Nikṣepa, we recognise one object or
individual through four different aspects without
altering its fundamental nature.
In the first instance, recognition is through a term or
name; in the second, establishment concerning the
114

Various aspects of Knowledge

original form of an individual or object; in the third,
connection with the past or future times, and in the
fourth, the present relation of that individual or
object with their current attributes. These four types
of identification have been expounded.
After explaining these aspects of identification, we
now comprehend the relation between Naya and
Nikṣepa.
'Naya' signifies knowledge-based,
knowledge-oriented. Using 'Naya,'
knowledge of an object.

verbal or
we attain

This Nikṣepa is related to the meaning of a word.
Sometimes, a word may denote a name, a form, or a
shape. Sometimes, it indicates their past or future
form and sometimes, it is a real attribute. We
establish a connection between a word and its
meaning. Thus, we can say that Nikṣepa is a part of
knowledge i.e., Naya. Naya denotes an angle of
thinking. Nikṣepa also denotes an angle through
which the relationship between a word and its
meaning is seen.
We have now understood the meaning of the term
'Naya,' acquired a general knowledge of it, and
thereby grasped the context related to it. Now, we
proceed to understand the seven types of Naya
sequentially for a comprehensive understanding.

115

CHAPTER 11

7 Naya
In the previous chapters, we understood the concepts
of Pramāṇa and Nikṣepa in common terms. We can
see that these concepts provide us with knowledge
about objects or substances. In this manner, we
acquire reliable knowledge.
When we contemplate Naya, we will understand that
through direct and indirect evidence i.e. Pramāṇa, we
gain complete knowledge of the true nature of an
object. On the other hand, Naya provides us with
knowledge of the partial aspects of objects, seen
differently through various perspectives.
Hence, we will also know Naya as opinions. Like
Pramāṇa is pure knowledge, Naya too is pure
knowledge. The only difference is that we find out
about the integral nature of things through Pramāṇa.
Through Naya, we become aware of partially
different forms of things.
In every laboratory, when an object is submitted for
analysis, the laboratory scientist, after examining it,
provides us with a list. If we scrutinize this list, we can
discern the extent to which the object has been
dissected, thereby gaining a comprehensive
understanding of its components.
An understanding about the elements of blood can be
given by any pathologist to consider. After conducting
116

7 Naya

tests on various diseases related to the blood, a
precise diagnosis can be made, and effective
treatment can follow. All of us are aware of this.
In the same way, the method of knowing about
different things through Naya is an analytical process.
Jain philosophers have established seven types of
laboratories for categorizing the different aspects of
objects, and they have named them as Seven Naya.
Below stated are the 7 Naya:
Naigama Naya
Saṅgraha Naya
Vyavahāra Naya
Ṛjusūtra Naya
Ṡabda Naya
Samabhīrúḍha Naya
Evamabhūta Naya
Let us now understand each of these Naya separately
in order. One thing to keep in mind here is that Naya
is a vision, a perspective to see things from. In this
chronology of Naya, the latter ones are more subtle
than the former ones.
1. Naigama Naya: When we contemplate both the
general and specific aspects of an object separately,
we refer to it as Naigama (figurative knowledge).
In this context, the original word is Nigam, which
means resolution or decision. Nigam can also be
interpreted as imagination. However, this
imagination does not refer to the creation of unreal
qualities but rather to the understanding of the
117

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

manifestation of real qualities. The term Nigam is
applied to describe outcomes based on this
understanding.
Two specific points are highlighted in this Naya. One
is that this Naya shows the past, present and future,
all of them as present. Let us see how that is.
After deciding on plans to go to America, our friend
Parmanand says to us, "I am going to America." Now,
when he says this, at that moment, he is right in front
of us in India. However, he has made the resolution to
go on this journey and thus say, "I am going." We do
not disagree with that. He will be going in the future
but since he has made the resolution, we can use the
present tense considering this situation.
Addressing students in medical colleges, the term
'doctor' is common. Students say, "Once I pass, I will
work as a doctor in the future." But when they make
this resolution and start their studies, they say, "I am
going to become a doctor." Considering their practical
actions, we use the present tense in this context.
In all these cases, the use of words in practical
situations follows the concept of Nigam. Here, the
term 'resolution' comes into play. Thus, it is known as
Saṅkalpa Naigama.
In life, a person sometimes faces situations where
they encounter a challenging moment or accident,
such as falling off a bicycle. In those instances, they
exclaim, "I have died... oh my goodness... I am
finished." We can see that they say this, but they
haven't finished or died. In business, when someone
makes a significant mistake and realizes it, they use
118

7 Naya

phrases like "It's clear, it's over, I am done" to indicate
that mistake. But one can say, "Everything is
finished," at the time of paying and settling liabilities.
Similarly, when a wall or a roof of a building collapses
or falls, people may say, "The building has fallen."
In this context, events or occurrences that are
anticipated to transpire in the future or are
reasonably expected to occur are being described, but
only partially. The practical acknowledgment and
acceptance of such matters are referred to as Anṡa
Naigama.
Similarly, when we say, "Tīrthaṅkara is liberated," we
refer to both the past and the future in the context of
the present. Thus, in these expressions, both the past
and the future merge into the present.
When a task is initiated, but it is not yet completed,
even then we say, "This work is done." This happens
quite often. When we start cooking in the kitchen and
say, "Today, I have prepared gourd curry," even
though the dish is not ready yet, or it might just be in
the initial stages, we still refer to it as "the dish is
prepared" in a present context. When something that
hasn't been done yet, we refer to it as if it's already
done. This is referred to as 'Āropaṇa Naigama.'
For instance, when we say, "This is my right hand, this
is my protective umbrella, this is my heart's garland,
this is my everything," referring to various objects for
distinct reasons, we use this Naigama to represent
these kinds of expressions.
On the day of the death anniversary of a Tīrthaṅkara,
we say, "Today they attained Nirvāṇa." In this context,
119

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

the word 'today' is indicative of the present, while the
act of attaining Nirvāṇ a might have occurred many
years ago, making it historical. Even then, we mention
it in the present tense. When we do so, we are
attributing the past to the present. This phenomenon
is also described as Āropaṇa a Naigama.
Through this, we have seen that Naigama helps us
reframe past and future events as well as the
incomplete events of the present into the present
context. This is something that must be greatly
understood when it comes to Naigama Naya.
The second thing is of a general and specific form. We
have said above that Naigama Naya considers the
general and special dual forms of different things. Let
us take an example to understand this.
As we mentioned above, when we prepare a group
photo including our family and friends on occasions
like weddings or similar events, we describe this
photograph as our friend circle and family photo
when we refer to it in a general context. However,
later, when we identify individuals like sons,
daughters, wives, brothers, sisters, etc., by naming
them separately in the same photograph, we use
specific terminology to describe them.
When we describe both the common and special
characteristics of every object in such a way that we
give them a common identity, but still recognize their
individual identities separately, Naigama Naya
accepts it; however, it provides distinct identification
to each in different ways. This is what Naigama Naya
is about.
120

7 Naya

2. Sangraha Naya: This Naya provides recognition of
the general form of an object. In Naigama Naya, both
the common and special characteristics of the object
are mentioned, but in this, we primarily recognize the
general form of the object.
This is also known as the Collective or Synthetic
Approach. Here, Synthetic means Synthesis which is
defined as unification.
This Naya provides recognition to each object based
solely on its common characteristics. The essence of
this concept is that there is nothing that exists which
is separate from common. For example, it does not
differentiate between different plants, such as
oranges or mangoes. It does not consider individual
fingers separate from the hand, nor does it see the
hand as distinct from the body.
Naya, neither should become impure and remain
pure, so here we should first keep the word 'Syāt' in
mind. The name of this Naya is 'Sangraha,' which
means, it shows us the collective form of objects.
When we use words like living beings, humans,
animals, minerals, etc., we include various kinds
within each word. Through this Sangraha Naya, we
recognize these objects based on their common or
collective attributes. For instance, in a cupboard,
there may be various types of clothing like coats,
shirts, dresses, trousers, and saris, but this collection
is identified as 'clothing.' In the context of grains, you
may have wheat, rice, lentils, chickpeas, etc., but
instead of mentioning each separately, this collection
is referred to as grains.

121

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

We can observe that in practical usage as well, we
only emphasize the common meaning in this way.
This usage is based on Sangraha Naya.
Let's first recall the word 'Syāt.' In the Naigama Naya,
we find two aspects of an object: the common and the
specific. We have accepted the 'common' aspect and
based on this 'Sangraha Naya' has been established.
However, when we bring the word 'Syāt' into the
picture, we realize that this 'Sangraha Naya' only
recognizes the common aspect of an object. It does
not oppose the other aspect.
3. Vyavahāra Naya: According to this Naya, we only
consider the unique aspect of an object. The Sangraha
Naya considers the common form of an object, while
this Vyavahāra Naya introduces us to the specific
aspect, different from the common.
This Naya is called the Practical, Individual,
Distributive, or Analytical Approach. It can also be
referred to as Gradations.
Vyavahāra Naya considers the object to have specific
characteristics. According to its perspective, a word
like 'animal' encompasses both animals with tails and
tailless ones, those with horns and hornless ones, and
many other variations of animals. Consequently, its
precise meaning becomes unclear. The specific
meaning is not clear from this, so it is not understood.
When someone says, "Buy fruits," what does that
mean? It can include neem, guava, mango, and all the
common fruits. If it does not specify a particular
meaning, who will buy what? It does not function
effectively with just the 'common' aspect. It is only
122

7 Naya

through specific meaning that transactions occur
effectively.
The Sangraha Naya is completely the inverse of
Vyavahāra Naya and in daily life, both perspectives
are useful and necessary. We also use them in our
day-to-day affairs based on the situation. For
example, when buying sweets at a sweet shop, we say
'shop of sweets' in common sense. But when we need
to buy a particular sweet, we say 'shop for that
particular sweet.' This means that both perspectives
complement and are useful in practical life.
Here too, let's keep in mind the word 'Syāt'. When
discussing Syādvāda, it is essential to focus on the
specific aspect of an object. However, it is essential to
remember that Syādvāda also accepts the synergy of
other opinions. That is the specialty of Anekānta.
Previously, we said that these three Naya are
Dravyārthika or related to the common meaning of
the object. However, here, we see that Vyavahāra
Naya reveals the specific forms in the context.
Someone naturally asks, "Why like this?" Here, we
must remember that what we reveal in the 'specific'
is 'common-specific,' so it is included in the
'Dravyārthika.' The last four Naya are 'Paryāyārthika'
Naya. The view of this Naya is subtler than the first
three. Now, let's look at the fourth Naya.
4. Ṛjusūtra Naya: This Naya shows the current state
of the object in both the physical and subtle senses. It
believes the object which exists at present and its
own self. In simpler terms, it believes the thing in its
present condition. This opinion does not consider the
object's past, or future; it only considers its present
123

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

state or form. It does not accept the attributes of
someone else's object. It suggests that one's work is
never done by someone else's alternatives. Due to the
inability to perform during future, past, or someone
else's work, this Naya considers these things as nonexistent and unobtainable.
The Ṛjusutra Naya, which accepts the distinction
between the subtle and physical differences of the
present time, is both the simple present and
continuous present. The words 'today' and 'right
now' indicate the present time. With these two
distinctions, the present time is accepted in this
Ṛjusutra Naya.
From the point of view of this Naya, a thing which is
not present in the present time and does not have its
own self, is considered incomplete and useless in the
present time. For example, only what we have in the
present with us is what is useful to us. The past or
someone else's object is of no use. If we have a bicycle
that is useful in the present, this Naya considers it as
a bicycle, otherwise, it doesn't.
5. Ṡabda Naya: The Naya which is used to denote the
difference in meaning based on grammar elements
such as gender, number, tense, and others is known
as Ṡabda Naya. This Naya considers an object as only
one though it is identified by various words, i.e.,
synonyms. However, if the gender and number of the
word are different, then the object too is different. For
example, when using the word 'house' and 'home,' for
an object, this Naya will consider this object as only
one with no differences. When using the words Ghado
(pot) and Ghadi (a small pot), one is a masculine term
124

7 Naya

and the other is a feminine term, making them
different from each other.
We can take the word 'person' as an example, where
'man,' 'woman,' and 'others' mean three different
things. When changing to the plural form, there are
still differences in meaning. Similarly, when we use
words like sweetness, beauty, tenderness, strength,
quality, etc., gender and number affect their
meanings. Therefore, even when using the plural
form, there may be differences in meaning.
A word that indicates a specific meaning, is used to
indicate the same meaning while considering this
Naya. For example, instead of referring to man and
woman in the common term as 'people,' it specifies
them as male and female.
So this Ṡabda Naya shows the differences in meaning
based on gender, species, number, tense, and other
grammatical elements. Since grammar is an essential
part of language, we can refer to this concept as a
grammatical approach.
6. Sambhiruḍha Naya: Word differentiation leads to
differentiation in meaning. When a single object is
distinguished through different words, we refer to
these words as synonyms. These distinct words have
different meanings due to differences in their origin.
This concept acknowledges the differences in
meaning attributed to distinct words. So, when you
use various words like pot, jug, and vessel to identify
a single object, Ṡabda Naya considers it as synonyms,
as they refer to the same entity with subtle variations.
Ṡabda Naya emphasizes that changing the name of an
object (using synonyms) doesn't necessarily alter its
125

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

meaning. In Ṡabda Naya, when you switch from 'vase'
to 'bottle,' the essence of the object remains the same.
While Sambhiruḍha Naya considers it as different
objects because of differences in their origin.
This is also referred to as specific knowledge. This
concept is particularly significant because it focuses
on the original and specific meaning of a particular
word. For example, Ṡri Kriṣna has various names.
Each name has a specific meaning. All those names in
practice indicate the same name of Ṡri Kriṣna,
although they all have different meanings from the
point of view of this Naya.
The word 'ruler' means the one who rules. When the
word 'protector' is used for him, at that time, there is
a difference in meaning because of the difference in
words. Here, to rule and to protect both the practice
comes with the rule. But, Sambhiruḍha Naya will use
the word where applicable in its true sense. Thus, this
Naya explains the differences in meaning when we
use different words.
7. Evambhuta Naya: This is an active Naya. It accepts
the meaning of a word based on its activeness and,
when the action is taking place. Its name is
Evambhuta because it signifies the word's meaning.
Evam means like that and Bhuta means an object. It
accepts only those objects that are functioning at
present with respect to the meaning of that particular
word, that makes sense, otherwise it will not.
We see above that Ṡabda Naya shows the difference
in meaning based on its grammar. When Evambhuta
Naya indicates differences in meaning based on its
functioning. It is worth noting that in the context
126

7 Naya

where the actions mentioned in the word's meaning
are not taking place, that meaning is not accepted.
For example, singer denotes the one who sings as its
primary meaning. However, the Evambhuta Naya
does not accept singers when someone is not singing.
In contrast, when a person sings, they can be referred
to as a singer. Similarly, when a person performs a
ritual, they can be referred to as a worshipper.
This principle is commonly observed in practical
situations. For example, a government employee or
labourer engaged in their duties experiences a
different approach from the administrative system or
industrial process during working hours compared to
how they are treated after their duties are completed,
i.e., during non-working hours.
In the case of a labourer working in a mill, if they are
accidentally injured or die while working, then at that
time, they become eligible for compensation.
Similarly, on the road, if a mill worker meets with an
accident or if any place they are visiting for other than
work purposes leads to an accident, then the workers'
compensation will not apply.
In both these cases, when these individuals were
actively engaged in an action, when they were
performing an action, at that time, Evambhuta Naya
acknowledged them as government officials and
workers. Once the action is completed, Evambhuta
Naya recognizes them based on their original names.
These scenarios demonstrate that Evambhuta Naya
applies to those names that are associated with
actions.
127

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Thus, we have seen the seven Naya. We call them
cognitive entities for specific human understanding.
This is the general definition and not an absolute
understanding but rather, a relative one. This fact
shouldn't be forgotten.
As mentioned earlier, these Naya become purer in
ascending order. Furthermore, each new aspect is
subtle, and even though they are different sides of the
same object, when all these aspects come together,
they provide a complete understanding of the object.
Combining these aspects makes the object whole.
The seven Naya, when combined, are referred to as
reliable knowledge. It is important to note that if all
these viewpoints are mutually consistent and
harmonious, then it represents the truth. In such
cases, they are also termed as Sunaya (good naya).
Otherwise, it is considered false or erroneous, leading
to misconceptions. In such cases, one viewpoint
contradicts the other viewpoint, which is termed as
Durnaya.
It is important to revisit the term 'Syāt' once again.
This term is used to indicate relativism in the context
of other viewpoints. To accept a certain characteristic
in a certain manner, considering it relatively correct
without contradicting other perspectives, is the
essence of Syādvāda and is a unique feature of Jain
philosophy.
Every object possesses multiple characteristic
attributes. With the help of Naya, knowledge arises
that comprehends these distinct characteristic
attributes, and this knowledge, too, varies. According
128

7 Naya

to each individual's caliber and category, they can
perceive or comprehend it.
In the four types of viewpoints that we observe in the
later pages, these viewpoints do not create significant
differences when it comes to the entirety of the
object. However, when we approach the object with
partial perspectives, distinctions in viewpoints do
exist. To resolve these differences in viewpoints, we
have the concept of Naya.
Our
cognitive
understanding,
defined
as
Adhyavasāya, is our perspective. This perspective
functions in two different ways: through word and
meaning.
There are two types of words. One is conventional,
and the other is derived from origin. Similarly, in
meaning, there are also two distinctions. One is
common, and the other is specific.
From the 7 Naya that we have seen, the first four
namely Naigama, Saṅgraha, Vyavahāra, and Ṛjusutra
are meaning oriented. The last three - Ṡabda,
Sambhiruḍha, Evambhuta are word-oriented Naya.
The Naigama Naya encompasses both the common
and specific meanings of an object. The Sangraha
Naya, on the other hand, only accepts the common
meaning. In Vyavahāra Naya, people accept the
specific meaning used without considering common
meanings. When the Ṛjusutra principle solely accepts
the present moment, it defines the practical meaning
of present actions. Thus, these four Naya are
considered as Naya dealing with meaning.

129

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The Ṡabda Naya is the acceptance of the conventional
behaviour of words. The Samabhiruḍha Naya directs
our attention towards the interpretation of words
based on its origin. The most recent Evambhuta Naya
accepts the present active state and acknowledges
the object as it is when it is in action. Through this,
these three Naya are dealing with words.
This is all about philosophical and thoughtful
discussions. However, when it comes to ethical or
religious practices, Jain philosophers have outlined
two Naya to be more specific. These two Naya are
meant for the cause of ethical conduct. Jain
philosophers have explained these two Naya as
Vyavahāra Naya and Niṡcaya Naya.
Here, Niṡcaya has one meaning as destination,
whereas the former one is considered as an
instrument to reach its destination. For instance,
when you meditate, you enhance your inner strength.
In this context, enhancing inner strength is the goal,
which is called destination. The act of meditating falls
under the category of instrument. Similarly,
practicing Sāmāyika helps attain equanimity. Here,
equanimity is the goal, and the practice itself is part
of the practical activities.
Vyavahāra refers to the adaptable external body of
Niṡcaya. As an example, Niṡcaya Samyaktva signifies
experiencing the true essence of the soul. An external
code of conduct which is adaptable to that experience
is Vyavahāra Samyaktva.
What's important to note here is that practicing code
of conduct while keeping Niṡcaya in mind is
130

7 Naya

considered to be a true Vyavahāra. Otherwise, it
would be an illusion to Vyavahāra.
This concept of Niṡcaya and Vyavahāra is very
important. In both the spiritual and material realms,
our true elevation can be achieved through our clear
understanding of Niṡcaya and Vyavahāra.
Let us talk about the spiritual realm.
"The soul is inherently pure and bound by karma,
meaning it is impure." This statement is universally
accepted. In the spiritual field, if we desire to
progress, we must decide to purify the impurities
attached to the soul by avoiding actions that bind the
soul and focusing on establishing the true nature of
the soul. When we make such a decision and act upon
it, a pure vision (Niṡcaya) will develop. Now, when we
make such a determination and develop such a vision
once, and then, by focusing on the soul's only goal of
eliminating impurities, it will continue to guide us in
purifying all our behaviours.
The Jain philosophers have expressed the means of
attaining liberation as jñāna-kriyābhyām mokṣah
(liberation through knowledge and action.) This
sentence mentions both vision and conduct (Niṡcaya
and Vyavahāra). Here, 'We must liberate our soul,'
this knowledge is what vision is, and for this, any
action, behaviour, etc., in the form of activity is
conduct.
When we observe our body in a conscious state, we
see the connection of conscious life with material
substance. The doer and experiencer of all our
activities, the soul, is always the same; this fact we
131

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

also know. The ultimate goal for this soul is to become
free from its impure nature mixed with material
substances and, by doing so, to manifest its pure
nature. This pure form is the fundamental element of
the living form itself. Here, the perspective that
touches the pure state of the soul is the Niṡcaya Naya.
And the perspective which touches the present state
is Vyavahāra Naya. Here, a perspective that touches
the soul's present state, bound with conditions of
Niṡcaya Naya, shows us a course of action to reach the
destination. This was a brief conversation on
Vyavahāra.
While talking about our behaviour, we need to decide
on the code of conduct, keeping our eyes on Niṡcaya
Naya. The vision that looks at Niṡcaya is the pure
perspective of knowledge. In our conduct, it works to
eliminate impurities and prevent them from entering.
Without keeping our goal in focus, our actions might
end up being futile. Similarly, in the spiritual realm,
by separating the vision, we mold our course of
action, and that only leads to our downfall. Therefore,
Jain philosophers have emphasized that the internal
and external aspects of life should be kept pure and
elevated.
If we have a pure perspective but improper
behaviour, or if we have an improper perspective but
pure behaviour, it will be harmful for us.
Jain philosophers have mentioned that the actions
which bind the soul become a reason for liberation,
for knowledgeable and discerning ones. Whereas, the
actions which may liberate the soul become a reason
for bondage for ignorant or undiscerning. This
132

7 Naya

statement is profound, indicating that the same
actions can be bondage for the ignorant and
undiscerning individuals, while liberation for the
knowledgeable and discerning ones, freeing them
from the shackles of karma.
For example, when a knowledgeable and discerning
person engages in charitable activities, they need to
maintain a genuine sense of benevolence and avoid
any sense of ego. They need to consistently cultivate
a humble attitude. Conversely, when someone lacking
knowledge and undiscerning performs even a
seemingly small act for the welfare of others, it may
either be tainted by arrogance, leading to harm, or it
may nurture a profound sense of pride as if he has
performed a significant act of service. Here, the
distinction lies in understanding - for one, the action
leads to the dissolution of karmic consequences,
while for the other, it becomes a source of karmic
bondage.
When a vision of Niṡcaya and Vyavahāra maintains
continuous contact, as mentioned above, there is no
resulting karmic bondage. We recognize Niṡcaya as
the destination and Vyavahāra as the means to
achieve the destination. In this, it becomes evident
that the outcome is such that if what is 'achievable'
does not align with the 'means to achieve,' then the
effort becomes fruitless and futile. This effort, aiming
for the achievable, will remain ineffective until it
aligns with the means to achieve. If one's focus
deviates from the destination, distortions in one's
efforts will definitely occur.

133

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The subject of vision of Niṡcaya demands a vast
understanding. Jain philosophers have marveled at
this phenomenon. If this concept is fully
comprehended, then the path of practical conduct
naturally becomes certain. Thus, the ascertained path
of practical conduct becomes an unwavering and
elevated path.
In the discourse on religion and philosophy, we
observe that philosophical knowledge provides us
with contemplation, and religion teaches us conduct.
This contemplation is what is known as the vision of
Niṡcaya, and the practical aspect of it is the vision of
Vyavahāra. The profound contemplation and
corresponding conduct are intricately intertwined
and are equally significant, and we understand this.
Similarly,
destination
and
conduct
are
interdependent. The destination cannot be reached
without virtuous conduct, and virtuous conduct
cannot be achieved without a clear destination.
Spiritual science guides us to our ultimate goal. This
ultimate goal by keeping our gaze unwaveringly
focused on it, involves practicing the same behaviour
and conduct that is truly the true vision of Vyavahāra.
We have clearly understood what is the vision of
Niṡcaya. Now, a little consideration is needed about
conduct. Because understanding a destination and
holding it as a belief is one thing, and sustaining
behavior is another. It is not difficult to accept a goal.
Conducting oneself according to that acceptance may
pose a real challenge.
In this world, we observe many individuals who,
despite having noble intentions, cannot engage in
134

7 Naya

virtuous conduct. The reasons for this can be diverse.
Jain philosophers, who have formulated the doctrine
of Syādvāda and Anekāntavāda, have also given equal
consideration to this matter. In the context of Niṡcaya
(destination) and Vyavahāra (behavior), Syādvāda
beautifully balances both. For those engaged in the
bound worldly life due to karma, adopting a certain
perspective in their conduct can be quite challenging.
This is why they have mentioned the concepts of
Utsarga (a main course of conduct) and Apavāda (a
diversion from the main course of conduct) in
practicality.
Utsarga means adhering to a certain path directing
towards Niṡcaya – a right royal highway. On the other
hand, Apavāda means adopting strategies in defense
of the original path, taking the form of a diversion.
This Apavāda is not only for the protection of the
original path but also to successfully follow it. It has
been presented as one of the effective methods for
achieving success in the pursuit of the achievable.
To understand this, let's take a simple and practical
example.
Suppose you are traveling from Ahmedabad to Agra
by a motor vehicle, following the National Highway.
Along this route, there might be roadblocks due to
repairs, or other issues. What would you do at such a
point? Would you stay stuck and unmoving? No.
Instead, you would divert to the side roads and take
an alternative path, the adjacent path of the main
route. This alternate path is also utilized, which
enables you to come back to the original main road.
Here, instead of being hindered by roadblocks or
135

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

pitfalls on the main path, you are using another path,
but your focus remains on eventually returning to the
original main road.
Through the vision of Vyavahāra, we realize that in
the conduct of religion as well, we may encounter
situations where, due to not having any other option,
we need to resort to the path of Apavāda (alternative
means). However, even in such alternative means,
our focus should remain on Niṡcaya. Whenever we
have to employ an alternative means or diversion, we
should use it for protection and adherence to the
original path. If, in the use of such alternatives, we
deviate from the path of Niṡcaya, we may find
ourselves in a cycle of setbacks. It is crucial to
remember this.
Even in daily practical life, both of these perspectives
are very useful for us. Let's understand this as well.
Suppose we have opened a clothing store. After
determining the location of the store and other
details, when we go out to purchase the merchandise,
where should we go? To the clothing market or to the
iron market? Certainly, after deciding to engage in the
business of clothing, if we are purchasing bags of nuts
and bolts, what will happen?
Even in the affairs of household management, many
situations arise. When a person wants to build a
house, he will start making savings from his income.
After saving a small amount by adopting a thrifty
lifestyle, one's focus might shift towards items like
radios, refrigerators, motors, or other unnecessary
indulgences. As a result, the house that was supposed
to be built remains unconstructed.
136

7 Naya

Before closing the topic of Niṡcaya and Vyavahāra
Naya, let's recall once again that Niṡcaya is the
destination or pure essence of a thing and Vyavahāra
is the means to attain it. By abandoning the means,
just as something can become unattainable, similarly,
there is no progress by abandoning destination.
Making decisions about these two things, Syādvāda
becomes our friend and helper; when we engage in
any practical or ultimate action to check whether
there is any mistake in it or not. Consequently, it is
realized that before honoring any transaction, by
examining it through this unique, unprecedented, and
extraordinary Syādvāda method, the possibility of
making a mistake in it does not remain.
Does the knowledge of Anekāntavāda, which is
enriched by this Syādvāda, now seem wonderful,
complete, unique, and acceptable to you?
Before fully completing our contemplation about
Naya, it is necessary to look at one thing. The
discussion here is a comprehensive and fundamental
introduction. For thorough and philosophical
contemplation, many examples and extensive
explanations are required.
Here, the seven Naya that have been mentioned are
the main ones. Besides these, there are many
divisions in Naya, and there are hundreds of
distinctions. There are many numbers of Naya and
their application as the number of words and their
meaning. One thing we should care about is that we
should stick to Sunaya and not fall upon Durnaya.

137

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

When considering any question, idea, object,
substance, or problem, it becomes essential to
remember that what is seen at first glance or known
immediately may not be the only perspective. There
are many facets to every aspect. By paying attention
to each distinct aspect, one can discover the true
essence of things. Viewing each issue from different
angles not only provides a comprehensive
understanding but also allows us to find something
new and beneficial. Remembering this, perspective is
crucial.
Sometimes, questions arise that many matters in life
need to be decided hastily. Considering this, we find
ourselves living in the era of 'fast times'. During that
time, it is advisable to engage in both broad and
subtle contemplations. In such situations, one should
not rush into decisions. What should be done in such
scenarios?
The answer to this is that if we adopt a new
perspective and think through the method of
Syādvāda, then we won't find it difficult to make
decisions quickly. In mathematics, once we learn that
18 x 5 = 90, then we don't need pencil and paper to
solve the same equation again. Using the same
analogy, adopting this vision of Naya and the
Syādvāda approach will make it easier for us to make
quick decisions without facing any difficulties. In
some situations, making decisions quickly without
hesitation can be more beneficial than letting the
situation linger and trying to solve it with a pen and
paper.

138

7 Naya

Even after making a decision, if we keep the
consideration of the overall perspective of that
decision, then, we will benefit from it. If a mistake is
discovered after planning, it is easier to go back to it
from the beginning, in the initial stages. If we do not
think about it, the mistake is not understood by us,
and later, when it is understood, it becomes difficult
to go back from there and avoid the consequences.
Therefore, when making decisions, before and after
making them, at both times, we should make it a point
to take advantage of the new perspective that we
have gained and apply it.
Taking time to make decisions allows us to
improve our understanding. It also helps us
develop important qualities such as empathy,
tolerance, resilience, patience, truthfulness,
generosity, and interpersonal skills. These
virtues come naturally to us when we practice
patience and avoid making hasty decisions.
Before concluding this chapter on Naya, there is a
request to make. This request, advice, suggestion,
whatever you may call it, is to never let our-self be
overshadowed by arrogance, and by belief of being
perfect or smart. Seeking advice, suggestions, or
assistance from others should not make us feel
deprived. We must always be ready to receive
guidance from a qualified teacher, parent, or friend.
Knowledge is not acquired without a preacher.
Now, we need to contemplate the concept of
Saptabhaṅgī. However, before that, it is extremely
crucial to first understand the word 'Apekṣā.'
139

CHAPTER 12

A point of view
Now, in the subsequent chapters, we are going to
contemplate on Saptabhaṅgi. This consists of a
collection of seven distinct statements from different
points of view. In the analysis of this, we will
consistently be mentioning the term, Apekṣā (A point
of view).
Regarding the four bases that we discussed earlier,
we have acquired some basic understanding of the
quadrilateral i.e., matter, space, time, and
characteristic. Anyhow, before going deeper into the
concept of Saptabhaṅgi, we commence by first
grasping the meaning of what Apekṣa is as that itself
is crucial for us before we go ahead.
The word 'apekṣā', in the present context, means
reference or basis. It is framed as, 'with reference to a
certain context,' or 'a certain point of view.'
Sometimes, it also means, 'in certain respect,' which
is used when talking about something specific. It is
also synonymous with various other words like,
'relativity,' or 'in relation to.' When we say, 'in relation
to,' it is said to have a connection with another thing.
There are 4 types of apekṣā in relation to matter,
space, time and characteristics. When mentioning
these four in the context of an object, for example, an
ornament, it refers to the relationship with matter,

140

A point of view

i.e., gold, and the same is true for its relation with
time, space, form, shape, etc.
The term 'Apekṣā' is a Sanskrit word. The Sanskrit
language has a vast vocabulary, and its linguistic
power is immense. Even in fewer words, more and
more can be expressed. If there is any language that
has the power to express precisely, it is the Sanskrit
language. It has many such small words that one must
delve deeply into to understand or explain
thoroughly.
When we say with respect to matter, we take into
consideration the substance of it. For example, when
we talk about a chair with respect to matter, in
practical terms, it will come to our mind as wood. This
wood could be mango wood, jungle wood, teak, or the
like.
When discussing any ornament made of gold, with
respect to matter, irrespective of its shape, we will be
discussing the essence of 'gold.' Similarly, when
discussing space, time, and characteristics, it will lead
us to a clear understanding of the object's connection
with respect to time, space, and attributes. In
contrast, discussion of non-self matter, space, time
and characteristics will also come into play.
Previously, we mentioned Utpatti, Sthiti, and Laya
(origin, state, and destruction). The Jain philosophers
have used the words Utpāda, Drauvya, Vyaya
(creation, stationariness, and dissolution) to refer to
the same concepts, and we have also discussed them.
In the context of using the terms as mentioned above,
the word 'apekṣā' holds special significance. In origin,
state and destruction, there is no pre-existing relation
141

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

to any particular attribute. Hence, these are indicative
of absolute terms. These words indicate
the solitariness. It's a big misunderstanding. In
creation, dissolution, and stationariness, the explicit
mention of relativity is a clear indication of the
Anekāntavāda. The substance itself transforms, and
in every transformation, like water remains in a
steam, the fundamental aspect of the original
substance remains unchanged. Therefore, when
discussing the substance in the context of
transformation, it can be water or air depending on
the form it takes.
The meaning attributed to the word 'origin' is such
that, when considered, it is not something that existed
before. Now, the assertion that 'it did not exist before'
is incorrect. The same is the case for Laya. It's a belief
that everything completely ends during cosmic
destruction. If that is the case, then how would Utpātti
be possible? Even though we come across many
stories of cosmic destruction, pure logical reasoning
disproves this idea.
When we consider these three states relatively,
attributing the concept of 'a point of view' to them, we
make ourselves enlightened through it. In which
there is no relation with others or with different
entities, such a thing does not exist in this world. One
substance has a connection with various states, in the
same way that one substance has a connection with
another substance. All these relationships are subject
to specific and separate points of view. This relativity
is a fundamental and unchallengeable principle of
this world.

142

A point of view

Instead of using the term 'origin,' Jain philosophers
have used the term 'creation.' This term is also
contextually relevant. The meaning of creation is to
come into existence, yet there is a difference between
origin and creation. In origin, the existence of another
thing beforehand is not considered; whereas, in
creation, the existence of another thing beforehand is
clearly considered.
Similarly, in the word 'Laya' (destruction), the sense
is that nothing remains after it. Whereas, the term
'Vyaya' (dissolution) explicitly suggests the clear
concept of an original substance persisting even after
the destruction of one state and the attainment of
another state, demonstrating a distinct meaning and
significance.
When the human body undergoes the process of
cremation or destruction through fire, the conscious
self in the form of the soul, which existed in the living
body, and the remains of the body, both exist in
different forms. This is why, in the term 'Vyaya', there
is no complete annihilation, but the underlying
essence of matter maintains its continuity. Even after
this, the principles of relativity remain valid.
In the first triad, the term 'Sthiti' (state) is used. The
meaning attributed to this term in the context, and
the meaning of the Dhrauvya (stationariness) used by
Jain philosophers in the triad where it is employed,
are significantly different. The operational meaning
of the 'state' term is 'to remain in a state where one is
situated.'
Now, we come to know that each object's state
constantly keeps changing. The complex interplay of
143

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

variability unfolds itself, bringing forth something
new while seemingly remaining imperceptible.
Moreover, in this continuous process, any one form
that seems to consistently persist over an extended
period is also subject to variability at each moment.
This clearly means that the state does not persist, it
keeps on getting consumed or dissolute. The
sequence of dissolution and the emergence of new
and novel forms continues through transformations.
Therefore, Jain philosophers have given the word
'Dhrauvya' instead of 'Sthiti' because it signifies that
in every change, there is always a relativity of a
permanent aspect that lies.
We perceive three distinct states in the interplay of
utpāda, vyaya, and drauvya, but these states are not
separate; they are interdependent. It is the three
different states of the same entity. These three states
are intricately related to each other.
In the transformation of every entity, its substance
element remains constant, the previous state
undergoes dissolution, and the subsequent state
comes into existence. The substance element of an
entity, also known as Dhrauvya, remains constant,
while the attributes undergo creation and
dissolution. In other words, the fundamental
substance endures, and the previous state ceases
while the subsequent state arises. Thus, in every
entity, these three aspects of attributes, Utpāda,
Vyaya, and Drauvya persist throughout endless time.
The substance element of an entity, known as
Drauvya, is eternal and an element related to creation
and dissolution, making it impermanent. In this
manner, Jain philosophy articulates that an entity is
144

A point of view

exclusively characterized by the eternal and
impermanent aspects. There is nothing that can be
described as absolute eternal, or absolute
impermanent.
Someone might ask, "The cycle of Utpāda and Vyaya
are related to forms, whereas Dhrauvya is related to
matter; then how can one describe the three
attributes of Utpāda, Vyaya and Dhrauvya as
belonging to a single entity?"
Its answer is also clear. Paryāya (form) is not separate
from the object. The substance is also not separate
from the object. The object is a substance as well as a
form. Therefore, these three attributes are of one and
the same object.
The substance alone has various separate aspects,
and each aspect is not independent. In every aspect,
there is a relationship with each other through some
kind of dependence. When explained by
philosophers, applying the vision of Naya and the
table of Saptabhaṅgi, very boldly it is said by others
that, "this Syādvāda is incomplete as well as
uncertain." But this is not correct.
Jain philosophers have not described any concept
incompletely or imperfectly. In any such discussion,
at any time, there is no uncertainty. Conversely,
certainty remains clearly evident.
The words 'certainly' and 'also' are used freely in our
language. Both of these words carry a definite
meaning. In Saptabhaṅgi with 'Syādasti', 'Eva' word is
used, which denotes certainty.
The combination Syāt asti eva in a sentence translates
to, "A thing certainly exists. Apart from that, there is
145

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

also something on the other side." This fact is
mentioned by the word 'Syāt' in a definite and certain
way. These words 'certainly' and 'also' do not indicate
any uncertainty, possibility, or doubt. These words
demonstrate certainty in one and another type. If this
point is clearly understood, there will be no confusion
in understanding Saptabhaṅgi.
In Saptabhaṅgi, when the concept of a point of view
arises, even that is a definite state but not used in
incomplete or uncertain meanings. In statements like
'The hat exists and the hat does not exist, with
different points of view and different discussions,
even though they may seem contradictory, all these
discussions are definite. When a child grows up and
the hat that used to be a toy is no longer useful as a
hat, it is indeed true that it is not a hat anymore.
Similarly, the words 'Sva' (self) and 'Para' (non-self)
are not indicators of uncertainty; they are indicators
of certainty. For example, when we look for a knife in
the house for cutting vegetables, whether it is present
or not, like when we say, "there is a knife," or "there
is no knife," both responses contradict each other, but
both are definite answers. When the knife is present,
it is a definite response, and when the knife is not
there, that is also a definite response.
Now, when the response "There is no knife" is
received, at that time, there was a different type of
knife. This knife was blunt and used by children to
play and enjoy as a toy. This means that the
distinctive, required knife was not present.
Therefore, when it is said, "there is no knife" it refers
in relation to different matter, space, time, or
characteristic. When there are many things in the
146

A point of view

house other than a knife, the knife in relation to 'SvaDravya' (self-matter) is not there. In other houses,
even though it might be there, in our houses we do
not have. This is in relation to 'Sva-Kṣetra' (selfspace). Thus, when we say "it is there" or "it is not
there," it doesn't become an absolute statement, but
rather becomes relative.
The phrase, 'point of view' can lead to the magical
growth of our understanding and intellect, and if
ignored, may lead to moving backward instead of
progressing forward in the journey of life.
In the doctrine of Anekāntavāda in Jain philosophy,
the concept of 'point of view' holds active and
significant importance in the exploration of various
dimensions of life. If we choose to abandon this
interplay of point of view and relativity in the diverse
fields of life, we might end up wandering in darkness.
This point of view-centric or Syādvāda approach is
not just a discussion, practice, or intellectual exercise;
rather, it is a realistic perception of the true nature of
multi-characterized things. It presents the complete
and genuine aspects of an object. Through this
approach, the entire essence of an object can be
comprehended. It does not add something new to an
object, but like a guide, it exposes an object. It states
that Ram is the father and the son as well, making it
evident through the lens of Lav-Kush and Dasharath.
The meaning of the word 'Sāpekṣa' is relativity. Once
we understand the meaning and equivalence of the
term 'Sāpekṣa', Saptabhaṅgi becomes easier to
understand.
Now, let's proceed to discuss Saptabhaṅgi.
147

CHAPTER 13

Saptabhaṅgi
In this Saptabhaṅgi, we are now set to understand the
seven types of sections, like examining a golden pot
through a specific test. To examine gold, there is a
particular test, and for the goldsmith, there is a
testing stone that serves as a means to check it. To
recognize, examine, and understand an object, Jain
scholars have developed a decision-making machine
called Saptabhaṅgi. They have created an approved
decision-making mechanism for this purpose.
To know anything, humans must first pose a question.
The seed of a question lies in doubt. Doubt can be of
seven types, meaning seven types of questions and
seven types of answers to obtain clarification. Doubt
has arisen, and we have identified seven types of
doubt and their resolutions.
Jain philosophers use seven questions to explain the
types of queries and answers that can be derived
from questioning. The eighth question has never been
posed to anyone yet. Try finding the answer. The
eighth question, as a seeker, has never emerged. Jain
philosophers have explained seven types of doubts
related to this. It is not like we accept these 7 types
only because it's been said by Jain philosophers. In
reality, no eighth type can be revealed. That means,
148

Saptabhaṅgi

accepting these seven is acknowledging the truth, and
by accepting this fact, we should move forward.
For any work, business firms appoint an assistant. An
aspirant goes through different tests before he
succeeds and receives an appointment letter. For the
work, knowledge and experience about it are the first
assessment. Then, whether this person is diligent or
not, whether he is authentic or not, whether he is
trustworthy or not, whether he is healthy or not,
whether he comes from a good family or not, whether
he has good references or not, whether his speech
and writing style are appropriate or not, and whether
he can have an impact on others or not—all these
aspects are examined thoroughly before making a
complete assessment. Even after that, it is checked
whether the person is contentious by nature and
thoughtful about being calm, sweet, and peace-loving
or not. After conducting such assessments, the person
is given a permanent position. Before that, a practical
period of three to six months is assigned, involving
the Probation period. Whether he or she is the uncle
or aunt's son or daughter, their matter is considered
separately.
After performing these evaluations, the person,
whether contentious or peaceful, is also analyzed for
his thoughts on such matters. These considerations
are based on a timeless practical principle. Jain
philosophers have prepared this Saptabhaṅgi in such
a way that it serves as a group of formulas.
After raising this issue about the assessment, the
decision-making mechanism is prepared, and there is
no room for doubt or hesitation about how
149

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

trustworthy and complete it will be. Once the decision
is made through this method, it does not burden the
mind of the person. Without worrying or thinking
about the concerns of any caste, one can go on with
ease.
The uniqueness of this formula lies in the fact that it
is completely intelligible and rational. Nothing is too
deep, profound, or unattainable in it.
Let's now examine the seven-fold decision-making
mechanism. According to the principles of Jainism,
the object 'pot' is used in many places. We should also
start from there. Here, first, the full significance of the
words 'Syāt' and 'Eva' is considered, and we proceed
with both these words.
Test 1: स्यात् अस्स्त एव घटः। Syāt Asti Eva ghatah.
This means, "a pot does exist somehow."
Here, we have used the word 'Syāt.' It's meaning we
understand from the previous pages. The sentence
"The pot does exist with respect to a certain
viewpoint." It also hints at some hidden suggestions
of other viewpoints.
Earlier, we discussed these four bases, and we have
added the word 'self' to them, self-matter, self-space,
self-time, and self-characteristics. With this
clarification, we face no difficulty in saying "A pot
does exist." A definite statement has been made. Now,
when we apply the four bases to this pot, let us
understand how it applies.
With respect to matter, the pot is made of clay. Clay is
its self-matter.
150

Saptabhaṅgi

With respect to space, the pot is placed in an outer
section. This becomes its self-space.
With respect to time, the pot exists during the January
month. This becomes its self-time.
With respect to characteristics, the pot is black. This
becomes its self-characteristic.
Along with this pot, we also have a flower vase. This
flower vase is made of brass metal. When we arrange
the flowers in it and place it on the table, it becomes a
flower vase. When we remove the flowers and fill
them with beans, it becomes an ordinary utensil.
Now, let's examine it with the four bases.
The self-matter of the flower vase is brass metal.
The self-space of the flower vase is the drawing room.
The self-time of the flower vase is the time until the
flowers remain in it.
The self-characteristic of the flower vase is to
enhance the beauty of the drawing room.
As it completes all viewpoints mentioned above, we
call it a flower vase. By applying the four bases to the
pot and this flower vase, we have concluded with
bases that it does exist.
A specific viewpoint related to the pot and the flower
vase has been indicated by the word 'Syāt.' And
certainty has come by the word 'Eva.' In this manner,
we are on the right path.
It means that through this first case, we have decided
that "a pot does exist."
Test 2: स्यात् नास्स्त एव घटः। Syāt nāsti eva ghatah.
151

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

This means "somehow a pot doesn't exist."
After concluding that, when a pot does exist based on
self-matter,
self-space,
self-time
and
selfcharacteristic, we began to think, "Can any other
decision be made in this case that indicates the nonexistence of the pot?" As we explored another type of
question, through this alternate perspective, we came
to know that the pot that exists is with respect to selfmatter, self-space, self-time and self-characteristic.
However, the same pot does not exist with respect to
non-self matter, non-self space, non-self time and
non-self characteristic. In other words, the pot as
described above is real, but it is not made of copper,
it is not in any other room, it is not in March, and it is
not red.
Even in the case of that flower vase, we will reach the
same conclusion that the flower vase does not exist.
The one which does exist with respect to its own
attributes ceases to exist with respect to non-self
matter, non-self space, non-self time and non-self
characteristic. There shouldn't be any confusion in
accepting this. If we pluck the flowers from it and fill
it with beans, then it ceases to be a flower vase.
Therefore, now we have to decide by considering all
four bases that "the flower vase does not exist."
Upon considering all this, we will clearly understand
that what was initially deemed as a pot with respect
to its own attributes does not remain so when viewed
solely with respect to non-self attributes. The
existence of the flower vase was also dependent on
the viewpoint of one's own and when considered

152

Saptabhaṅgi

from the viewpoint of others, it turned into nonexistence. The second criterion tells us that:
It is not a pot with respect to the copper metal (nonself matter).
It is not a pot with respect to the inner room (non-self
space).
It is not a pot with respect to the month of March
(non-self time).
It is not a pot with respect to the red colour (non-self
characteristic).
Similarly, even when the flower vase is not made of
brass, when it is not placed in the drawing room, it
does not hold flowers, and it does not enhance the
beauty of the window, at that time, it does not exist.
Hence, through this second criterion, we understand
that neither the pot nor the flower vase exists. This
analysis has made this a definite fact. The Syāt
suggests the relative nature of this decision, and the
Eva indicates certainty.
After satisfying these two types of inquiries, a third
question arises: "Is the pot both existing and nonexisting?" To answer this, a third criterion is
prepared. Holding it in hand, before applying a
mathematical mechanism, we will call matter, space,
time and characteristic as the 4 bases henceforth. We
will add the words 'self’ and 'non-self' as required, as
4 self bases and 4 non-self bases. Now, the answer to
the third question is as follows:
Test 3: स्यात् अस्स्त नास्स्त च एव घटः। Syāt asti nāsti ca eva
ghatah.
153

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

This means "the pot exists as well as it doesn't exist
with some respect."
Our intellect's true test now lies here. Through the
first two criteria, we have understood that the pot
exists and doesn't exist simultaneously. Therefore,
we have comprehended that it is indeed as it is, where
it is, and isn't where it isn't. However, the third
criterion, "is and isn't," presents a complex
proposition. Is it so, or is it not? If it is, then it is; if it
isn't, then it isn't. Therefore, why do we need to
entertain the doubtful proposition of "is and isn't"
here?
Firstly, we should understand that no uncertainty or
doubt lies here, which seems like a knowledge puzzle.
The propositions presented here seem an addition of
the first two criteria, but that is not the case. This is a
third attribute, which is certain.
Recall the statement we previously discussed: "Many
attributes, even though contradictory, reside in the
same entity." This statement was made earlier.
This third criterion speaks of a phenomenon that is
not articulated in the first and second criteria. When
a thing has different aspects or forms, it is not a
conglomerate or a confluence of diverse aspects like
in the case of a pot. It is an independent attribute. This
is something we know through experience. Even if it
appears to be a combination, its nature remains
distinct.
When the third colour is formed by mixing two
colours, we recognize it not by the name mixture of
two colours but by a third name. This is evident from
154

Saptabhaṅgi

everyone's experience. Similarly, when we
differentiate between two states, one based on self 4
bases and the other based on non-self 4 bases, and
make the third decision by combining the two states
that "is and isn't", then the addition disappears, and a
third definite statement is formed. To understand this
concept equally, we take the example of alcohol.
We will encounter different opinions about alcohol.
