If we ask a moderate drinker, they will say, "Alcohol
is beneficial."
If we ask a prohibitionist, they will say, "Alcohol is not
beneficial."
If we ask a doctor, they will say, "Alcohol is both
beneficial and harmful." If we ask for clarification,
they will disclose that alcohol is useful as medicine
but harmful as a habit of drinking.
These three opinions are based on different
perspectives, and all three are independent opinions.
Do you understand this point?
Let us talk about diamonds. One diamond is Bluish
White and the other is Redish White. Despite this,
when both contain the colours Blue and White, and
Red and White, we use a third distinct word. This
gives us an understanding or knowledge about the
third thing.
Similarly, when we use both 'is' and 'is not,' both
together convey the third distinct thing to us.
Here, there is no ambiguity or uncertainty anymore;
it has now been clearly understood.

155

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

This third method is aimed at explaining the inherent
nature of an object through its third aspect. This
statement is relative. Now, having understood the
concept of "is and is not" of the pot and the flower
vase, before moving forward, one must remember so
well that in this third definite statement, due to the
presence of the word 'Syāt', the boundaries and
viewpoint of self 4 bases and non-self 4 bases remain
at their places. This word indicates the different
viewpoints even in this third method.
Indeed, in this third perspective, there is no doubt or
uncertainty. Hence, instead, we are given the third
sight to understand the object.
After these three decisions, a new curiosity arises and
it leads us towards the fourth aspect.
Test 4: स्यात् अवक्तव्यः एव घटः। Syāt avaktavya eva ghatah.
This means "the pot is somehow indescribable."
Avaktavya means that which cannot be described by
speech or words.
Now we need to use our understanding power
intensely here. Initially, we said "it is," then we said
"it isn't." In the third, we understood that "it is and
isn't." These three statements have entered our
minds, but what is 'indescribable?' Why can't the
description of this pot be made?
Understanding this matter is not difficult at all. We
often encounter such word usage in our daily lives.
For example, someone who has been heavily
burdened with some kind of gratitude may express
themselves verbally or in writing. They might say, "I
156

Saptabhaṅgi

don't have the words to fully describe my feelings."
Similarly, as the severity of any kind of feelings
increases, we often hear people saying, "I am unable
to describe what is in my heart."
Now, individuals using such expressions may
understand their sentiments, but not everyone can
comprehend them. However, they resort to uttering
such sentences to express their feelings, as they
cannot find precise words to articulate their
emotions.
In the first three scenarios, the statements regarding
the existence of a pot are sequentially as follows: In
the first, we accept the existence of the pot. In the
second, we accept its non-existence. In the third, we
accept the statement of existence and non-existence
of the pot. In all three scenarios, there is a sequential
statement about the nature of the object, but if
someone says that, "Tell me simultaneous statements
about the nature of the pot," then it must be said that,
"This is indescribable."
When we go to inquire about the health of a sick
person, typically the response given is, "I'm okay, I'm
fine. But these are the problems." Here, okay and fine
are relative terms. They are saying they're fine when
compared to yesterday, but when they say they aren't
fine, it is in context to their previous health
conditions. When we ask them to tell us exactly about
their health, then the answer he gives is, "I am not in
a position to say anything."
In the battlefield, in scientific laboratories, and
in courts of justice, such situations are often seen and
experienced, where questioning the consequences of
157

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

ongoing behaviours, statements like "It cannot be
said exactly what will happen", and "can't say
anything" are often encountered. Such responses are
not vague answers. It is a true and clear assessment
of the situation.
Similarly, the pot is also an inexpressible one. Even in
the phrase 'yes and no,' by removing the word 'and,'
we cannot describe the situation.
Here also comes the word 'Syāt.' The word 'Eva'
ensures the certainty of the statement, keeping intact
the original concept of certainty, even when
mentioning other possibilities besides this
interpretation.
The difficulty in describing an object with existence,
non-existence, existence as well as non-existence
simultaneously is removed by introducing a fourth
perspective called 'Avaktavya.' In this way, by
refraining from making false statements, we avoid
pitfalls and attain acceptance of a true statement as a
means to move forward. Thus, this fourth perspective
presents us with a new standpoint, offering a fresh
perspective for understanding the object.
In the Vedantic philosophy, 'Neti Neti' (not this, not
this) is a method that fully embodies the fourth
perspective to understand the essence. Due to the
inability to describe, the words 'Neti Neti' (not this,
not this) spontaneously manifest. These words, when
uttered, signify one's attempt to comprehend
Brahma. But Brahma is not any specific thing.
Similarly, in this fourth perspective, the term
'avaktavya' indicates a certain aspect of relativity,
serving as a clear means of resolution. Thus, by
158

Saptabhaṅgi

understanding this fourth perspective, we have
decided that pot and flower vases are inexpressible.
In both philosophy and practice, this perspective
carries a lot of importance. Now, let's move forward
to the fifth section.
Test 5: स्यात् अस्स्त अवक्तव्यः च एव घटः। Syāt asti avaktavya
ca eva ghatah.
This means that "somehow the pot does exist and
somehow, the pot is not expressible."
In this fifth perspective, we encounter a new and
distinct view. In the fourth perspective, we got stuck
by using the term 'avaktavya' with relative vision. By
saying this, we did not deny its existence. If we do so,
then the 'syāt' word becomes meaningless.
During the fourth perspective, we already had three
perspectives viz. existence, non-existence, and both
based on the object's other independent
characteristic, we formulated avaktavya as the fourth
independent provision.
The fourth provision is clear, understandable, and
applicable in practical life, which we have accepted.
Now, based on the fifth perspective, we formulate: "It
does exist and is inexpressible."
The fifth provision accepts the existence of the object
based on its self 4 bases and with it, adds the
statement that the description cannot be made based
on 4 self bases as well as 4 non-self bases.
Since the first four sections for understanding all the
attributes of the object are not complete, the

159

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

necessity of the fifth and subsequently the sixth and
seventh parts arises.
After understanding the first four provisions, there is
no difficulty in understanding the fifth provision.
"The object exists and is inexpressible" is a definite
proposition according to this criterion. To
understand this, consider the following analogy.
In digging a well, the place chosen is where water is
more likely to emerge. There is water under the
ground, which is a definite fact. However, it cannot be
said that water will emerge from the chosen place for
digging the well. In this scenario, if someone asks, "Is
there water here?" then the only clear answer that
can be given is, "Yes, but nothing definite can be said."
This fifth statement is relative and does not deny
other definite statements based on other points of
view. Rather, it presents a specific and new
proposition apart from the first four.
Once again, in anticipation of self 4 bases, "it does
exist" is also a definite statement, keeping in mind
while keeping self 4 bases and non-self 4 bases in
mind, it cannot be described simultaneously. In this
way, for both the pot and the flower vase, through this
fifth criterion, we express a clear provision: exist and
are indescribable.
Test 6: स्यात् नास्स्त अवक्तव्यः च एव घटः। Syāt nāsti avaktavya
ca eva ghatah.
This means "the pot does not exist and is
indescribable."

160

Saptabhaṅgi

By combining the first criterion related to existence
and fourth criterion related to inexpressiveness, we
have formulated the fifth criterion. In the sixth
method, by combining the non-existence of the
second method and the indescribable of the fourth
method, we have established these two approaches
together.
According to non-self 4 bases, the pot does not exist.
But based on self 4 bases and non-self 4 bases, and the
word Syāt, the pot is inexpressible. By combining
these two attributes, we get the independent sixth
criterion i.e., the pot does not exist and is
inexpressible.
To illustrate the concept once again, we can consider
the digging of a well. Some terrains are identified as
'no-water-bearing places.' When digging deep into
such terrain, no water emerges. Therefore, in the
context of digging a well, it is definitively established
based on the nature of the land that there is no water
beneath this land.
Here, where there is land, there is water; since this
fact is well established, but despite digging, water has
not been obtained, these two facts lead us to conclude
that, according to scientific validity, there is indeed
water beneath the land. If water has not been found
here, then why hasn't water been found? Through
further efforts, such as digging at another location or
drilling a borehole, we are not in a position to say that
the water will not be found with certainty.
Thus, for no-water-bearing land, we can give an exact
picture of the situation by mentioning that water does
not exist and is inexpressible. This is crystal clear.
161

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Similar to, the 'existence and inexpressible'
proposition of the fifth section, here the 'nonexistence and inexpressible' proposition comes in
which is a new point of view. This is an indispensable
part of the chain of Saptabhaṅgi. Let us accept that the
pot and flower vase do not exist and are
inexpressible. Now, let us get answers to the seventh
enquiry.
Test 7: स्यात् अस्स्त नास्स्त अवक्तव्यः च एव घटः। Syāt asti nāsti
avaktavya ca eva ghatah.
This means "somehow the pot exists, yet it doesn't,
and it's inexplicable."
A new picture was created by combining the first and
fourth segments in fifth one and the second and
fourth segments in the sixth one. Now, in the seventh
segment, by combining the first, second, and fourth,
we have created this latest picture.
Similar to sections fifth and sixth, in the seventh
section, an independent situation is shown. This is a
decisive and definite statement. We get a clear picture
after adding 3 different things. Once we understand
the fifth and sixth segments, it's not difficult to
comprehend this. The seventh stage of the test
presents a new and unique picture by amalgamating
these three components. As the six segments depicted
decisions that were different from each other, so even
this seventh picture is distinct from them. From this,
a new understanding emerges. This understanding is
separate and distinct from the previous six.
In this sentence, the indicative of certainty is the word
'Eva.' Furthermore, the word 'Syāt' also indicates
162

Saptabhaṅgi

agreement with the other viewpoints that denote
other definite forms present in this object. By
combining these, the seventh test presents us with a
clear and definite picture.
With respect to different viewpoints, existence, nonexistence, and inexpressiveness depict a beautiful
and marvellous picture.
Recall the analogy of the well and the water. In one
well, there is water; in the other, there isn't. The
digging was the same, but where water was found on
one surface, it didn't emerge in the other well.
Now, in this land, where two wells have been dug, on
some plots there is no water, on some there is water.
Whether water will emerge from digging other wells
in this land, we won't be able to say definitively. If we
want to explain this with certainty, then we still have
one option left, and that is, "The water exists and it
doesn't, and nothing can be said." In response to
questions like "Is there water in this land?" if one
were to engage in such a discussion, then this answer
alone would become equivalent to reality.
In the seventh statement, certainty is understood
through the word 'Eva' and 'Syāt' helps us understand
various other viewpoints. This is also a relative
statement and is thus a truthful statement.
In practical life, we encounter situations of this
nature. Let's consider an example.
A gentleman named Devjibhai started a business with
the capital of fifty thousand provided by another
nobleman named Nagjibhai. As the business

163

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

progresses, this capital will either increase or
decrease; no one can predict the outcome.
Whether his business will prosper or decline,
whether he will attain success or face destruction,
these things cannot be predicted today. In these
circumstances, if someone asks whether Devjibhai's
capital does exist, then in reply we have to say
"Devjibhai's capital exists, does not exist and is
inexpressible." A possibility which is included in the
seventh proposition is that Nagjibhai may demand his
capital back, which adds another layer of uncertainty
to the situation.
Now, let's talk about Nagjibhai. If someone asks the
same question we asked for Devjibhai, what would be
the answer? Since the capital belongs to him, it means
his capital does exist. Since that amount is not with
him right now, the capital does not exist from his
perspective. Whether Nagjibhai will receive the
capital back from Devjibhai or not depends on
various factors such as the nature of Devjibhai's
business, its development, success, or failure, and
sportsmanship of Devjibhai. Therefore, based on this
viewpoint, it is not possible to answer only yes or no.
So, if we ask questions regarding Nagjibhai's capital,
the only response would be "it exists, it doesn't exist
and is inexpressible," which would provide the only
clear picture of the situation.
In this way, by considering various viewpoints
related to self four bases and non-self four bases
separately and simultaneously by placing them under
the umbrella of the seventh principle, a clear and
independent statement can be made that the pot and
164

Saptabhaṅgi

the flower vase
inexpressible.

Even in this seventh principle, both the words 'Syāt'
and 'Eva' exist, making this method definitive and
relative. This is the most beautiful answer to our
latest and seventh question.
Through the sevenfold analysis, we have made
separate decisions regarding the attributes of an
object. However, even though each proposition
provides clarity, certainty, and independence, it does
not present a complete picture of the object in each
method. By allowing each of these to remain
independent and by understanding that we perceive
an object's complete image, then that understanding
would become solitary and misleading. In our
exploration of the object's multifaceted nature, we
have examined the picture of the object as seen
through the various lenses of the seven criteria. When
we combine them all, we will see the complete picture
of an object. Otherwise, by judgment, we would find
ourselves flawed.
In these sevenfold decisions, though each decision
was able to maintain its independence, all decisions
are related to each other and are interconnected with
respect to their self four bases and non-self four
bases. The significance of this aspect should not be
forgotten, but constantly remembered; hence, the
word 'Syāt' has been included in each criterion of the
formula.
When we consider all seven forms together, a
complete picture will emerge. However, even after
creating a complete picture in this way, we cannot bid
165

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

farewell to the word 'Syāt'. By removing the word
'Syāt' from this, the resulting complete picture is
incomplete and lacks an aspect. In this way, we fall
into the error of one-sided decisions. Because without
the 'Syāt', a thought may arise that something exists
at one time and does not exist at another. With the
help of the Syāt, the same thing can exist as well as not
exist.
Looking at it from this perspective, the presence of
the word 'Syāt' is indispensable in all contexts, it is of
great importance and universal relevance. Hence, the
term 'Syādvāda' has emerged in the realm of
philosophy. Without the word 'Syāt' in it, nothing
remains. This fact becomes clear and definite through
Saptabhaṅgi.
According to this, statements like "exists," "doesn't
exist," as well as "exists as well doesn't exist," initially
seem contradictory but now can be understood as
consistent with various analogies and examples.
Linking these seven curiosities with the attainment of
infinite happiness, when we contemplate, a vivid
picture of the path to the ultimate state will unfold
before us. In which way we should live, we can
become enjoyers of infinite happiness; this
understanding will dawn upon us.
So now, we find ourselves engaged in discussing an
important and substantive issue concerning living a
good and true life. Some people ask questions such as,
"What benefit do we derive from the intellectual
exercise of Saptabhaṅgi, where we perceive seven
different aspects of a single object? Has it yielded any
benefit? Let's assume that in the realm of
166

Saptabhaṅgi

philosophical understanding, there is the doctrine of
Anekāntavāda, the unique interpretation of Naya, and
the beautiful grammar of Saptabhaṅgi. What then is
the benefit?"
The benefit is invaluable indeed. To describe it is like
filling page after page with nectar. The question asked
here is not spiritual but related to worldly affairs.
Whether the Saptabhaṅgi is useful in attaining
worldly happiness, and understanding in daily life or
not? A logical answer is demanded by asking such
types of questions. The aim of living a happy life
pervades the universe which we all understand.
Understanding how this daily-life applicable
knowledge of Saptabhaṅgi can be utilized is essential.
After understanding the analogy given in the next
chapter, a thought will arise on how the entire
Syādvāda is immensely beneficial for us.
For this purpose, let's introduce a fictional character
named Barrister Chakravarti to present a new
scenario before us. Let's get acquainted with it.

167

CHAPTER 14

Barrister Chakravarti
In the earlier chapters, we observe that Saptabhaṅgi
is a chain of wonderful formulas. It is a proven and
approved method. There is no doubt, ambiguity, or
uncertainty in it.
Through this approach, we examined a solitary object
from seven distinct perspectives across various
scenarios. In doing so, we described its
wonderfulness using a pot and a flower vase.
Engaging in contemplation from seven different
angles has assisted us in deciding our behaviour or
conduct in daily life. Consequently, we achieve
enhanced clarity and guidance, drawing from the
examples provided.
For this reason, we created a character named
Barrister Chakravarti. This person and name are
purely fictitious, based on an imaginary character,
and have no relation to any living person, past,
present, or future. Having made this point clear, we
move forward.
Barrister exemplifies a distinct quality: generosity.
Generosity, regarded as a virtue of the soul, embodies
a facet of kindness. If we could consider the soul as
substance, then generosity is considered to be its
characteristic, an element of 4 bases. It is not a
substance; it is a component of the soul's inherent
nature.
168

Barrister Chakravarti

Furthermore, as an example, we consider the
generosity of Barrister Chakravarti as a thing. We say
this to understand the practical utility of the
sevenfold analysis. Therefore, we formulate the initial
sentence as "Barrister Chakravarti is generous."
Now, for the generosity of Barrister Chakravarti, we
consider the 4 bases around matter, space, time, and
characteristics.
Matter: The matter of generosity of Barrister
Chakravarti is the wealth he possesses. This quality
becomes active when he has surplus money.
Space: The space or area of Barrister Chakravarti's
generosity is his community. However, even in this
area, it is only among the poor class where his
generosity becomes evident, otherwise not.
Time: Barrister Chakravarti spends his time in daily
routine to meet his family's needs and prepare for
court-related work. During the day, they are often
occupied with court cases. In the evenings,
occasionally, they spend some time at the club.
Meanwhile, sometimes they indulge in a bridge game
at the club. On Sundays and holidays, they stay at
home. Therefore, their time for generosity is when
they are not busy with work, not at the club, and not
indulging in intoxication. During leisure time, they
make their generosity active. Therefore, for their
generosity, the related time is their 'leisure time.'
Characteristics: For Barrister Chakravarti, the
characteristic associated with his generosity is his
love for education. Apart from charity, they do not
spend money on any other activities. This means that
169

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

even if someone is starving, they do not donate a
single penny. In all other matters except education,
they are not generous. They are willing to spend
money on education-related matters.
So, for his generosity, money and wealth refer to
matter. Space signifies the poor class from his
community. Time is when he is not indulging in
leisure or intoxication. And his love for education is
the characteristic. Likewise, when he does not have
extra money, it is considered non-self matter for him,
and apart from those from the poor class are
considered non-self space. When he is occupied in
work or indulging in intoxication, it is non-self time,
and apart from education and charity, all other
subjects are non-self characteristics.
According to the above-mentioned 4 self bases,
Barrister Chakravarti is generous. And according to
non-self 4 bases, he is not generous. Now, according
to the seven criteria, let us examine this generosity:
First Criterion: Barrister Chakravarti is generous.
Second Criterion: Barrister Chakravarti is not
generous.
Third Criterion: Barrister Chakravarti is generous
and not generous.
Fourth Criterion: Barrister Chakravarti's generosity
is inexpressible.
Fifth Criterion: Barrister Chakravarti is generous and
inexpressible.
Sixth Criterion: Barrister Chakravarti is not generous
and inexpressible.
170

Barrister Chakravarti

Seventh Criterion: Barrister Chakravarti is generous,
not generous, and inexpressible.
Now, there are two words, Syāt and Eva present in all
the 7 criteria, and based on that, we proceed. These
seven variations express the different perspectives.
Now, in practical life, we examine how Barrister
Chakravarti's generosity functions based on the
seven different viewpoints mentioned above. To
investigate this, we introduce two gentlemen named
Chaturbhuj and Gangadhar to this fictional setup.
Both these gentlemen are aspiring to benefit from
Barrister Sir's generosity. When they come together,
they ask, "Will we benefit from Barrister's
generosity?"
Out of the two brothers, Chaturbhuj is a member of
Barrister sir's community. Considering the
requirement of self-space, he becomes an eligible
person. So, we tell him that "You will benefit from
the Barrister's generosity." Here, according to the
first perspective, it is clear that "Barrister is
generous."
Initially, Gangadhar is not a member of Barrister's
community. As he does not fulfil the requirement of
self-space, he falls into the category of non-self space.
And as it falls under this, we tell Gangadhar that
"Barrister is not generous."
According to the first and second propositions,
Chaturbhuj becomes hopeful as he sits with us, and
Gangadhar leaves from there. He came to know the
criterion of this community and understood that
Barrister's generosity is not for him.
171

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

After Gangadhar has left and Chaturbhuj alone
remains, he is hoping for his own benefit. He asks
again: "If I am from this community, will I benefit
from Barrister Sir's genuine generosity?"
We are to give a clear answer in one sentence. We will
say, "He is generous and not generous." We know that
with respect to his self-space, Barrister is generous.
But we don't know whether he fulfills the other
condition or not.
When Chaturbhuj seeks clarification upon hearing
the contradictory responses, then we tell him that
Barrister Chakravarti's generosity does not manifest
consistently, universally, or in all circumstances.
Therefore, he is generous if all 4 conditions related to
the bases would be fulfilled and not generous
otherwise.
As Chaturbhuj belongs to the same community as
Barrister Chakravarti, he fulfils one of the
requirements. After having a thought of going to the
barrister, he asks us, "So, if I go to the barrister, will it
benefit me?" If we are to provide a clear and definite
response, then for us, the most appropriate and
realistic answer would be to simply say:
"Nothing can be said."
Here, we cannot definitively say whether Chaturbhuj
will benefit or not. Whether he gains or not is
contingent upon self bases and non-self bases. We are
demanded to provide a definite and unequivocal
response to Chaturbhuj. We do not know how many
conditions he fulfils, and we do not wish to keep him
in darkness or false hopes. Hence, following the
172

Barrister Chakravarti

fourth proposition, this is the definite opinion we
must present before him.
We do not disappoint Chaturbhuj by giving the
response, "Nothing can be said." We informed him
about all requirements related to Barrister Sir's
generosity through our response. Now Chaturbhuj
informs us: "I am familiar with Barrister Sir's
generosity, and I need assistance for my son's
education."
Here, this answer fulfils the requirement of self
characteristic. Hence, this case becomes strong.
Therefore, we should consider the benefit of
Barrister's generosity to him. While other
requirements may have been met, we were unaware
of them. Hence, taking refuge in the fifth condition, we
can provide him with a clear response stating:
"Barrister is generous and nothing can be said." Here,
we want to convey that Barrister Sir is generous but
we can't say accurately if Chaturbhuj will benefit from
it or not. Hence, our response is transparent and
realistic.
Now, from conversing with Chaturbhuj, we come to
know that they are generally content. According to
their disclosure, their regular household expenses
are manageable; however, covering the expenses for
their son's college education poses a challenge.
From this, it becomes clear that they are not poor in
the strict sense. The fulfilment of their requirements
regarding the Barrister's generosity, which would
have been complete due to their known generosity, is
now in question. And other requirements are also left
unchecked. In this situation, we conclude that the
173

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

benefit of Barrister's generosity does not reach them.
We must now base our unequivocal opinion on this
situation, whether their belief in Barrister's
generosity proves successful. However, inspite of
this, whether Chaturbhuj gets the benefit or not will
depend on how Barrister sir perceives this
requirement with respect to his self-space. Therefore,
taking refuge in the sixth condition, we can tell
Chaturbhuj, "Barrister's generosity is not there, and
nothing can be said.
In other words, if Chaturbhuj is not poor, then we do
not necessarily perceive Barrister Sir as generous
with respect to his non-self space. When the picture
is unclear with respect to other requirements of
points of view, we do not know what the outcome will
be if Chaturbhuj approaches him. We cannot describe
this unknown condition.
Our response presents Chaturbhuj with a clear
picture that Barrister Sir's generosity may not
necessarily be for him, but still, nothing can be said.
This answer provides Chaturbhuj with a new
perspective, and therefore, guides him to carefully
approach his own case to obtain the desired object
from the barrister, ensuring that misunderstanding
does not arise.
After understanding all this, Chaturbhuj is now
prepared to approach Barrister Chakravarti. While
leaving, he asks, "If I talk to them carefully, will I
surely benefit from Barrister Sir's generosity?"
To answer this question, we will have to resort to the
seventh proposition. We do not want to create false
hopes, nor do we seek to disappoint him. Therefore,
174

Barrister Chakravarti

we tell him, "Barrister is generous, not generous, but
nothing can be said." With this answer, we have given
Chaturbhuj a new independent perspective by
ranking Barrister Sir's generosity, with respect to self
and non-self 4 bases and bringing them all together.
In this way, by giving Chaturbhuj seven types of
opinions according to the seven propositions, we
have prepared a complete picture of Barrister
Chakravarti's generosity.
While talking about Barrister Chakravarti's
generosity, what is it; what is it not; where is it; where
is it not; when is it; when is it not; who can benefit
from it; who cannot; where it may occur; where it may
not; when it may happen; and when it may not;
explaining all aspects through seven different
answers, by summing up all these answers, a clear
picture emerges. In this way, the whole picture is
governed by the concept of Syāt (conditional) word.
This is not a mistake because the entire picture is
based on each point of view. In this picture,
singularity, and plurality both coexist.
We have understood the example of generosity of
Barrister, and hope it is easy to grasp and creates no
conflict or problem in the reader's mind. But, let's not
forget that we have created a fictitious character to
illustrate the multi-faceted aspects of an object, refine
our comparative strength and make it stronger. This
rationale has been applied evenly here.
Similarly, in the judicial proceedings of Barrister, the
use of Syādvāda can also be tried effectively and
accurately.
175

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

For example, suppose there is a murder case
involving a member of the Mehta family. In this case,
as defence counsel, we present this hypothetical
character, Barrister Chakravarti.
Matter, Space, Time, and Characteristic – all 4
viewpoints apply to the case.
(1) Blood is shed after money collection by the
accused person - Matter
(2) There has been bloodshed in Mumbai city - Space
(3) Blood has been shed after noon during
2:30/3:00pm - Time
(4) Blood has been shed with the intention of looting
- Characteristic
Focusing on the defence of the accused and targeting
the above circumstances and facts, Barrister has
prepared a case based on the viewpoints of matter,
space, time, and characteristics.
(1) The accused did not commit the murder - Matter
(2) The accused was not near the laundry area - Space
(3) Between 1 PM and 3 PM, the accused was in
Thane, and there are witnesses to prove it - Time
(4) The accused is not a person who would commit
murder; he has not harmed even an insect or a
mosquito in his life – Characteristic
Now, the murder has occurred is a certain fact; it is
indeed a definite reality, and this fact is being
presented in the case. The question now arises for the
defence of the accused.

176

Barrister Chakravarti

The defence is in the hands of the Barrister. Facing
them is the government lawyer for the prosecution.
Together, they stand, poised, before the judge to
proceed with this case. Witnesses are also expected
from the prosecution side and defence side. Among
the proceedings, members of the jury are also to be
present.
Justice (delivering a verdict) is the responsibility of
the judge. They must first receive the opinion of the
jury. They, with their inherent nature of impartiality,
hold a neutral stance throughout the proceedings.
The prosecution will work diligently to prove that the
accused committed the crime. If the accused is
innocent, the Barrister will leave no stone unturned
to prove it, striving with utmost dedication. After
considering all aspects, determining what is true, and
delivering an unbiased verdict, the ultimate
responsibility to render a fair judgment lies with the
judge. Now, let's see how the judge confronts various
aspects during the proceedings.
(1) After reading the charge sheet submitted by the
prosecution, an opinion emerges that "the accused is
guilty."
(2) Upon reviewing the defence counsel's statement,
another opinion arises that "the accused is not guilty."
(3) Considering the expectations of the charge sheet
and the defence statement, the impartial judge notes:
"The accused is guilty and not guilty."
(4) In these circumstances, rendering a verdict
inexpressible. Nothing can be said about it.

177

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

(5) The statements of prosecution witnesses are
noted, and the defence counsel conducts crossexamination. Upon noting the statements of
witnesses, the accused appears guilty; however, upon
cross-examination, it cannot be concluded that he is
guilty. Hence, "the accused is guilty but nothing
conclusive can be said about the verdict."
(6) The statements of defence witnesses are noted,
and the government lawyer conducts crossexamination. Upon noting the statements of
witnesses, the accused does not appear guilty;
however, upon cross-examination, it cannot be
concluded that he is not guilty either. Therefore, "the
accused is not guilty, but nothing can be stated
definitively about the verdict."
(7) The case has been presented strongly by the
prosecution side, and similarly, there has been a
serious presentation from the defence side as well.
However, the jury's decision is still pending, meaning
no conclusive decision has been made yet regarding
the verdict. Therefore, it appears that the accused is
guilty, he is not guilty and nothing can be said about
it.
Before the honourable judge, these seven distinct
viewpoints have been presented, and a verdict has
been reached. Each perspective, in its own unique
way, has been carefully examined, and collectively,
they are laid before the honourable judge. However,
despite each opinion being different, the honourable
judge is presenting them collectively to the jury. The
honourable judge refrains from prematurely
influencing the jury's decision on whether to deliver
178

Barrister Chakravarti

a verdict of guilty or not guilty. Their own opinion on
the matter will be considered only after the jury's
decision, and then the verdict will be delivered.
Now the jury members proceed to a separate
chamber to deliberate on the case. They exchange
thoughts and opinions amongst themselves. The
defence attorney has presented their case in a clear
and comprehensible manner, aligning with
established laws and regulations, and explained it to
the jury in a way that they can understand.
Considering the evidence regarding the matter, time
and characteristic bases, the jury is deeply impacted
and influenced. Where the crime allegedly occurred,
the accused was not there, and at the time when the
crime allegedly occurred, he was elsewhere, not at the
location of the alleged crime but at the Thane. Such
substantial and credible evidence has been presented
to the court by them. After considering all this, and
conducting thorough deliberations, the jury delivers
a verdict that "the accused is innocent." This verdict
is fair and appropriate, and after complete
deliberation,
the
honourable
judge
must
acknowledge it and declare, "The accused is innocent
and must be acquitted." Such a verdict is accepted.
The accused is acquitted. The barrister achieves
success. The strategy of Syādvāda is victorious.
In this entire case, we have seen that the judge
remains completely impartial and vigilant about their
integrity. The understanding of Syādvāda requires
qualities such as impartiality, extraordinary
intelligence, and profound seriousness to deliver a
fair verdict.
179

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

It has been previously stated that Syādvāda is a
supporter of truth and justice. In this case, the
Barrister presented the accused's defence using this
method of Syādvāda. If he had made a unilateral
statement to present the defence, it could not have
been called Syādvāda. Similarly, even in the verdict
given by the honourable judge, a thorough
application of this principle played a significant role,
which resulted in a successful outcome as evident
from the example provided.
Jain scholars consider Anekāntavāda and Syādvāda to
be extraordinary forms of knowledge and
philosophical understanding. This is a very profound
subject, and Jain scholars have prohibited the
dissemination of this knowledge to everyone. They
have laid down a condition that those whose intellect
is of a high calibre, who are seekers of liberation, who
seek to understand this philosophy for the attainment
of knowledge, and who are fully awake and deeply
serious about life and its goals, only such
distinguished aspirants are to be imparted with
knowledge of this subject. Jain philosophers, for
centuries, have adhered to this condition. As a result,
the widespread dissemination of this wonderful
philosophical knowledge has not occurred.
It is claimed that today we are living in the era of
rationalism. The satisfaction that prevailed in
different fields of life in the past is now replaced by
dissatisfaction. The world today, which you know, is
not content to sit satisfied. The thirst for discovering
new things and understanding them has now begun
to emerge.

Barrister Chakravarti

The time has now come to promote the doctrine of

today than ever before. This emphasis was never

knowledge should now be done with full force.
Derived from the foundation of philosophical inquiry,
the understanding of Anekāntavāda can prove to be

181

CHAPTER 15

Five Types of Knowledge
What should be considered 'knowledge' is a
controversial question on its fundamental premise.
The thirst for knowledge, the aspiration to acquire
knowledge, the curiosity, and the efforts put forth for
it, have become common questions in today's era.
People listen to sermons, read books, and engage in
discussions and deliberations. The intense desire to
attain knowledge arises primarily from pursuing
happiness in this physical world. Learning new things
about various subjects and topics has become a
common practice today, showcasing the dominance
of human thought. This is a positive sign.
However, when it comes to understanding the true
nature of things, what will we gain if we strive
diligently but remain unaware of the object's
essence? Whatever we find, is it the same thing we
wanted or is it something else? How can that be
decided?
The Jain philosophers have formulated a statement
for attaining liberation (Mokṣa):
"Through knowledge and action, liberation can be
attained."
By considering knowledge and action together, a
clearer meaning can also be drawn that liberation can
be attained through action guided by knowledge.
182

Five Types of Knowledge

In this statement, if we substitute the word
'liberation' with any other word or thing we want to
attain, we still need to use the words 'knowledge' and
'action.' These words are eternal. Instead of these two
words, we can also use words like 'eternal ever' and
'eternal couple.'
In this world, both men and women are considered as
the two wheels of a chariot. These two wheels are
inseparable; both are equally important. Only
through both of them can the chariot move; however,
the combination of knowledge and action is so
essential and necessary that the pair of man-woman
will be considered trivial compared to the pair of
knowledge and action.
Be it the smallest or the easiest task of the physical
world or the most difficult task of the spiritual world
for the attainment of spiritual liberation of the soul,
without the unprecedented cooperation of
knowledge and action, it cannot be achieved.
A pair of faith and Intellect is of immense importance,
however, much more significant is the union of
knowledge-action pairs. If we separate these two,
nothing can be achieved. This is an inseparable pair.
If knowledge stands alone, it becomes as useless as a
miser's wealth. Similarly, action separated from
knowledge also becomes unproductive and useless.
For beginners, Jain philosophers have given a very
simple and beautiful explanation of knowledge and
action. 'Certain tasks need to be performed,' this
understanding is knowledge and actually performing
those tasks is action.

183

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

For example, 'speaking untruth is a sin.' This
understanding is knowledge while refraining from
speaking falsehood is action. Similarly, 'telling the
truth is righteousness,' this understanding is
knowledge, and acting accordingly is action. Here we
understand that we should only speak the truth and
never tell lies. If we do not put such understanding,
known as knowledge, into action, then what is the
benefit of such knowledge? In short, if the action that
corresponds to knowledge is not performed, no real
benefit can be derived from that knowledge.
Similarly, what is righteousness, what is worth doing,
and what is worthy of abandoning, if we engage in any
action without understanding these, it will also
become meaningless and sometimes even harmful.
Without these two, we cannot make any progress.
Let's take a simple example. When cooking in the
kitchen, knowledge of various dishes and then the
effort i.e. action required to prepare them will
culminate in the preparation of a delightful meal.
Similarly, in this world, whatever we aspire to
achieve, accomplish, or renounce, whether in the
spiritual or physical realm, cannot be attained
without the combination of knowledge and action.
The phrase 'with the help of knowledge and action' is
powerful and meaningful. We could add a word that
represents the goal or achievement we're aiming for.
For example, we could say, "climbing the mountain
through knowledge and action," but not "mountain
through knowledge and action."

184

Five Types of Knowledge

This verse always applies universally and, applicable
to all objects of attainment. However, this method
must be understood considering other perspectives.
It would be more interesting if we add the word
'knowledge' behind this sentence. Both knowledge
and action are essential for acquiring knowledge. This
statement needs to be understood properly. Here we
divide knowledge into two categories. One is, 'to
attain is knowledge,' and the other is, 'knowledge is
necessary to attain it.' Here the former means, 'the
aim' and the latter means, 'the instrument.'
If one intends to travel from Mumbai to Tokyo,
knowledge of the routes is necessary. Simply
boarding any train, ship, or plane will not get you to
Tokyo. Here, first we decide that we want to go to
Tokyo, then we must find the appropriate routes to
get there. Depending on convenience and necessity,
we determine whether to take a ship or a plane based
on factors like resources and time. Then, the
necessary action, based on knowledge, is taken to
reach Tokyo.
We want to witness the sea. Therefore, where there is
an ocean or coast, we choose such places. Instead of
doing this, if we start trekking in the forests of Gir or
in the Himalayan Mountain ranges, will we be able to
witness the sea?
When we engage in 'knowledge through knowledge
and action,' these things become certain:
(i) We seek to acquire knowledge.
(ii) If we do not have the knowledge of the path to
acquire the knowledge we seek to acquire, then we
185

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

cannot obtain that knowledge. Knowledge about the
process of acquiring knowledge is also a type of
knowledge itself. Therefore, by obtaining an
understanding of the pathway to acquire knowledge,
we gain knowledge.
(iii) By acquiring knowledge of the path to acquire
knowledge, we perform actions according to that
knowledge; hence, we become engaged and active.
Now, in this, there naturally arise several questions:
(i) Our motive behind acquiring knowledge.
(ii) The purity, innocence, and sanctity of this motive.
(iii) By gaining the knowledge we desire, will our
motive be fulfilled?
Before we acquire the necessary knowledge behind
our pure and innocent motives, it's necessary to
understand whether this knowledge will help us
acquire our desired motive. Without clarifying and
comprehending this point, if we engage in any work,
we may end up wasting our efforts, mistaking
illusions for knowledge.
Ignorance, too, is a form of knowledge. False
knowledge holds its place as a kind of understanding.
In today's world, recognizing one's ignorance is a
mark of awareness and wisdom. Acknowledging "I
am ignorant" is, in itself, a profound form of knowing.
There is a popular saying about the ancient
philosopher Socrates. Once, some people heard a
voice from the sky saying, "In this era, the wisest and
most knowledgeable person is Socrates." Upon
hearing this, they went to Socrates, told him about the
heard voice, and asked him, "Is this statement true?"
186

Five Types of Knowledge

After pondering for a while, Socrates gave a response
that is profound and worthy of contemplation. He
said:
"Yes, this statement holds true: I know nothing, yet I
know that I do not know." Thus, someone who
declares, "I am ignorant," stands on par with those
possessing great knowledge, for the curiosity to seek
understanding keeps them alive and vibrant.
However, this curiosity often leads to a great deal of
trouble. The reason is, the existence of false
knowledge (misconceptions) about this world is
there.
For this reason, whoever desires to attain knowledge
should first seek to understand what true knowledge
is. Therefore, one should first seek knowledge and
then action. The action here refers to effort.
To understand the distinction between true and false
knowledge, we should start by recognizing it by
'discrimination between good and bad.' Where does
this understanding come from? How does it arise?
For this, first and foremost, we must gain control
over ego. Until we break free from the notion that "I
know everything, what I understand is only the truth"
we will not progress; it is only when we discard this
notion will we be able to move forward.
The phrase 'Knowledge without a guru is futile' is not
meaningless. First and foremost, one has to become a
disciple. It is necessary to start this with a blank slate.
Today, almost everywhere, everyone appears to be a
'guru,' offering advice and teaching to everyone,
ready to give but not ready to take. And, most of the
187

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

disciples don't come with discipleship but rather the
guruship. They will consider us their guru only until
we agree with or confirm their beliefs. If we say to
them, "Your thinking is wrong," from that very
moment, they will no longer consider us as their guru.
Perhaps they may not speak directly, but they will
start criticizing behind our backs. This is the influence
of ego.
A gentleman considers himself extremely humble and
free from ego. In conversations, they often say, "I
don't have any ego." He continually strives to teach
everyone he meets to renounce ego. Once someone
told him, "You are not what you think of yourself. You
are an ego-centric person." "What?" he asks. Startled,
and looking straight into the person's eyes, he lost his
temper and his eyes turned red.
Such incidents are common nowadays. We need to
come back to the original point of discussion.
After determining the cause of acquiring knowledge,
whether the path to attain that knowledge can lead us
to our desired goal, and whether that knowledge is
true or false, without making sure of these, we cannot
take a single step forward. However, this task of
verification is not easy. It is extremely challenging.
Wherever you look, you see people sitting in the
market of knowledge. Signs displaying "Come here,
we have true knowledge with us, everything else is
false" are hanging everywhere, where should one go?
Whom should one approach?
Real-God, Real-Guru, and Real-Religion - these three
words have become important due to the situation
mentioned above.
188

Five Types of Knowledge

When a garment is torn, we seek a skilled tailor to
mend it. But if the fabric itself is poor in quality, what
response can we expect? Similarly, if we approach a
cobbler with excellent fabric and ask them to make a
shirt, what could be the result?
If we don't cultivate our own curiosity in a pure and
egoless manner, then, how much can a Dev, a Guru, or
Dharma help us? And when we see hangings like,
'Only we have Real-God, Real-Guru, and RealReligion', everywhere, then, how will we determine
who is real and who is not?
"Even God holds a love for his praise," is a saying. All
the brightness of a person becomes secondary in the
presence of his praise. Those who often tell us, "You
are a very good person," we count them as good
people. Those who praise us, we like them, similarly,
when a Guru who praises us says, "You are religious,
you are devoted to religion, you have a high level of
ability," we also like them. Later, the path that this
Guru shows us also seems good and true to us.
From this discussion, we should understand that by
eliminating both attachment to praise and ego, we
should set out in search of Sudeva, Suguru, Sudharma,
and Samyag Jñāna (True Knowledge). If we try to
understand the doctrine of Syādvāda and
Anekāntavāda, then we will receive much assistance
in understanding what true knowledge is and what
the true path to attain it is. We will see now what the
philosophy of Jainism wants to say about knowledge.
Jain philosophers have divided knowledge into two
main categories:
i. Samyag (true) knowledge.
189

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

ii. Mithyā (false) knowledge.
The scholars have further subdivided Samyag
Jñāna into five categories.
i. Mati Jñāna
ii. Ṡruta Jñāna
iii. Avadhi Jñāna
iv. Manahparyava Jñāna
v. Kevala Jñāna
Three types of Mithyā Jñāna (False Knowledge)
are identified:
i. Mati Ajñāna
ii. Ṡruta Ajñāna
iii. Vibhaṅga Ajñāna
There are many other sub-divisions within Samyag
(True) and Mithyā (False) knowledge. We are
concerned with true knowledge therefore, we will
explore the five main types of Samyag Jñāna .
Mati Jñāna: Knowledge obtained through the senses
and mind is called Mati Jñāna.
Ṡruta Jñāna: Knowledge acquired based on words or
scriptures is termed as Ṡruta Jñāna.
Avadhi Jñāna: This is the Knowledge acquired without
the aid of physical senses and the mind. In this, the
soul directly perceives the knowledge about the
characteristics of tangible substances within the limit
of dravya, kṡetra, and kāla.
Manahparyava Jñāna: This Knowledge is perceived
directly without the help of senses and mind. As per
this, one can visualize the things going on in the minds
of humans and animals that possess all the 5 senses.

190

Five Types of Knowledge

Kevala Jñāna: Kevala (Omniscience) is the highest
level of knowledge that can be attained by a soul. It is
absolute knowledge. Nothing remains to be unknown
after attaining Kevala. A kevalī can comprehend and
visualize everything in the universe at all times - past,
present, and future.
This is complete knowledge (omniscience) and those
who attain this knowledge are referred to as Sarvajńa
or kevalī. The complete knowledge of the soul, which
is in the form of self-nature, manifests entirely within
it. This is not just self-knowledge but encompasses
the entirety of the universe and all its creations,
making it the ultimate, complete knowledge.
Out of the 5, the first two types of knowledge, Mati
and Ṡruta, are indirect knowledge because they
involve the senses and the mind as intermediaries.
The last three, Avadhi, Manahparyava, and Kevala, are
direct knowledge; because in these, the soul attains
self-evident and direct knowledge without any kind
of medium.
When we contemplated the sources of knowledge, we
classified perception achieved through the senses
and mind as 'Pratyakṣa' evidence. It's explained here
that both Mati and Ṡruta rely on the assistance of the
senses and mind, hence categorized as Parokṣa Jñāna
or indirect knowledge. The reason for this apparent
contradiction lies in the distinction between practical
and spiritual matters, where there is a difference. In
practical affairs, we replace indirect knowledge with
direct knowledge because the knowledge gained
through the senses is termed as direct rather than
indirect. However, in the spiritual domain, on a higher
level, the situation changes. In practical life, we rely
191

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

solely on the support of the senses and mind. In the
realm of spiritual development, by transcending the
senses and mind, and gaining complete control over
them, the aim is to manifest the pure essence of the
self, known as self-realization.
Jain philosophy distinguishes between two types of
direct evidence based on two separate perspectives:
the senses and mind on one side, and the soul on the
other. These are categorized as 'Sāmvyavahārika
Pratyakṣa' (empirical perception) and 'Pārmārthika
Pratyakṣa' (transcendental perception) respectively.
Knowledge acquired through the senses and mind is
considered indirect in the spiritual domain. However,
in the practical realm, it is classified as direct, also
known as empirical perception. When, what is
termed direct knowledge in the spiritual realm, it is
perceived as transcendental perception. Knowledge
which is revealed, obtained without the assistance of
the senses and mind, is termed as true direct
knowledge. This is because it is an inherent attribute
of the soul, and when knowledge occurs within the
soul without the aid of the senses and mind, it
becomes true direct evidence. In the scriptures, it is
known as 'Pārmārthika Pratyakṣa.' In this meaning,
Mati and Ṡruta are both forms of Parokṣa (indirect
knowledge).
Here,
we
classify
'Avadhi,
Manahparyava, and Kevala' as Pratyakṣa (direct
knowledge). However, within this classification, there
are two divisions. Avadhi and Manahparyava are
termed as Deṡa Pratyakṣa or Anṡa Pratyakṣa (partial
direct knowledge). Kevala is termed as Sakala
Pratyakṣa or Púrṇa Pratyakṣa (complete direct
knowledge).
192

Five Types of Knowledge

Avadhi and Manahparyava do not know all
substances and all alternatives; hence, these two
pieces of knowledge are incomplete as compared to
Kevala. Therefore, they are termed as partial direct
knowledge. But this does not mean that these two
types of knowledge are less pure or less important. As
compared to Mati and Ṡruta, Avadhi and
Manahparyava have a very high status.
Nowadays, the state commonly identified by terms
such as 'self-realization' or 'liberation in life' is more
likely the Mati stage. By harnessing the powers of the
mind, one can achieve significant spiritual
development and thereby attain extraordinary and
fantastic results. Some people count the experiences
obtained in this manner as 'self-realization'.
However, this state is merely a stage of intellectual
knowledge (Mati), implying that it is only a primary
stage of spiritual development. There is also room for
false knowledge within it.
The human mind is a vast subject. Its power is
limitless. Through control and regulation of the mind,
one can understand and comprehend many things.
The mind encompasses one's own eyes, ears, nose,
tongue, and skin. All of these are subtle and hence not
accessible to the physical senses. Through various
ways like yoga, devotion, concentration, etc., one can
achieve limitless progress to the mind's power.
Without the assistance of eyes, the mind can
understand shapes and forms. Without the help of the
ears, it can comprehend distant conversations. Even
without the aid of the nose and touch, the mind can
experience smell and touch. The mind can even read
the thoughts of other individuals.
193

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

By nurturing this extraordinary power of the mind,
individuals sometimes effortlessly become 'great
souls' in this world. Devotees often gather around
powerful spiritual figures. As they immerse
themselves in worldly desires, it becomes an obstacle
to the spiritual development of the so-called social
figure. What is the factor of surprise in it? Sometimes,
success breeds arrogance in these individuals,
leading to a sense of superiority. They start
recognizing themselves as the initiators of religion,
God, or a divine figure.
Yet, those who remain unaffected by such illusions
and instead focus on liberating their minds, entering
the realm of pure consciousness, find a path to true
progress and enlightenment. For others, caught in the
cycle of birth and death, this journey remains elusive.
If this concept is understood quickly, then it can be
very helpful in understanding and achieving Sudeva,
Suguru, and Sudharma. If we cannot be completely
free from physical desires and worldly temptations
even for a moment, then what is so surprising in
considering the so-called and already great souls as
God on Earth? However, from a spiritual perspective,
knowledge acquired through the mind is indirect
knowledge, and only those who have attained direct
knowledge can provide guidance. If we understand
this much, then we will not be amazed by miracles or
dazzled by miracles, nor will we be tempted by them.
Another point to remember is that even in the
spiritual world, the possibility of false or opposite
knowledge exists in both Mati and Ṡruta. Even if the
mind is powerful, still there are chances of it
remaining ignorant. If Ṡruta Jñāna is true, then entry
194

Five Types of Knowledge

into the field of the soul i.e., direct knowledge
becomes possible. However, if it is false or opposite,
then instead of development, regression begins from
there. It leads to the loss of progress and, as a result,
one has to start from where they were. This is a very
understandable matter.
The power of the human mind to perform miracles is
not the means of spiritual development. As we
progress through spiritual practices, in the course of
which our development unfolds, it is natural for the
power of the mind to awaken, and occasionally to
demonstrate some miracles. It is a state similar to the
development state of a flower, fruit, or tree. This is not
the goal. It is not even the means of liberation of the
soul. It is a state of bondage, deception, and
temptation. If caught up in it, then we are at a loss.
How can this be understood better?
Previously, we discussed 'knowledge through action.'
If we understood the crux of this, then the abovementioned matter can be understood completely.
This is the reason why we require a true religion or a
true guru. To get their support, we need
discriminatory knowledge. That knowledge and the
required path are shown by Anekāntavāda and
Syādvāda as a part of Jain philosophy.
We don't expect blind belief in what we're saying, it's
not about blindly following the guru's words like,
"Whatever I say is the only right thing." Instead, we
encourage understanding through appropriate
means and personal experience. True knowledge
comes from direct experience, not indirect sources,
and it should be personally witnessed.
195

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The experiences we have through the help of
the senses and the mind are indirect experiences;
even if they occur on the right path, they are still
incomplete experiences. A complete experience can
happen only through the soul, without the help of any
external medium or assistance. This experience alone
is direct; nothing else.
In politics, for democracy, there is a saying, "of the
people, by the people, for the people." Similarly, in the
field of spirituality, the experience we seek to attain
should be "of the soul, by the soul and for the soul."
This is how self-realization should be perceived.
Through the lens of political philosophy, eminent
thinkers have envisioned the highest ideals of
complete governance and enlightened rule, stating
that "true sovereignty does not necessarily lie in any
form of political structure; rather, it resides in the
people themselves living a noble, virtuous life
without the need for any kind of state apparatus such
as police, military, courts, or even the concept of a
state. When the sentiment of Ṡiva Mastu Sarva
Jagatah (May all beings be happy; may all beings be
peaceful) adorns human society, it transcends
selfishness and narrow-mindedness, embracing the
ultimate truth and universal goodwill. This is the real,
direct democracy.
When equality is embraced, then the state of true and
direct democracy is achieved, both in the political
sphere as well as in the spiritual realm.
In the spiritual domain, the mechanism driven by the
senses and the mind is replaced by the self-operated
mechanism of the soul itself, functioning directly and
196

Five Types of Knowledge

independently. There is no need for any mechanism,
authority, power, or medium; this is the ultimate
spiritual goal for human life. It is where the pursuit of
knowledge leads us, and the attainment of knowledge
becomes the means to validate the inherent potential
of the soul.
Instead, we find ourselves wandering. Our attention
is not directed towards what truly matters, but rather
towards the grasp of our senses and desires, which
have rendered us spiritually inert. We accept what is
untrue as true and what is true as untrue, without
realizing it. We remain indifferent to what truly
matters.
The types of knowledge described by Jain
philosophers are contemplated here. It becomes
apparent that the knowledge accessible to us is
rational and scientific. There is no room for blind faith
or solitary speculation. Just as experiments are
conducted in scientific laboratories, similar
experiments can be conducted in the laboratory of
our souls, where we can experience accuracy,
meaningfulness, and productivity.
From this contemplation on knowledge, we extract
the understanding that true knowledge is
experiential knowledge. Regardless of whether it is
indirect or direct, knowledge is essentially an
experience. We must now accept this fact.
Instead of this, what do we find in this world?
Transitioning from the spiritual realm to the practical
realm, we will realize that here too, there exists a
distinction between knowledge and experience.

197

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

When we address someone as 'knowledgeable' in
practical life, most of them are nothing but a vast
repository of encyclopaedia-like information. What
they have is not knowledge but merely information;
and even that, if compared to available books or
encyclopaedias, it is scanty. Many who are identified
by the name 'knowledgeable' may possess only a
small fraction of true experience.
Why do we engage in accumulating information and
then fail to put it into practice? What benefit do we
derive from all our efforts if we keep the information
recognized as knowledge amassed in our minds
without applying it in our actions? Nothing.
Therefore, wise men have said that instead of having
an ocean of information known as knowledge, it is
better to have a drop of experience in practical life.
The one who transforms knowledge into experience
is truly great.
Hence, the choice of knowledge we make to achieve
our goals, which means to validate our reasons,
should lead us towards direct experience and should
be right. We should select the right path for the
attainment of knowledge. Our intention should be
pure, noble, and spiritual. Upon thoughtful
consideration, we will now understand that:
• True knowledge is the philosophy of Anekānta.
• The true path is Syādvāda.
• The true means is the direct experience of the
soul's true nature.

CHAPTER 16

Karma
'Karma' is a term that signifies Kriyā (action),
indicating that there is action even in a state of
stillness, just as there is action in motion.
Two types of kriyā (actions) are described here: (1)
Dravyakriyā (physical action) and (2) Bhāvakriyā
(mental action). Dravyakriyā refers to actions
performed through the body and senses, also known
as physical actions. Bhāvakriyā, on the other hand,
refers to actions carried out by the mind, such as
thoughts, and emotions like attachment, and
aversion. We can also call this an internal action.
Now, when it comes to karma, if we were to ask a
common person who walks on the streets, they might
answer, "Whatever actions we perform are karma."
While a little more knowledgeable person will say,
"Your actions lead to your karma. You reap what you
sow."
Indeed, the practical understanding of karma is
universal and pervasive; however, the scholarly
understanding of karma is not widely known, not
widely spread.
According to the understanding of Philosophy,
"Karma is not just any action or behaviour; it is an
entity. It is a substance of matter. It consists of its

199

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

material particles. These particles are called the
karma vargaṇā (particles of karma)."
Through mind, speech, and body, whatever actions
we perform are considered activities. Because of
these activities, karma vargaṇā which pervades the
universe, gets attracted and attached to the soul.
When these particles bind the soul, they are known as
'karmic bondage.'
Therefore, from the perspective of philosophy, the
specific formation of matter through action is termed
karma. In this action, after the activity is performed
through mind, speech, and body, there is also the
inclusion of activity related to mithyātva, avirati,
kaṣāya into the soul.
According to the types of actions of the mind, speech,
and body, the accumulation of karmic particles
occurs, which binds and affects the soul. Thus, the
aggregation of particles attracted and chosen by the
soul through actions or activities is referred to as
'karma' in scriptural definitions.
In this context, there are two types: 'dravya'
(substance) and 'bhāva' (mental disposition). The
karmic particles bound to the soul are termed as
dravya karma, while the qualitative results such as
attachment and aversion in the soul are termed as
'bhāva karma.' Both types of karmas have a causeand-effect relationship with each other. The activity
of bhāva karma leads to dravya karma, and the
activity of dravya karma leads to bhāva karma.
This karmic process has been functioning since time
immemorial; it continues to operate until eternity.
200

Karma

What we call life is also just an action. Therefore, from
this perspective, life itself is also karma.
According to the philosophers, there are two types of
soul. These are termed 'Liberated Soul' and 'Bound
Soul.' Here, the term liberated implies karma-free.
The relationship of the soul with karma is also
eternal, just like the eternal existence of the universe.
However, when the soul attains liberation, its
relationship with karma ceases to exist. This implies
that until all karmas are exhausted, the soul is not
'liberated.'
So, as long as the soul wanders in any body, until then,
it continues to perform action. This results in birth,
life, death, and subsequent rebirths across 84 lakh
yoni (the favourable situation for a living being to
take birth), rotating between heaven, earth, and hell.
In the earlier chapter, while discussing 5 reasons, we
see karma as the underlying cause of any event to
take place. It is perceived as the primary cause behind
the soul's worldly experiences, attachment to the
body, likes and dislikes, happiness, and sorrows.
We cannot see 'karma' through our eyes. It is the
subtle form of matter known as Pudgala,
imperceptible to the senses.
According to Jain philosophy, which outlines the
fundamental constituents of the entire universe,
Pudgala is also considered one of the substances. It is
described as a collection of extremely subtle particles,
including atoms and sub-atoms, within this Pudgala
substance. This substance exhibits various
contradictory qualities and attributes.
201

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

In modern science, the tools are created to observe
even finer particles but have not yet reached the level
of detecting the most subtle and sub-subatomic
particles. Just like other subatomic particles, karma is
also a collection of extremely subtler-subtlest
particles, with properties of getting associated and
dissociated. Physical science currently lacks the
means to perceive these. There was a time when all
science enthusiasts did not accept this. However,
today they do not reject it.
Jain philosophers have been saying this for millions of
years. Sound is also a part of Pudgala. It is a group of
matter. At one time, this idea was not accepted by
scientists. When research was done to record sound
on gramophone records, the whole world accepted
this idea.
Without the help of wires, with the advent of wireless,
a revolutionary era of research began in the field of
science. What we speak in Mumbai can resonate
across the entire world in a matter of moments,
thousands of miles away, through scientific
instruments. After experiencing this, it is understood
that the subtlest of matter, akin to subatomic
particles, does exist in this world. This concept from
Jain philosophy has been widely accepted.
Similarly, karma is also a group of Pudgala, a
substance. Although this concept may not be accepted
by the scientific community today, sooner or later, the
acceptance cannot be avoided by the scholar.
In Jain philosophy, 'thought' is also considered a form
of substance. In a pure elemental sense, 'thought' is
the activity of subtle substances in the mind. Just as
202

Karma

speech is the activity of substance, thoughts are also
recognized by the term 'mental processes' and are
considered the activity of substances identified as
Mano Vargaṇā.
Emotional activity occurs through the mind, where
the predominance is of thoughts. Within the mind,
there are two divisions: 'bhāva-mana' (mind
concerned with emotions) and 'dravya-mana' (mind
concerned with physical structure). Here, dravyamana is associated with substance, not with
emotions. The substantial mind is material, while the
bhāva-mana is associated with a state of feelings and
emotions.
If we were to interpret the term 'Pudgala' directly, it
could be said that it is a thing that has the attributes
like purāṇa (that can be gathered), galana (that can
be separated), forms, taste, smell, touch, etc.
If we accept that Pudgala is only a substance which
can be seen by the naked eye or observed through
human-made instruments in laboratories, then that
would mean the end of human research; what
humans perceive becomes the only reality, and
nothing beyond that could be acknowledged. We
wouldn't be able to accept this. No wise person would
consider such a conclusion as acceptable.
Today, through the ongoing research and
experimentation conducted by science, which is
continuously yielding new findings and introducing
innovative practices, we are presented with a clear
and undeniable reality, akin to a shining lamp. The
clear and certain fact remains that "there is still much
to know."
203

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

If we think carefully, we will realize that through new
research, whatever has been presented to us isn't
entirely new. We haven't encountered anything new
about the nature of the world that wasn't already
spoken about by wise and knowledgeable beings
thousands of years ago and documented in
the scriptures.
In recent times, the excavation of remnants from
ancient civilizations, such as the remains of ancient
cities, has brought to light the production of
substances similar to plastic and bakelite, which were
thought to be recent innovations.
This revelation doesn't aim to degrade modern
science. What they discovered, revealed, and
continue to explore today is a testament to the
enduring intellectual prowess they possess. Our
sincere appreciation for the Scientists, who have
worked for the highest benefactors of humanity.
However, we cannot overlook the fact that the
instruments of human destruction are very
dangerous inventions by them. Whether in the 20th
century or throughout all time, scientific research
stands as a beacon in the annals of human intellectual
history, and this fact cannot be denied.
It's unfortunate that instead of seeking fleeting and
perishable pleasures, if human intellect had been
devoted to the pursuit of ultimate and eternal
happiness, then rather than instilling fear and terror
in the existence of human society, the world could
have been united through the transformative joy and
bliss brought forth by divine instruments of supreme
delight.

Karma

Learning about the methods of these scientists'
research can be quite intriguing. Typically, it begins
with making assumptions based on two types of
beliefs. One assumption, believing that 'a certain
thing exists this way,' and the other assumption,
believing that 'the thing does not exist this way.'
This contains some fascinating insights. Often,
important discoveries have come from the belief of its
non-existence, rather than faith. Many times, strong
convictions have emerged from efforts to prove its
non-existence. When we don't believe, we make
efforts to prove it, which ultimately strengthens our
beliefs.
In 1492, when Columbus set out to discover Bhārata,
he discovered the American continent. Information
about the route through land and trade with Bhārata
was readily available to Europeans, but at that time,
no one knew about the western hemisphere. In this
twist of fate, the interaction between the populations
living on both hemispheres was destined, influenced
by the wheel of time. Columbus became an
instrument of destiny, turning human curiosity into a
quest, and dragging him to the west instead of the
east. This serves as a perfect example of the profound
impact of five significant causes!
The outcome of Columbus's expedition illustrates the
diligent efforts of a person guided by noble
aspirations, along with other four reasons, to receive
some fruits; whether it's Bhārata or America. One
may have stumbled upon diamonds while searching
for the coals.

205

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Isn't human curiosity marvelous? How remarkable is
human curiosity! Believing that something exists or
should exist, one makes an effort to prove it; and
believing that something does not exist, one also
makes an effort to prove, 'it must not be like that'.
All science students know about this. Assuming that
something 'does not exist', they begin and conduct
experiments in their laboratories and then reach the
conclusion that it 'does exist'. Science students
conduct practical experiments in their laboratories
using many different methods. One of them is as
follows: A student takes a piece of paper and believes
that 'this is not paper'. Then, after conducting
experiments and in-depth analysis, they concluded
that 'this is paper'. Initially, they make assumptions of
non-existence and then correct these assumptions
based on their findings. The discussion about the
paper is just an example to illustrate this method.
In this manner, those who conduct experiments
based on the assumption of 'yes' or 'no' do indeed
yield some results. Failure is also a result, and for the
truly determined individuals, it is the precursor to
success.
Therefore, we must desire that scientific research
continues, but is there any need to reveal that our
desire should align with the sentiment of human
welfare?
In this world, the question is whether the
understanding of sukṣma (subtle) substances can be
achieved through physical laboratories. For this
purpose, there is no other way but through 'Ātmika
Anubhāva,' (self-experience). Today, what can be
206

Karma

called as sukṣma, science has reached even the level
of atoms and subatomic particles. These are all
tangible (material) substances. Among these, the
ones that are subtler and subtlest, the experimental
discovery of it is not impossible to achieve.
In actions and thoughts, the subtlest of particles is not
only proven by scripture but also by experiential
evidence. Modern scientists, whether attempting to
conduct experiments by assuming 'This is Pudgala' or
by striving to prove 'this is not Pudgala', will
ultimately benefit humanity, there is no doubt in that.
Similarly, because the Pudgala are extremely sukṣma
(subtlest substances), and because they cannot
become the object of the senses, the efforts to
perceive them are unlikely to bear fruit.
However, it is rightful to hope that the hard work of
modern scientists will lead them to one or the other
results in this regard. While science accepts the
existence of physical particles in the body, research
on the particles of karma would not go in vain. Atleast
by conducting such studies, ultimately, they will be
attracted towards spiritual laboratories.
All the activities, peculiarities, complexities, and
development or degradation of this world are
attributed to 'karma,' and there is currently no
disagreement on this matter. Numerous significant
texts have been written on the science of karma. Some
people consider karma to be the sole cause for all the
activities of this world, deeming all the actions of the
world to be the result of karma alone. This topic has
been written about since ancient times, continues to
be written about, and will remain a subject of writing.
207

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Moreover, Jain literature available on karma is so
vast, profound, and extensive that it is unparalleled
anywhere in the world. When we read the
karmagranthas (scriptures on karma) written by Jain
monks, we are astonished and overwhelmed by the
abundance of details. We find so many intricacies in
the discussion of karma that we are fascinated. The
minute analysis of karma is found only in Jain
literature. This fact has now been acknowledged
worldwide. The detailed description and complete
details of the science of karma are found only in Jain
literature.
The relationship between the soul and the karmic
particles, as well as the unfathomable and boundless
play of Pudgala, is such a vast subject that specialized
capabilities are required just to comprehend it.
Literature that fully explains this subject and makes it
easily understandable is still alive in Jain philosophy
today. This is a great fortune for both us and the
world.
Karma, as a concept, and the fact that karma alone
governs much of the world's happiness, sorrow,
equality, inequality, and more, is now universally
accepted. Even the proponents of theism, when they
say, "Sitting as a mere spectator, Rama distributes
everyone's rewards according to their deeds," are
also referring to this concept of karma. In this verse,
the term 'deed' indicates karma.
The relationship between the soul and karma is an
eternal struggle. It is often described as a 'tug of war,'
pulling in opposite directions, representing a game
that has been played between the soul and karma
208

Karma

since time immemorial. The true beginning of this
game is when the soul sets out on the path of
liberation, and from that moment, it becomes
comprehensible.
Kartā and bhoktā (doer and bearer) of Karma are the
soul itself. The power to become liberated from
karma is also the soul's power. The soul experiences
all sensations of pleasure and pain. The reasons for
these experiences are also known to the soul.
However, this power of knowing has been obscured
by the soul's own karmas, which Jain philosophers
refer to as 'obscuring karmas.' Through such actions,
the soul itself creates its difficulties. Soul's own
Antrāya karmas prevent one in its own development,
