in the manifestation of its pure form, and spiritual
progress.
When a child or an animal is pricked by a thorn, the
pain experienced is not just associated with the body
alone; it is associated with the soul. Because if this
connection were not with the soul but only with the
body, then even a lifeless body would experience such
sensations. This does not happen. On a typical day,
when a person gets slightly burnt by a matchstick,
they react. However, when the entire body is reduced
to ashes in the crematorium, even in that state, there
is no reaction, nor does the thorn cause any
discomfort. All sensations of pleasure and pain, all
consciousness, are not with the body but with the
soul. The conscious entity residing in the inert body is
associated with the soul. To understand this and to
accept it, just one example is enough. Many examples
could be provided to explain this, but today, it is
209

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

unnecessary to elaborate extensively on this matter,
as this concept has been widely accepted.
The soul is of the nature of consciousness, while
the body is non-living. Karma, being a form of
Pudgala, is inherently non-living, like the material
constituents of the body. The conscious soul residing
in the body is disturbed and influenced by these
karmic particles which have been binding the soul
since time immemorial.
Due to the strength of karma, one cannot escape from
it. However, the soul can understand the concept of
'we should and can escape from this even then'. When
the power of karma becomes strong, one forgets the
concept of 'escaping.' As a result of repeated actions
and experiences, the one keeps revolving in the
endless struggle between the soul and karma.
Sometimes, what the soul desires, it cannot attain,
even with effort, it is unable to attain it. For example,
two disciples of the same guru work equally hard; one
becomes enlightened, and the other remains
unlearned. The effort of the latter is not any less;
however, he is not able to attain the knowledge. Here,
the karma which is responsible for such a hindrance
is termed as 'Jñānāvaraṇiya karma' by Jain
philosophers. However, karma that perpetually
obstructs him now, would not let it happen always.
The boundaries of time to shed off that karma depend
on the efforts of a soul.
The classification of karma, as done by Jain
philosophers, reveals the fundamental eight types of
karma, followed by 158 sub-categories. There are
uncountable sub-categories to it.
210

Karma

Among these, the eight main types of karma include
'Jñānāvaraṇiya karma', which veils the soul's
inherent knowledge qualities and does not allow
them to manifest. It hides the soul's knowledge
attributes from all directions, like a moonless night
covers the Earth with a black, thick layer. Sometimes,
it deludes the soul into ignorance, while at other
times, it dims the desire for acquiring knowledge, the
desire for gaining enlightenment.
The effects of this karma i.e. degree and extent could
have minimal or maximum impact. According to these
factors, it obstructs the soul's quality to know.
The second main type of karma is identified by the
name 'Darṡanāvaraṇiya karma.' This karma obstructs
the soul's power to see. It obstructs even the
awakened state of the soul. There's also a degree and
extent to this.
The third main type of karma is identified by the
name 'Mohaniya karma.' Moha is a formidable enemy
of the soul. It distorts the soul's pure nature. Due to
this, the cause of suffering appears to be happiness. It
deludes the soul into attachment, thus leading it
astray. These are two types of mohaniya karma
known as 'Darṡana mohaniya karma' and 'Cāritra
mohaniya karma.'
Darṡana mohaniya karma obstructs the soul's
inclination towards true knowledge, creating
confusion in the understanding of reality. Cāritra
mohaniya karma hinders dispassion, giving rise to
attachment, aversion, jealousy, deceit, malice, and
other such emotions, fostering desires and delusions.
211

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The fourth type of karma is known as 'Antarāya
karma.' Through this karma, obstacles arise in
virtuous actions. It creates hindrances in the
acquisition of objects for charity and enjoyment. The
customary practice of conducting a ritual to dilute the
effect of this karma is a common Jain tradition.
Behind the performance of this ritual lies the belief
that it helps in dispelling obstacles caused by
antarāya karma, allowing the soul to remain pure.
The purification of intentions is considered a kind of
auspicious karma, as it helps counteract the adverse
effects of antarāya karma. Furthermore, through
devotion to Tīrthaṅkara Arihaṅta and austerity,
liberation can also be attained from this karma.
These four karmas are identified as 'Ghāti-karma.'
The term Ghāti-karma is used because these karmas
have the potential to destroy the essential qualities of
the soul due to their destructive power.
The fifth main type of karma is referred to as 'āyuṣya
karma.' This karma determines how long the soul will
remain in a particular body while roaming across four
gati.
The sixth main type of karma is called 'Nāma karma.'
This karma determines the specific characteristics
such as the body shape, form, and color in which the
soul will be mandated by this karma. The soul's
attributes such as body, form, color, senses, behavior,
characteristics, fortune, misfortune, subtlety, or
grossness are determined by the Nāma Karma.
The seventh main type of karma is identified as 'Gotra
karma.' This karma is responsible for determining the
212

Karma

birth in different levels such as revered or nonrevered caste.
The eighth main type of karma is known as 'Vedaniya
karma.' This karma affects the soul by imparting
feelings of pleasure and pain. It is divided into two
categories: 'ṡātāvedaniya' (feelings of pleasure) and
aṡātāvedaniya (feelings of pain), which are Jain
philosophical terms. Even sensations like hunger and
thirst that we experience are considered one form of
aṡātāvedaniya karma for the soul.
The last four karmas are referred to as 'aghāti
karmas'. These karmas do not directly affect the
primary attributes of the soul; instead, they regulate
its movement. They determine in which realm the
soul will reside, whether it will inhabit the body of a
monkey, a human, or any other form. They decide
whether one will be born as a beggar, wealthy person,
or a scholar, and what kinds of experiences of
pleasure and pain the soul will undergo, whether it
will reside in pleasant or unpleasant circumstances.
They do not directly contradict the soul's primary
qualities. These karmas are not directly obstructive
or harmful to these qualities.
So, these are called aghāti karmas; however, these
karmas incite or stimulate ghāti karmas.
The eight main types of karmas are described by Jain
philosophers. To attain liberation, the soul must be
free from the bondage of karma. When a soul gets free
from the cycle of birth and death, it achieves
liberation. Only after the kṣaya (destruction) of these
eight karmas that the soul attains the state of Siddhapada.
213

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

After the destruction of the first four karmas, Jain
Tīrthaṅkaras attain omniscience. The soul becomes
the Paramātma (Supreme Soul), and Kevalajañāna
(Omniscience) manifests after the destruction of the
first four karma. After attaining Kevalajañāna, the
supreme soul imparts knowledge to the living beings
in the form of Samyagjñāna (Right Knowledge),
Samyagdarṡana (Right Vision), and Samyagcāritra
(Right Conduct). During this time, four aghātikarmas are still there on the soul of the supreme soul.
Aghāti karmas do not obstruct the basic quality of the
soul. When the time comes for the Tīrthaṅkara soul to
leave the body by experiencing the effects and closing
down the account of the remaining four aghāti
karmas, they attain liberation.
When Tīrthaṅkara attain omniscience, they perceive
and understand all material substances, both tangible
and intangible, along with their inherent
contradictions. When a Tīrthaṅkara attains
Kevalajñāna, he understands rūpī (visible), arūpī
(invisible), sukṣma (subtle) and sthūla (gross objects),
as well as all of its multiple and opposite properties.
Without reaching that level, this knowledge cannot be
obtained. With the intention of kindness, and to make
the world aware of it, they have shown us a way
which is Syādvāda.
The knowledge directly acquired by Tīrthaṅkara can
be understood by all beings, considering their
capability in terms of matter, time, space, and
characteristics. This knowledge is obtained through
Syādvāda. The particular importance of Syādvāda lies
in the fact that: "Whatever the omniscient knows
214

Karma

directly through Pratyakṣa Jñāna, is also known
indirectly through Syādvāda. Both kevalajñāna and
syādvāda are illuminative in nature; one is direct and
the other indirect. In other words, Parokṣa Jñāna
(indirect knowledge) obtained by Syādvāda, becomes
Pratyakṣa (direct) by kevalajñāna."
This statement is true. In other philosophies, the
great souls who come to know the truth through their
experiences, write according to their experience. For
humans, apart from faith, there is no other means.
Omniscients have benefited the world through their
direct experiences, which can be understood
indirectly, even by scientific or philosophical
methods, in a way that is accessible to the intellect.
This method is called Syādvāda. Everyone is invited to
see, know, and understand it; we can offer it without
hesitation and with complete confidence. However,
one should approach it with sincere faith and then
examine it critically.
A notable point to remember is that this process,
wherein the soul can perform actions, is possible only
when embodied in a human form. Destroying all
karmas isn't just possible for other living beings,
except for humans. Ekendriya (living beings with only
one sense) upto tiryaṅca paṅcendriya (animals with 5
senses) cannot manifest this power of the soul.
As a result, when experiencing human existence, the
soul attains a state of utmost elevation during its
journey. The soul, even if assuming the form of a
celestial being in heaven or a divine being in higher
realms, cannot exert extraordinary efforts there for
its liberation. It is only possible when the soul
215

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

incarnates into a human body to attain liberation
from the cycle of birth and death. The human body is
the most suitable instrument for the soul's liberation,
and without it, the soul cannot be redeemed.
The human body, the luminous side of human
existence, is indeed bright, but there is another side
to it. The body is the highest means for the soul's
salvation, providing a path to liberation, yet it can also
lead to naraka (hell). In this life, the human body can
deepen karma or increase its bondage, causing the
soul to experience intense struggles. It is within this
human existence that the soul feels the full weight of
karma and the effort to transcend it.
One might ask: Why is it so? Liberation can be
attained only from the human realm, and why not
from any other realm?
The answer to this is simple and straightforward. All
the ingredients like discernment, contemplation,
renunciation, detachment, and the power of speech,
along with all other skills and virtues, reside within
the human body where the soul dwells. Now, in which
other body could they be found? The creative energy,
which is as much as in the human body, exists in
which other body? The soul, along with the supreme
medium, will endure for its liberation.
An airplane takes us higher and higher into the sky.
But if it breaks there, one can hardly find the remains
of the bones. Similarly, by using this human life
properly, the soul can manifest its highest qualities;
or by misusing it, the soul deteriorates itself. Even the
extreme worst situation can arise. Except for humans,
216

Karma

no other being in any other realm has the potential for
such drastic deterioration of the soul.
In all of the soul's wanderings, the karma that plays
an important role is classified simply as punya
(virtue) and pāpa (sin).
Once, a spiritual seeker approached a saintly person
seeking a concise explanation about sin and virtue.
The seeker asked the saint to explain in just two
sentences and within eight letters each. The saint
replied:
"परोपकारः पुण्याय, पापाय परपीडनम्" (Harming others is a sin.
Helping others is a virtue)
In essence, "Virtue is attained through benevolence,
while sin is incurred by causing harm to others. Any
action that causes pain or suffering to others is
considered sinful, while thinking of the welfare of
others and nurturing feelings of compassion is
virtuous." These two sentences encompass profound
understanding.
Virtuous deeds provide both material resources and
spiritual enlightenment to the soul. Sinful actions, on
the other hand, subject the soul to physical and
spiritual
distress.
Jain
philosophers
have
meticulously analyzed the intricacies of actions in
both virtuous and sinful domains.
Punyānubaṅdhi punya, punyānubaṅdhi pāpa,
pāpānubaṅdhi punya, pāpānubaṅdhi pāpa, etc. are
concepts found in greater detail in Jain philosophy.
(Punyānubaṅdhi punya refers to the accumulation of
new merits, i.e., the rise of virtue even in affluent
217

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

circumstances. Likewise, the other three also should
be understood).
The karmas, which bind the soul through the body,
i.e., the senses, are lesser when compared to the
karmas that bind the soul through the mind. The
bondage and liberation of karma are greatly
influenced by the internal mind (subconscious mind).
Moreover, with respect to the soul, mental activities
such as thoughts and desires play an important role
in bondage and liberation. The internal feelings of the
soul, its attachments, thoughts, and other movements
are known as 'adhyavasāya'.
A gambler within his own home, engages in various
activities worth millions via telephone, sitting idle.
Similarly, the soul performs internal activities
without any external connection. It is said in one
samaya (the smallest unit of time), our soul binds
countless karmas and releases them. Samaya is the
subtlest division of time, when we refer to a 'second,'
countless divisions of a second are recognized as one
samaya. Infinite atoms of karma are bound and
released within this time span.
The complete knowledge of karma theory is
extremely profound, intricate, and vast. Its complete
and direct knowledge is only possible when one
attains omniscience. However, to understand this
indirectly, the treasure of this knowledge is available
to scholars of the Jain scriptures.
In this world, people tend to have fear about diseases,
poison, tigers, snakes, deception, knives, swords,
pistols, machine guns, and bombs. We should be
afraid and cautious about karma-pudagals. The
218

Karma

dreadful nature of these material elements for the
soul is not found in any other objects.
However, it is not necessary to despair from this
karma. The path to liberation and salvation from it is
the right royal highway, which Jain philosophers have
opened up to the entire world. To understand this
path, the principle doctrine is Anekāntavāda, the
method is Syādvāda, and to practice it, there are
'Samyag Darṡana' (Right Vision), 'Samyag Jñāna'
(Right Knowledge), and 'Samyag Cāritra' (Right
Conduct).
After understanding the concept of karma to this
extent, we will grasp another new concept. It is a dual
activity. On one hand, we (our soul) perform actions.
At the same time, from the perspective of karma's
particles, they also engage in activities of attachment
or liberation. This means that the activity of both our
soul and the particles of karma takes place
simultaneously. During the lecture when we speak, at
that time the activity of speaking and the activity of
listening
by
the
audience
also
operate
simultaneously, this we know. Similarly, the
speaker's bhāva kriyā (emotional state) attracts the
particles of karma towards oneself, and the impact in
the form of impressions in the listener's mind also
attracts the particles of karma toward oneself. Hence,
for both the speaker and the listener, there is a dual
perspective with respect to the activities of karma.
It's very interesting to know that, the karmas
attracted by different listeners do not necessarily
mean one type. For instance, when a speaker
discusses a principle opposed by society, one listener
219

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

might feel less inclined, thinking, "Oh Lord, let him get
the true sense," while another listener might think,
"He must be punished and executed." The thoughts
that arise in the minds of both listeners, of different
types, pull and push the elements of karma, which
manifest in different ways. It might also happen that
hearing one sentence, a person's karma might be
bound, while it might lead to the dissolution of
another's karma.
But, don't be disappointed by this understanding of
karma. The path to liberation from it and to avoid it is
a well-explained concept in Jain philosophy. We can
practice it by Samyag Darṡana (right vision), Samyag
Jñāna (right knowledge), and Samyag Cāritra (right
conduct).
To explain this concept thoroughly, Jain philosophy
distinguishes karmas into two types. One is 'samyak'
(true) action and the other is 'mithyā' (false) action.
The action performed with a proper understanding of
the soul's true nature is called 'samyak' action. On the
contrary, the action performed without such
understanding is termed as 'mithyā' action.
Here too, jñāna and kriyā (knowledge and action)
complement each other. Knowledge without action is
as futile as treasures buried in the ground, which are
of no use until discovered. Similarly, action without
true understanding, without true comprehension, is
also fruitless. For instance, if someone plants a tulsi
sapling in a pot in their courtyard and instead of
watering the soil, they pour ghee, what will happen?
Although ghee is more nourishing than water, will it
be beneficial to spend money on ghee rather than
220

Karma

water even if he is very rich, if the sapling needs water
and not ghee, then what good does it serve? Actions
without knowledge are like this, futile and ineffective.
In samyag kriyā (right action), the observance of
jñānācāra, darṡanācāra, cāritrācāra, tapācāra and
viryācāra are included. There are two types of these
actions: one is Dravyakriyā (physical action), and the
other is Bhāvakriyā (mental action). A person who
possesses true knowledge, and doesn't observe kriyā,
then his knowledge only becomes a store of
information. It may be of use to others; it is worthless
for oneself. This knowledge comes into practice when
both Dravya (physical) and Bhāva (mental) have a
relationship with each other. Without Dravyakriyā,
Bhāvakriyā does not arise, and without Bhāvakriyā,
Dravyakriyā does not yield fruit.
If external actions are dry, inert, devoid of
understanding, and solely devoid of emotion, then all
such kriyā would not lead one to liberation. This holds
true in practical life as well. A task performed without
focus does not yield good results. During daily
activities, worship, study, contemplation, devotion,
and singing hymns are all physical actions. The
purpose of this is to awaken emotions, organize the
mind's fluctuations, and cultivate equanimity. When
performing all these physical actions, if there is a lack
of understanding of "what am I doing and why am I
doing it," then such activities are chosen fruitlessly.
Moreover, if the mind wanders elsewhere during
these actions, then such actions would go in vain.
Therefore, it is said that any action, whether it be
obtaining liberation or frying fritters, is fruitless
without a combination of knowledge and action.
221

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

When internal awareness is not present during
external actions, there is no need to abandon those
actions. However, if they are abandoned, it can lead to
dangerous consequences because, after the cessation
of external actions, only harmful false activities such
as violence may remain, and there is no power to
instill virtuous feelings in these false activities.
Creating ṡhubh-bhāva (good intentions) is not a piece
of cake. Therefore, considerable efforts must be made
to understand the proper actions beforehand. Hence,
during external actions, if bhāva is not present, then
instead of abandoning those actions, it is beneficial to
continue them to maintain alertness and keep the
purpose alive. Sometimes, at an opportune moment,
internal awareness will arise. If unprecedented
awareness is attained, then after there is a chance, we
will attain unprecedented meaningfulness. The
condition for this is only that essential understanding
and effort are necessary. To obtain the perception of
feelings, we must remain diligent; if internal
awareness is not present in us, it is the deficiency of
our efforts, and we should devote all our abilities to
it. If we neglect this aspect, then such dry actions will
always remain unfruitful.
We have obtained a general understanding of karma,
the nature of karma, and its effects here. However,
there is a curiosity to know more about this subject
that will certainly arise. For this reason, it is essential
to engage in the company of a knowledgeable person
in the form of a Sadguru or a saint. If we set out to
search, we will surely find answers. Let us engrave
this quest in our minds and move forward.
222

CHAPTER 17

Development of the Soul
In the previous chapter, we discussed karma. The
relationship between karma and the soul is eternal,
and we understood this.
Now, let's also ponder over the soul. What is the soul?
Who?
A friend had gone to visit a house and he saw that the
main door of the house was closed. But from inside he
could hear friends and family members in friendly
conversations. To confirm that he had come, he
knocked on the door.
The question asked from within was, "Who is there?"
The one who knocks says, "It's me, Chandubhai."
This answer means that the one who knocks is me
and 'me' is known by the name of Chandubhai. Hence,
Chandubhai is Nāma Nikṣepa of I.
Each person uses the word 'I' for themselves.
So now the question arises, when each person uses
the word 'I' for themselves, what is 'I?'
Tongue does the talking, knocking on the door is done
by the hand. For each part of the body and senses
work, it is said that "I am seeing, I am talking, I am
hearing, I am breathing, I am getting burnt, I am
walking" Using these sentences comes the word 'I,'
who is I?
223

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Is it the tongue? The eye? The ear? The nose? The
hand? The foot? The skin?
You will say, "No, it's all parts of the body. It's all of
them. Whatever makes up the entire body is 'I'."
Now, if we consider the body as 'I,' then why doesn't
the voice of 'I' come from the dead body?
The answer to this question is simple: when the soul
departs from the body, nothing can be called 'I' in the
body. However, as long as the soul resides in the body,
the word 'I' is used.
The living being used not only the word 'I' but also the
word 'my.' When it says 'my body,' 'my hands,' 'my
eyes,' 'my nose,' 'my ears,' etc., it conveys another
piece of information. This information is that what I
call 'mine' is not 'I.'
When we use words like 'my home' or 'my clothes,'
these things are separate from me, the owner.
Similarly, when the 'I' residing in the body uses
phrases like 'my body' or 'my hands,' it indicates that
the 'I' and the body parts are separate. This clarifies
that the 'I' being referred to is the soul. The conscious
entity that identifies itself as 'me' is the soul. So, when
we say 'I' or 'mine,' including the body, we are also
referring to the soul. This is a relative statement, so
keep that in mind.
In earlier chapters, we discussed two main types of
souls: the liberated soul and the karma-bound soul. A
soul liberated from all karmas has no sense of 'I' or
'mine.' Therefore, when we think of the soul, we refer
to the soul as 'I,' we should understand it as the
karma-bound soul.
224

Development of the Soul

How are these karmas tied to the soul? The answer to
this question is given by the knowledgeable saints:
"The relationship between karma and the soul is like
that of milk and water." When karma binds, the bond
formed between karma and the soul is like the
combination of milk and water. Therefore, after the
combination of milk and water, the unity that arises
between them. Similarly, once the bond is formed, the
soul remains with karma. This explanation is quite
understandable. The original substance, the soul, is
pure. Due to the bond with karma, it becomes impure.
This point is worth understanding. Essentially, the
soul is pure. It becomes impure due to the association
with karma. The states of purity and impurity do not
exist separately. Together, they form an independent
existence. Therefore, when the independent soul and
the independent karma particles combine, the third
independent existence that emerges is referred to by
scholars as the karma-bound or worldly soul. We
recognize it as a living body.
Now, when this soul identifies its body by saying
'mine,' it is because, being encompassed in it, we can
also call the living body 'soul,' with a relative
perspective. It is understood from this that karmabound soul knows its independent existence, and
hence it distinguishes itself as 'I' and identifies the
body and its parts as 'mine,'
Now, let's elaborate sequentially on 'I' and 'mine.'
This should be quite interesting.
First and foremost, the direct perceptual relationship
with the soul that the body exhibits, the word 'I' is
used for the body too.
225

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Now, let's move forward with this understanding.
"This is my father; this is my mother, this is my wife.
These are my sons and daughters. This is my friend."
When we pronounce such words, at that moment, in
our minds, there is a sense of ownership.
In one aspect, it operates through impressions; in
reality, the soul and the body are distinct, and both
substances are different, but due to the association of
karma, when they become one when a predisposition
to become one is found, they become one from two.
Let's take this discussion further. When we refer to
family and friends as 'mine,' we start to feel a sense of
'oneness' with them. In this manner, as we
contemplate, with the imprint of 'mine' on
everything—my family, my caste, my society, my
home, my village, my district, my region, my country,
my Earth, my sky, and my universe - we can
experience unity and acknowledge that all this too is
a part of 'I.'
In this way, when the sense of unity with all living
beings in the world emerges due to collective
experiences, the feeling of 'I' will naturally arise
among all living souls. Similarly, by associating our
soul with the inert substances of the world, the
impression that "all this is also mine" will settle in the
mind, leading to a realization of 'I' (self-nature) in all
aspects.
When we can cultivate the feeling that everything is
'mine' or 'I,' rather than having the notion of 'mine,'
yours,' and 'theirs,' then one of the main enemies of
the soul, dweṣa (aversion), will certainly be defeated.
226

Development of the Soul

Therefore, from a relative perspective, when we seek
to understand the soul, we conclude that the soul is 'I'
and 'I' is the entire universe.
Having heard such discussions about the soul, will
you also pose a similar question regarding 'Advaita'
from the perspective of Ṡri Ṡankarācārya?
No, this Advaita is not as per the doctrine of Vedanta.
The perspective of Vedanta is only single-ended. The
discussion we had was from a relative perspective.
There are clear shades of Anekāntavāda. This should
not be misunderstood. From the perspective of
Sangraha Naya (inclusiveness), we relate to the entire
universe as an embodiment of Sat (existence).
Now, let's return to 'I'. This 'I' is indeed ourselves.
Understanding this, let's move forward.
"What do we desire? What do we wish for?"
If we want a short and precise answer to these
questions, we hear a word of nine letters in response.
"Happiness."
We seek happiness. This happiness we need 'for
ourselves'. We must make efforts to attain this
happiness. What we desire is 'happiness’, not
'sorrow'. Even if there is a little sorrow in it, we do not
consider it 'happiness'.
For our happiness, if our efforts cause someone else
to suffer or experience loss, that sorrow becomes
ours as well. True happiness is not just for us alone.
As we develop this feeling of 'mine', we come to
realize that, "I am the entire universe." Therefore, if
any suffering affects any body, mind, or life in the
227

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

universe, it also affects us. If something we create
causes others sorrow, we should see it as our own
suffering.
In reality, this is true. Even if we don't acknowledge it
due to selfishness, ultimately such efforts lead to our
own sorrow. Accepting this fact is the only way to
avoid suffering. This understanding reminds us of our
ultimate duty. Jain scriptures summarize this duty in
four sentences.
khāmemi savva jīve
savve jīvā khamantu me
mittī me savva bhūesu
veraṁ majjhaṁ na keṇaī
The meaning of these four sentences respectively is:
"I forgive all living beings."
"Let all living beings forgive me."
"I have friendship with all living beings."
"I have enmity with none."
To begin one's spiritual development, one must start
with these four sentences. With these four sentences,
unity and harmony must be experienced.
There is a prior condition for the manifestation of
unity and harmony. Its name is gratitude, meaning
whoever has done us a favor, thankfulness towards
all. Modesty is the essence of manifesting this
gratitude.
Modesty means the complete abandonment of ego. If
modesty is not present, the attitude of respect does
228

Development of the Soul

not arise. The tendency to forgive and seek
forgiveness does not arise if respect does not arise. If
there is no tendency to seek forgiveness, then there is
no inclination to cultivate friendship or to eliminate
enmity. If none of the above we can do, then not even
a single step is taken forward in the development of
our soul. If one does not progress, one never attains
happiness.
Here, when we speak of 'happiness,' it is said with
respect to treating all beings as own-self. By
understanding that if someone else's suffering causes
sorrow for us, then our effort to attain happiness
naturally moves towards the welfare of all living
beings.
If the abandonment of ego occurs, modesty arises,
Namaskārabhāva manifests, the tendency of
forgiveness emerges, friendship is cultivated, and
enmity is eradicated, then as its natural consequence,
the attitude of 'Ṡivamastu Sarvajagatah' - 'May the
welfare of all living beings be accomplished' arises
within us. If this does not arise, then nothing
indicated above has developed within us; then we are
merely human in appearance. If it seems otherwise, it
must be a misconception.
Someone might ask here, "What is the necessity of
Ṡivamastu Sarvajagatah? Keeping a sense of
friendship towards all living beings and avoiding
enmity towards any, isn't that sufficient? Doesn't this
sense of neutrality naturally arise within us? By
embracing this sense of neutrality, shouldn't we
remain engrossed in our welfare?"

229

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The answer to this is simple. The genuine sense of
neutrality lies only in the sentiment of welfare for all
living beings. Forgetting this fact, if we only engage in
our welfare - the welfare of our soul, then we might
fall into the flaw of 'selfishness' or 'neglect.' This
selfishness, combined with neutrality, can also lead to
aversion.
For example, suppose we live in a house with a
courtyard that extends to a neighbourhood square.
During the month of Ṡravana, a group of devoted
people gathers there every evening to sing hymns. If
we need to go out or come home at that time, and we
move around with an attitude of indifference instead
of having concern for their welfare, it won't take long
for us to develop resentment or aversion towards
those people, seeing them as obstacles in our way.
Let's consider the example of the elephant and the
hare, as well as an ant that is about to be crushed
underfoot. If we worry about our karma and keep one
foot in the air to avoid crushing the hare or the ant,
this thought may provoke frustration. However, if we
think about the suffering the hare and the ant would
endure and decide we should suffer instead, this
selfless thinking leads to the soul's development on
the right path.
This is a point that needs to be understood very
clearly on the path of spiritual development. That is
why an extremely thorough understanding is
necessary. Therefore, if we aspire to be happy and
wish to ascend the ladder of spiritual welfare, then
the aspiration for the welfare of all living beings is a

230

Development of the Soul

must. This is an extremely crucial step in the
progression of the soul's development
In this context, the information about the nine
principles advocated by Jain philosophers will prove
to be very fruitful and useful. The names of these nine
principles are as follows:
1. Jiva (living beings)
2. Ajiva (non-living things)
3. Punya (virtue)
4. Pāpa (sin)
5. Āṡrava (influx of karmic matter)
6. Samvara (stoppage of karmic matter)
7. Baṅdha (bondage)
8. Nirjarā (shedding of karmic matter)
9. Mokṡa (liberation)
Understanding the soul and its direct relationship
with these nine principles will open up a new world
before us; we will now examine these new principles
sequentially.
1. Jiva (living beings):
We have previously discussed nigoda and the beings
residing in nigoda. Considering that fact, it is accepted
that countless, innumerable, infinite beings have
existed since immemorial. Now, we will talk about the
beings that have emerged from nigoda and are
wandering in this world. A being is a soul that
possesses consciousness. Jain philosophers have
delineated two main distinctions of the soul.
231

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

1. Sansāri (Karma-bound)
2. Mukta (Liberated)
Sansāri souls are those bound by karma and
continuously wander in this world. Mukta refers to
souls that have become liberated by the exhaustion of
all karma and attained Mokṡa.
The main two distinctions among karma-bound souls
wandering in the world are 'sthāvara' (stationary)
and 'trasa' (mobile). The stationary souls are those
unable to move on their own, remaining stable
throughout a fixed lifespan, unable to make efforts to
achieve happiness or alleviate suffering; they are
termed as 'sthāvara.' In this category, there are beings
such as Prithvi kāya, Vanasapati kāya, Vāyu kāya, Jala
kāya, and Tejas kāya i.e. living beings that are
embodied by the earth, plants, air, water, and fire
respectively.
Sthāvara beings are of two types: sukṡma and sthula.
Sthula living beings are also called the 'Bādara Jiva'
according to Jain terminology. Sukṡma beings, cannot
be observed through countless microscopic eyes. But
Bādara (i.e. Sthula) living beings can be seen with the
naked eye. All these living things only experience
sensations through the sense of touch, since they are
uni-sensory beings; they do not possess other senses.
The souls capable of moving on their own are termed
as 'trasa jiva.' They possess two, three, four, or five
senses. Those with two senses, like skin and tongue,
are 'Beiṅdriya jiva.' Those with skin, tongue, and nose
are 'Teiṅdriya jiva.' Those with skin, tongue, nose, and
eyes are 'Cauriṅdriya jiva.' Those with skin, tongue,
nose, eyes, and ears are 'Paṅceṅdriya jiva.'
232

Development of the Soul

Paṅceṅdriya jiva is classified into four types:
1) Manuṣya (human)
2) Tiryaṅca (animals, birds, etc.)
3) Deva (divine soul residing in heaven)
4) Naraka (hellish beings)
The word 'jiva' is used for a being that moves through
various states, and experiences consciousness,
knowledge, and enlightenment. Whether called jiva,
cetana, or ātmā, fundamentally, it refers to the same
substance, expressing different names for the same
entity.
In the 4th chapter, we saw that there are six main
foundational substances in this world. Out of that, Jiva
is a living substance.
Sthāvara and trasa refer to the discussion about
karma-bound beings, i.e., those souls bound by
karma. Now, we would talk about Mukta jiva
(liberated beings), i.e., those who have attained
Mokṡa, in the last Mokṡa Tattva.
2. Ajíva (Non-Living Things):
All substances lacking life and consciousness are
identified by the term 'Ajíva.'
In the context of the six substances, we discussed
earlier, only the Jiva substance comes under Jiva
Tattva. The remaining five substances - Dharma,
Adharma, Pudgala, Ākāṡa, and Kāla - are Ajiva Tattva.
There is a relationship between the soul and these
five substances, and these six substances form the
universe. These six substances are the basis of the
233

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

occurrences in the universe. The world functions
through the combination of conscious and non-living
entities. In other words, the combination of jiva and
ajiva is what keeps the world moving. Jiva and ajíva's
combination is called sansāra.
3. Punya (Virtue) & 4. Pāpa (Sin):
In philosophical terms, what is stated above as
Dharma and Adharma is not Punya and Pāpa. These
two are substances. One that helps in the movement
of objects is called Dharma and one that helps in
maintaining stability is known as Adharma. When it
comes to practical usage, Punya and Pāpa can be
referred to as good conduct and bad conduct.
However, when we refer to the third and fourth
Tattva as Punya and Pāpa, we understand it as punya
karma and pāpa karma created by karma pudgal.
Punya karmas are to be understood as bringing about
the attainment of means of happiness, and Pāpa
karmas bring about the accumulation of materials for
suffering.
The discussion about the main eight types of karmas
in the context of karma is as follows:
First,
the
four
karmas,
'Jñānāvaraṇiya,
Darṡanāvaraṇiya, Mohaniya, and Antarāya,' have
negative consequences, and all of them are sinful
karmas. The last four karmas, 'Nāma, Āyuṣya, Gotra,
and Vedaniya,' are either virtuous or sinful. In other
words, each of them can be a good or a bad karma.
Physical happiness and suffering are based on
virtuous and sinful actions.

234

Development of the Soul

For the karma-bound soul, these virtues and sins in
the form of punya and pāpa represent paths in
opposite directions of progress and decline. The soul
resides in the middle, at the centre, and it embarks on
a journey towards progress or decline through the
paths of virtue or sin. Vice leads the soul towards
decline, while virtue initiates the soul's journey
towards liberation. From a philosophical perspective,
punya and pāpa are the causes of the soul's
wandering in this world. However, to acquire the
necessary resources for progressing towards
liberation, the soul needs to take refuge in punya
karmas. This marks the beginning of the journey.
5. Āṡrava (Influx):
In the discussion about punya and pāpa karmas, the
primary agent of earning punya and pāpa is the
mental activity of the soul. This mental activity
nourishes actions through speech and the body. The
activities performed through mind, speech, and body
establish a connection with the soul, and this
connection, influenced by the soul's disposition,
draws the flow of karmic particles into the soul. This
influx of karmic matter is known as Āṡrava. The cause
of Āṡrava i.e. actions through mind, speech and body
is also called Āṡrava.
The thoughts, whether good or bad, expressed
through speech, which may be beneficial or harmful,
and the actions performed through the body, whether
righteous or unrighteous, all contribute to the influx
of karmic particles into the soul. To explain it briefly,
Āṡrava refers to the entry of karmic particles into the
soul through mental, verbal, and physical activities.
235

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

This ā ṡrava has a direct connection with the
developmental stages of the soul. These
developmental stages are known as Guṇ asthānaka in
Jain philosophy. When the entry point for karmas
becomes small the development stage goes higher.
These are fourteen in number, and we will delve into
their discussion as we elaborate on these nine Tattva.
6. Samvara (Stoppage of karma):
Earlier, we mentioned that ā ṡrava is the entryway for
karma particles. Just as there is a door for entry, that
can also be closed to prevent entry. When these doors
are closed, it creates a barrier, stopping things from
entering. Similarly, the soul invites karma particles
inside through its actions of mind, speech, and body.
However, through pure and virtuous actions, the soul
can also prevent karma particles from entering. When
karma particles encounter closed doors and are
unable to enter, karma does not bind. This prevention
of the binding of karma and the actions that stop
karma particles from entering are both referred to as
'Samvara.'
In today's five-year plans, projects like the Bhakra
Nangal Dam are implemented to stop the flow of
water and store it in one place. The construction done
to halt this flow is called a dam. The water collected
in this manner is regulated to flow to the other side of
the dam through cement and iron gates built in the
middle of the dam. These gates, known as sluice gates,
can be opened and closed to varying degrees,
allowing water to pass through in controlled
amounts.

236

Development of the Soul

If we compare the soul to a dam for the reservoir of
karmic bonds, then the entry gate for karmic
particles, Āṡrava can be likened to the channels
leading to the dam. Similarly, the process of Samvara
(prevention of karmic influx) can be likened to
closing or restricting these entry gates. The degree to
which these gates are closed determines the amount
of karmic flow that can enter. If completely closed, the
karmic flow is entirely stopped. This task is
undertaken by the soul through its pure activities. As
the soul ascends the higher stages of spiritual
development, the ability to prevent karmic influx
increases. Conversely, as the soul increasingly
prevents the influx of karma, it rises higher in the
stages of Guṇ asthānaka, progressing further on the
path to liberation (Mokṡa).
7. Baṅdha (Bondage):
When karmic particles enter the realm of the soul
through Āṡrava in the absence of Samvara, they bind
with the soul, solidify, and adhere to it. This process,
called Baṅdha, determines the Prakruti (nature),
Sthiti (duration), Rasa (intensity), and Pradeṡa
(magnitude) of bondage. It is directly associated with
the entire realm of karma. The intense relationship
between karma and soul is explained in this category.
Previously, it was mentioned that beyond the primary
eight types of karma, there are 158 subcategories and countless divisions within them. The
world of karma is vast and filled with wonders.
Some karmas are like the gentle flow of a river i.e.
easily removable, some are like milk which makes
you feel a little oil, some are like castor oil, which
237

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

means thicker than milk, some are like mild thick
glue, some are like high-quality glue which doesn't
disintegrate, and some are like cement concrete
blocks that never crumble even after years.
When karma binds, its duration to remain with the
soul is also decided at that time only. When karma
binds with the soul, it does not give fruition (good or
bad consequence) immediately. It manifests as per
the rules and regulations, at the appropriate time.
When a duration is already determined at the time of
binding of karma except for Nikācita karma (karma
that cannot be altered), the soul can still alter it
through auspicious or inauspicious mental, verbal,
and physical activities. This fact is like the sweet
water flowing in a desert, or the twinkling of a star
amidst deep darkness.
This reality, elucidated by the constitution of karmic
bondage, gives constant enthusiasm to every soul
striving for liberation. Among the types of karmic
bondage, some are loose, some are moderate,
and some are deep, but the most intense, deeply
entrenched karmic bondage is termed Nikācita
Karma. This karma almost invariably leads to fruition,
no one can escape from it by any means. Rest other
karmas are such that, it can also be eliminated
through the strength of one's own sentiments and
efforts, without experiencing its consequences.
There are four types of these baṅdha, identified by the
terms:
i. Prakruti (nature)
ii. Sthiti (status)
238

Development of the Soul

iii. Rasa (intensity)
iv. Pradeṡa (magnitude)
When the karma Pudgala covers the knowledgerelated and other qualities of the soul, it is termed as
prakruti baṅdha. This nature conceals the soul's
qualities of knowledge, perception, and conduct by
covering the light of the soul. The eight primary types
of karma have been delineated from the eight
primary types of prakruti. When actions cover the
soul's qualities related to Jñāna (knowledge),
Darṡana (perception), and Cāritra (conduct), they are
termed Āvaraṇīya Karmas. These karmas remain
bound until the specified time or until changes occur
according to the soul's pursuits. When karma binds, it
remains bound within the boundaries of its specified
time, which is termed as Sthiti Baṅdha. The
magnitude or quantity of karmic particles that bind
the soul is termed as Pradeṡa Baṅdha. The intensity of
the impact during fruition of karmic particles that
binds the soul is termed as Rasa Baṅ dha.
The main causes of karmic bondage are categorized
into five types:
i. Mithyātva (State of False Belief)
ii. Avirati (Lack of Continence)
iii. Kaṣ āya (Passions)
iv. Pramāda (Inattentiveness)
v. Yoga (Mental, Verbal and Physical Activities)
The absence of self-awareness, lack of faith in
liberation, and disinterest in true knowledge,
perception, and conduct are referred to as the soul's
state of Mithyātva (state of false belief). The state of
Avirati (lack of continence) occurs when one does not
239

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

step back from committing sinful activities. The
passions such as Krodha (anger), Māna (Ego), Māyā
(deceit), Lobha (greed), Rāga (attachment), and
Dveṣa (aversion) are termed Kaṣāyas. Forgetting to
adhere to proper conduct or being inattentive in
performing virtuous deeds is termed Pramāda, and
the activities carried out by mind, speech, and body
are termed as Yoga. These are all the causes of
worldly existence.
To attain freedom from it, the soul closes the Āṡrava
through Samvara. This marks the initiation of the
soul's development where true knowledge dispels
falsehood. Righteous conduct through virtuous
actions and refraining from sins prevents the
continuity of karmic bondage. By relinquishing
attachment and desires, one achieves liberation from
kaṣāyas, and by being mindful of goals, duties, and
obligations, Pramāda is eradicated. Through the
auspicious utilization of Mana Yoga, Vacana Yoga and
Kāya Yoga, purity of the soul is attained. By
awakening inherent qualities, one can diligently
progress on the path to salvation. All these
endeavours are aimed at salvation and collectively
they constitute the process of karmic restraint known
as Samvara.
The constitution of karmic Pudgala, their formation,
and their dissolution, is a topic of immense depth and
wonder. Understanding the theory of karma fully
brings boundless joy and ultimate benefit. For
detailed insight, one must delve into Jain Literature.

240

Development of the Soul

8. Nirjarā (Shedding of karma):
In Baṅdha, we discussed about bonding of karma with
soul. In Samvara, we discussed stopping the bonding
of the karma and in Nirjarā, we would discuss how
one can get rid of this karma.
In the realm of karma, there exist two distinctions:
Sakāma Karma (Desire-driven actions) and Akāma
Karma (Desireless actions), and within these, there
are two types of Nirjarā: Sakāma Nirjarā and Akāma
Nirjarā.
Actions performed with desires are termed as
Sakāma Karma, while those performed effortlessly
are called Akāma Karma. Similarly, when the bound
karmas naturally come to fruition and are
experienced for a stipulated period, it is termed
Akāma Nirjarā, and when virtuous activities such as
austerity, recitation, fasting, observances, etc., are
employed to eradicate karmas, it is termed as Sakāma
Nirjarā.
There is a proverb, "Prevention is better than cure,"
which advises to address issues beforehand to avoid
complications. This applies to physical health, where
taking preventive measures before falling ill is better
than treating the illness later.
Similarly, actively engaging in actions to prevent
karmic bondage and then eliminating them through
Sakāma Nirjarā is a much more effective and
beneficial approach. The prevention of binding
karmas altogether through Samvara is a superior and
optimal path. If at all due to ignorance, there is a
bondage, then it is better to eradicate it quickly
241

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

through Sakāma Nirjarā instead of waiting for its
fruition. This is the subject matter of the internal
capacity of the soul and the most powerful means for
achieving liberation.
9. Mokṣa (Liberation):
The ninth and final element among the nine
principles is the Mokṣa Tattva or the principle of
liberation. Liberation is the name given to the state
where the soul, after shedding all karmas and
achieving the ultimate state of bliss and happiness,
transcends the cycle of birth and death and becomes
the enjoyer of infinite bliss.
The soul attains this liberation by reaching the
highest stage of development, where all the eight
types of karmas are destroyed. Firstly, karmas of the
first four categories i.e. Ghāti karma are destroyed
and the soul becomes omniscient. Then the karmas of
the next four categories i.e. Aghāti Karmas are
eradicated, leading to the attainment of Siddhahood,
i.e., Mokṣa. It is freed from the gravitational pull of the
material world and achieves the pinnacle of eternal
stability.
Among human pursuits of enjoyment and indulgence,
women are always in the foremost position.
Considering this nature of the soul, certain spiritual
leaders, and saints recognize liberation as the
ultimate goal of the soul's journey and refer to it as
the beloved one. In certain traditions, such as Sufism
in West Asia, liberation is known as the dear one.
In our tradition, among the followers of Krishna,
there are sects like Rādhāswāmi and Ṡri Rādhe that
242

Development of the Soul

worship Krishna's consort, Rādhā. Recognizing the
nature of men to make immense efforts to attain their
desired beloved, the founders of some such sects have
depicted liberation as "beloved." Various founders of
different spiritual paths have represented this
sentiment as something very dear.
In Jainism too, the ultimate liberation of the soul is
referred to by names like Ṡivaramaṇi and Mukti. The
worshipful goddess for each Jain soul is indeed this
liberation. Similarly, every human's worshipful
goddess should also be this liberation. The more
intense the love for the worshipful goddess Mukti
Ramaṇi, the greater the effort to attain it.
In the absence of the causes of karmic bondage
(samvara) and through the shedding of karma
(Nirjarā), liberation is attained. In Mokṣa, when the
soul departs and attains its rightful place, there are no
associations with the body, senses, or any other
causes of karmic bondage. It is pure blissful
consciousness, which resides in the purest form.
The great souls are classified into two categories:
Siddhas and Tīrthaṅkaras (Arihaṅta). Siddha achieve
liberation by completely ending all karmas, while
Arihaṅta first eradicates the four most harmful
karmas, attain omniscience, and then becomes
Tīrthaṅkara. Before attaining liberation by the
destruction of the remaining four injurious karmas
and entering Mokṣa, they first spend some time in this
world, providing extraordinary benefits by showing
the true path to the beings of this universe.
By paying homage to such great benefactors, the
revered Tīrthaṅkaras, who attained liberation
243

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

through the stages of spiritual development, and who
guided us by showing the path, let us now briefly
understand the fourteen stages of spiritual
development.
(1) Mithyātva Guṇasthānaka (Stage of False
Belief):
This is the initial stage of the soul's spiritual
development. Those who have not yet attained
Samyagdarṡana (right faith), those who do not
understand the truth of spiritual welfare, those who
do not know the right means related to it, and those
who are engulfed in various forms of ignorance and
delusions fall into this category. This stage is common
to all souls' qualities. Even ascetics and saints who are
revered in the public eye may fall into this category.
Even those progressing in understanding of the soul,
karma, dharma (religion), and mokṣa (liberation) can
get stuck at this stage. For example, someone who
worships a goddess performs rituals, or engages in
violent behavior may still be labeled as a devotee, yet
they remain in this initial stage. Similarly, great
scholars, practitioners, ascetics, and wealthy
benefactors, if not on the true path, also fall into this
category. Generally speaking, except for a few
fortunate individuals, almost everyone today is at this
primary stage.
At this stage, possessing virtues is also our primary
accomplishment. We have come here on the first
category after acquiring the quality of friendship
which falls under Mitrā Dṛṣti.

244

Development of the Soul

8 dṛṣti (visions) indicate the clarity of vision of a soul.
Under the first dṛṣti viz Mitrā, a soul attains
tenderness, non-hostility towards principles,
compassion, non-harming, truthfulness, and other
benevolent practices. These primary virtues,
manifested through Mitrā Dṛṣti, lead the soul to
engage in further actions. However, until the
attainment of samyag darṡana (right vision), where
the soul is not obstructed by wrong vision or
delusion, it remains in this initial stage and continues
to engage in actions filled with various delusions and
anxieties. In the material realm, it continues to
acquire various achievements and utilizes the virtues
attained through Mitrā Dṛṣti. But until it attains
samyag darṡana, it neither progresses nor moves
away from this first stage of virtue. We can term this
initial stage of virtue as a Perplexity house.
To progress beyond this primary stage of virtue,
Samyaktva (right vision) is a means and for this, the
knowledge of Anekāntavāda and Syādvāda is very
useful.
(2) Sāswādana Guṇasthānaka (Stage of Tasting
Virtues):
If one attains Samyag Darṡana, then the soul's journey
of development from the first to the fourth stage of
Guṇ asthānaka begins. However, this developmental
journey sometimes stalls due to the uphill climb
towards the peak of the mountain. Hence, it may get
stuck and slide down a bit, leading to the emergence
of the second Guṇ asthānaka. The reason for this is
intense attachment and aversion. This stage is

245

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

achieved only when you fall from the fourth
Guṇ asthānaka, and it remains for a very short period.
A person acquires a beautiful, delicate object, but due
to emotions like anger and attachment, they not only
break the object but also throw it away. Similarly,
when one receives the beautiful path of samyag
darṡana (right vision), but with the rise of
anantānubaṅdhi kaṣāyas (intense passion), they
become lax, lethargic, and move in the wrong
direction, eventually falling back to mithyātva (false
belief). The situation is unstable at this stage, and the
soul does not stay here for long. However, since this
soul already possesses samyag darṡana, it eventually
becomes vigilant again.
We can identify this stage of virtues as a bent house.
To move forward from this stage, the awakening of
samyag dṛṣti (right vision) is necessary. However, due
to the potential pitfall of non-faithfulness, it is not
possible to advance at this stage. This state remains
only for a short time and then falls back to the first
stage of Guṇ asthānaka. If the individual can exert
effort and vigorously strive to overcome nonfaithfulness, they can advance to the next level
without falling back. However, if they become
entangled in attachment and aversion with mithyā
bhāva (false belief), they will remain stable only at the
first Guṇ asthānaka.
(3) Miṡra Guṇasthānaka (Mixed Stage of Virtue):
Despite being one level higher than the first two, this
stage is a little strange. Swinging between Samyaktva

246

Development of the Soul

and Mithyātva, it is a mixed state of a soul in its
development process.
There is neither liking nor disliking for the principles;
it is the enterprise that demonstrates this mix of
Samyaktva and Mithyātva.
We can recognize this stage of virtue as the
indifference
house.
Liberation
from
this
contemplation leads to the upward journey of
spiritual development, a means of realizing
discrimination or understanding the essence of right
and wrong. During this period, when the intellect
truly uses discrimination, the soul is saved from
falling back.
Scriptures explain the concept of mixed feelings here
with the example of a person stranded on an island of
coconuts. He does not know what food is, so he
neither likes nor dislikes it. Similarly, this state is
indifferent to likes and dislikes towards the principles
of right and wrong. Therefore, there is neither
attraction nor aversion toward right vision. Here,
one's taste is indifferent to liking or disliking samyag
tattva.
(4) Avirati Samyag dṛṣti Guṇasthānaka (Stage of
Right Vision with No Conduct):
When the right vision is attained, the view towards
the path of truth also becomes stable. However, due
to the allure of physical pleasures, one may not be
free from sinful conduct and may continue to engage
in selfish actions. This stage is called avirati samyag
dṛṣti (right vision with no conduct).

247

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

At this stage, although internally the soul has the right
vision, externally, it still engages in sinful conduct like
mithyā dṛṣti (a person with wrong vision). However,
the difference between Samyaktva and Mithyātva
persists here.
Mithyā dṛṣti soul's focus is on worldly pleasures and
self-interest. They not only engage in sinful activities
but also applaud and take pride in them for their
achievements. In contrast, samyag dṛṣti soul may still
indulge in sinful actions due to attachment to worldly
pleasures, but they feel remorse and strive to refrain
from such behaviour. They understand the difference
between righteousness and sin. Even when engaging
in certain actions, if the soul perceives the distinction
between right and wrong and refrains from unworthy
deeds, it progresses towards the next stage.
The importance of right vision and wrong vision
differs significantly in these actions. Samyag dṛṣti
signifies the manifestation of purity within the soul,
while mithyā dṛṣti may be doing righteous deeds but
their status becomes tāmasika.
Because of the attainment of decisive stability on
the right vision, the soul is vivacious and burning with
enthusiasm to progress. The state of being detached
from sinful actions is named Virata (detached), while
the state of not being detached from sinful conduct is
termed Avirata. These are the technical terms.
In this stage of virtue, despite the presence of right
vision, one remains avirata (not inclined towards
ethical behavior), leading to what is termed Avirati
Samyag Dṛṣti . This stage, known as the Abode of
248

Development of the Soul

Development, serves as the fundamental basis for
spiritual advancement.
The means to progress further from this stage is the
practice of Aṇuvrata (small vows). Dedication to and
memorization of the Jain Namaskā ra maṅtra are
highly beneficial here. The fourth Guṇ asthānaka is the
stage that all souls in their progressive development
aspire to reach, representing a definite aspirational
stage for the soul's developmental journey.
(5)
Deṡavirati
Guṇasthānaka
Renunciation Stage of Virtue):

(Partial

Jain philosophers have outlined two essential means
for the development of the soul: the Deṡavirati and
the Sarvavirati paths. The Deṡavirati path is tailored
for householders living in the world, offering some
flexibility compared to the stricter rules of the
Sarvavirati path pursued by ascetics. Deṡavirati
entails the observance of 12 Vows (5 Aṇuvratas, 3
Guṇavratas, and 4 Ṡikṣāvratas), along with the nonkilling of Traskāya Jiva (moving creatures). While
adherents to this path may not completely refrain
from sinful activities, they gradually incline towards
non-sinful actions. To gain a comprehensive
understanding of Deṡavirati and Sarvavirati, one
should seek knowledge through the teachings of
Tīrthaṅkaras regarding Ṡrāvaka Dharma and Sādhu
Dharma.
In this fifth stage of virtue, even though the soul which
is far away from Vītarā gatā (dispassion), experiences
very little mental detachment from desires. Through
this perspective, the soul experiences a blissful state
and becomes an aspirant for the soul's developmental
249

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

journey. By adhering to the principles of the
Deṡavirati path, the soul becomes interested and
desirous for the path of sarvavirati , ultimately
becoming an ardent aspirant for a specific kind of
elevation.
We can identify this stage as an Advancement house.
In the journey beyond the fifth stage of spiritual
development lies the path of devotion to the
Sarvavirati dharma.
(6) Pramatta Guṇasthānaka:
As one progresses in the worship of the Deṡavirati
dharma, the soul eventually reaches the state of
becoming a monk known as Sarvavirati. This
attainment marks the sixth stage of spiritual
development, where the soul adheres to strict vows
renouncing violence and falsehood in thought,
speech, and action throughout life. However, even at
this elevated stage, moments of negligence, laziness,
forgetfulness, or similar distractions may occur,
leading to occasional deviations from true selfawareness and equanimity. While minor kaṣāyas
(passions) are not considered significant at this stage,
occasional lapses in self-awareness are termed as
Pramāda.
Even in the life of a saint, occasional lapses due to
habitual tendencies of previous birth cycles are
natural. Hence, this stage is termed as Pramatta
Guṇasthānaka. At this stage, he cultivates selfawareness through personal discipline and guidance
from a mentor to progress towards liberation.
However, this is a delicate situation, and he may
250

Development of the Soul

revert to the state of Pramatta Guṇasthānaka despite
efforts. While there are possibilities to advance
beyond this stage to Apramatta Guṇasthānaka, only
great devotees can achieve this feat. Nonetheless, the
devotee at the stage of Pramatta Guṇasthānaka
remains continuously engrossed in the right vision,
knowledge, and conduct. We call this Guṇasthānaka
as the relaxation house. The means to progress from
a relaxation house to the next level is to become more
vigilant and renounce negligence.
Among all the institutions of saints in the world, the
Jain monks' institution is undoubtedly regarded as
the best. In daily life, they also follow a high-level
discipline and penance. There is no such disciplined
institution in the world that can be compared to this
Jain monks' institution.
(7) Apramatta Guṇasthānaka:
By diligently renouncing negligence and embracing
vigilance, the soul advances to the seventh stage
known as the Apramatta Guṇasthānaka.
Once one reaches these stages of spiritual
development, stability may not be guaranteed.
Sometimes, there might be swift progress, while at
other times, due to frequent moments of negligence
(Pramāda), they may descend to the stage of the
relaxation house. Again, by embracing duty-oriented
activities, they can reach the stage of Apramāda. Amid
the tug between negligence and vigilance (Pramāda
and Apramāda), when the practitioner fortifies
themselves against negligence and experiences
unprecedented enthusiasm, paving the way for
further progress, then the path ahead opens up from
251

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

there. In this stage, diligent efforts and heightened
vigilance towards self-discipline i.e. Sanyam Yoga is
required. Hence, we will call this stage as Yoga house.
(8) Apūrvakaraṇa Guṇasthānaka:
While remaining Apramatta (vigilant), the
practitioner meticulously adheres to exemplary
conduct, abolishing or pacifying Cāritra Mohaniya
karmas. Here, they attain an extraordinary state i.e.,
Adhyavasāya previously unattainable. This unique
fervour of spiritual awakening distinguishes this
stage. The practitioner creates unprecedented
transformations in the status of karma, performing
five unprecedented tasks for the kṣaya (eradication)
and upaṡama (pacification) of karma. Hence, this
stage is known as Apūrvakaraṇa Guṇasthānaka in Jain
Āgama.
If we were to identify this stage, we would call it the
unprecedented house.
(9) Anivrittikaraṇa Guṇasthānaka:
The practitioner's experience while eradicating or
pacifying Cāritra Mohaniya karma is indescribable,
leading them to the ninth Guṇasthānaka and enabling
them to experience the pristine fruits of Apūrvakarṇ a
unhindered. We could aptly identify this stage as the
Experience house, where the initiation of dispassion
begins.
(10) Sukṣma Samparāya Guṇasthānaka:
When Cāritra mohaniya karma is eradicated or
pacified, only a subtle aspect of lobha (greed)
remains, marking the
Sukṣma Samparāya
Guṇasthānaka. Continuing to nurture vigilant effort,
252

Development of the Soul

the practitioner gradually becomes firmly established
in the almost absolute realization of the ultimate
spiritual goal, the state of supreme being.
(11) Upaṡāntamoha Guṇasthānaka:
This is a specific type of stage of spiritual
development. From the seventh to the tenth
Guṇasthānaka, the practitioner is endowed with
subtle attachments and aversions. In the eighth stage,
after the Apūrvakaraṇ a is over; then, in the ninth and
tenth stages, the practitioner engages in the
disposition of Upaṡama or kṣaya. If the action of
Upaṡama has been initiated, then it gets completed at
the tenth Guṇasthānaka and, the soul attains this
stage. Here, all attachments are completely pacified,
so it is termed as Upaṡanta Moha.
Let's grasp the meanings of Upaṡama and Kṣaya.
Upaṡama is akin to calming down a disease through
external treatments, while Kṣaya involves eradicating
the root cause of the ailment. Consider when a doctor
prescribes medication for temporary relief from pain,
it helps alleviate symptoms but doesn't fully
eliminate the underlying issue. This temporary relief
is akin to Upaṡama. However, to prevent the disease
from recurring, merely temporary treatment won't
suffice; the root cause needs to be addressed for a
lasting cure.
To quell a fire, there are two methods. One involves
covering it with ashes, gradually smothering the
flames; although subdued, the fire may still reignite if
stirred by the wind or other factors. This approach is
known as Upaṡama. In Upaṡama, the potential for the
fire to flare up again remains concealed, similar to the
253

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

possibility of karma recurring after it has been
pacified. Instead, if water is used to extinguish the
fire, then it does not reignite afterward. Hence, what
results from this method is not Upaṡama but Kṣaya.
Similarly, when actions are taken to eradicate
karmas, they do not flare up again in the future.
Therefore, if the practitioner has achieved such
unprecedented transformations i.e., the eradication
of karmas (Kṣaya), then there is no need to worry.
However, if they have pacified (Upaṡama) associated
karmas, there is a risk of falling to a lower
Guṇasthānaka at this stage. In this eleventh stage,
since the likelihood of falling to a lower stage is
certain, we can name it as the slipping house.
Generally, practitioners do not prefer to enter this
stage of spiritual development right away; instead,
they prefer to first eradicate the karma, skip this stage
and move forward to the next stage of spiritual
development.
(12) Kṣíṇa Guṇasthānaka:
The stage after the eradication of Cāritra Mohaniya
karma is known as the Kṣíṇ amoha Guṇasthānaka. In
this stage of spiritual development, the devotee
attains ṡukla dhyāna (supreme meditation). They
eradicate Jñānāvaraṇiya, darṡanāvarṇiya, and
antarāya
karma,
and
become
omniscient
(kevalajñāni). This twelfth and the eleventh
Guṇasthānaka are almost equivalent. However, the
difference lies in the stability of equanimity and
detachment; in the eleventh, there is no stability,
whereas in this Guṇasthānaka, there is stability.

254

Development of the Soul

Therefore, if we recognize this stage by the name of
Dispassion House, then it will be proper.
(13) Sayogí kevalī Guṇasthānaka:
After crossing the twelfth Guṇasthānaka, the devotee
becomes omniscient and attains kevalajñāna.
However, in this state, because of the existence of
activities (yoga) it is called Sayoga kevalī
Guṇasthānaka. The term Sayoga is used because
certain karmas, known as aghāti karma, are still
attached to the soul, remaining until physical
activities cease. Since, the physical activities such as
speaking and moving still persist, this stage of
spiritual development is termed Sayoga kevalī. In the
enlightening sun of omniscience, the practitioner can
visualize all the substances of the past, present, and
future of Loka and Aloka (the entire universe). If we
recognize the twelfth Guṇasthānaka by the name of
Eternal Knowledge house, then it will be appropriate.
(14) Ayogí kevalī Guṇasthānaka:
When the lifespan of the Sayoga kevalī comes to an
end at the thirteenth Guṇasthānaka, the soul becomes
completely detached from all bodily activities, it
becomes Ayogi kevalī. After all karmas are destroyed,
the Supreme Soul attains its final destination,
achieving complete liberation and reaching the end of
the upper realms of the universe. This marks the
culmination of the soul's journey, which begins from
the initial stage of Mithyātva Guṇasthānaka and
progresses to the ultimate state of realization in the
fourteenth stage of spiritual development. This final
and highest stage is the Perfection house and it
255

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

signifies the attainment of completeness and
fulfillment.
Reaching this state of perfection involves a rapid and
forceful culmination of the soul's pursuit of its
ultimate purpose. It unfolds like a divine drama,
beginning from the seventh Guṇasthānaka. In this
grand cycle of actions, there is a great battle against
the allure of worldly desires, and the solitary seeker
finds themselves engaged in an unprecedented
struggle. Like a great hero in an extraordinary saga,
the narrative of this journey leaves us amazed and
astonished. It inspires us to accelerate our pursuit
and aspire to assume the role of the hero in this divine
drama.
When will this extraordinary opportunity come for
us?

256

CHAPTER 18

Life is a hassle
Don't get angry by reading the headline. It's true, life
can be both a hassle and not at the same time—it all
depends on how we perceive it. If we choose to
complicate it, then indeed, it becomes complicated.
However, if we simplify things, life doesn't have to be
so complex. Regarding life, its purpose, and the way
of living, you will find various and diverse opinions.
If you meet a typical ascetic, he will say: "Life is a big
trouble. There is nothing good in the world. Leave it,
brother, leave this world and become an ascetic, and
work for the salvation of the soul!"
If you meet a public leader, he will say: "Engage in
activities that bring welfare to society and the
country. Sacrifice, give, serve humanity, the
distressed, and the poor."
Taking these two opinions, if you meet your father, he
will say: "Leave all that trouble, focus on earning,
that's the job of the rich and the poor. First of all, we
have to take care of our own home. Look here, don't
get into all that trouble!"
Then, if you are married and ask your wife, what will
she say? She will probably say something like this:
"What did Mrs. Gunvanti's husband have before? Her
husband was just empty-handed before. Today they
have a bungalow, a car, and servants; everyone has
gold, but they have such beautiful diamond and pearl
257

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

jewelry! Seeing it, I get tears in my eyes. The pride of
Mrs. Gunvanti is now boundless. Do something so I
can surpass her!"
Before continuing this, I remember a story about a
nobleman and a labourer's family from England. The
story is very interesting and meaningful, so I feel like
presenting it here.
Two friends, Fannie and Lucy, were inseparable.
Fannie married a wealthy person, while Lucy married
a laborer. Fannie enjoyed a vast collection of exquisite
diamond jewelry, whereas Lucy used to wear
imitation and fake jewelry.
Pretty much like Mrs. Gunwanti, Frannie takes pride
in flaunting her aristocratic status, often organizing
lavish parties in her grand mansion. Inviting her
friends over, she revels in showcasing her opulence,
basking in the admiration and envy of her peers. With
each event, Franny feels triumphant, relishing the
glory of being wealthy.
Once, during such an extravagant gathering, she
posed a question to Lucy, her friend from the
labourer's family, seated amidst them in mismatched
adornments. With a sparkle from her diamond, ruby,
and sapphire-studded jewels, Franny inquired, "My
dear Lucy, how do you clean your jewellery? I use
special solutions imported from Paris, Venice, and
even New York."
Franny's intention was to embarrass Lucy, but her
response was unexpectedly clever. With a smile, Lucy
retorted, "Oh, my dear Franny, I don't bother with

258

Life is a hassle

cleaning. When they lose their luster, I simply discard
them! I just throw them out!"
This witty comeback not only showcased Lucy's
intelligence and wit but also held a deeper meaning.
It reflected her contentment with simplicity, a stark
contrast to Franny's relentless pursuit of validation
through extravagance.
Despite being the wife of a wealthy millionaire, she
seeks solace in elaborate gatherings and deceiving
others, unaware that true fulfillment lies beyond
material possessions.
Lucy lacks wealth, and her husband is a common
labourer. However, she finds contentment in her
situation. It illustrates that one can find fulfillment
even in simplicity; sometimes, imitation jewellery can
be worn with more pride and dignity than genuine
riches.
This prompts reflection on the essence of satisfaction.
Rather than lamenting what one lacks, true happiness
stems from utilizing what one has for good. Such
contentment becomes a vital tool for a fulfilling life.
Life's twists and turns, influenced by circumstances,
karma, and more, dictate one's emotional state,
whether joy or sorrow, idleness or industriousness,
all contingent upon one's mindset. Amidst this, a
profound understanding of Syādvāda stands as a
potent remedy for calming the mind.
Living joyfully by embracing the inherent essence of
the world, amidst its perceived emptiness, offers an
unparalleled path for worldly souls. Syādvāda

259

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

provides a true vision of both worthfullness and
worthlessness of the world.
I have seen some worldly great men, distressed by
sorrow, say "The world is futile." I have also heard a
few solitary gentlemen say, "There is no happiness
left in this world; apart from embracing renunciation,
there is no other path left for us."
In such moments, the verses of Pujya
Haribhadrasū riṡwarji Mahārāja come to mind.

Ṡri

Pujya Ṡri Haribhadrasū riṡwarji Mahārāja delineates
that there are three types of vairagya (detachment):
• Embodiment of Pain
• Embodiment of Moha (biased attitude)
• Embodiment of Knowledge
Suffering, agitation, and anger are the root of
'ārtadhyāna' (a reflection born out of pain) When the
unbearable feeling arises from separation from
desired things and the conjunction with undesired
things, the resulting detachment is considered
embodiment of pain. In such detachment, where
ārtadhyāna is predominant, it is a cause of agitation,
filled with sorrow, and leads to self-destructive
tendencies. It is only from a worldly perspective that
this is called detachment.
In the second type, moha does not mean worldly
attachment but refers to a one-sided, deluded
perspective. Jain philosophers believe that even the
true detachment born from such a one-sided
perspective is misleading and that only detachment
accompanied by enlightened knowledge is worthy of
acceptance. Enlightened detachment refers to
260

Life is a hassle

detachment that follows the principles
Anekāntavāda and contains pure knowledge.

of

Ṡri Haribhadrasū riṡwarji Mahārāja has explained
what is meant by detachment that embodies moha
and that embodies knowledge. He has stated:
"Even when a person repeatedly sees the
worthlessness of the world through a unilateral
determination such as 'the soul is only one,' 'the soul
is eternal only,' 'the soul is bound only,' 'the soul is
momentary only,' or 'the soul is non-existent only,'
and practices restraint and virtuous conduct to
renounce it, such a person's detachment does not
embody knowledge but moha."
True enlightened detachment belongs only to those
who, relying on the understanding of Syādvāda, view
the soul as collectively one in consciousness but
individually many in consciousness, as eternal in
substance but momentary in modes, as unbound from
the absolute perspective but bound from the practical
perspective, and as non-existent from other's
perspective but existent from its own perspective,
and who accept both aspects as they are. They
recognize that in the worldly state, their soul is
subjected to external physical karma and influenced
by desires and other passions, causing them to
wander in the dreadful cycle of birth and death. To
free their soul from this condition, they properly
renounce the world. Their detachment is indeed
enlightened and becomes the unique means of
achieving perfection.
Now, consider the present-day ascetics, monks,
saints, sages, and others. How many of them have
261

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

renounced the world inspired by detachment based
on knowledge?
The intention here is not to criticize. There is no
doubt that the path of renunciation is excellent.
Seeing any renunciate or ascetic automatically makes
one bow their head in respect. The selflessness and
spiritual quest of Jain monks, who follow the path of
complete renunciation, and Vedantic ascetics, who
practice profound meditation on the oneness of the
soul with the entire universe, have always inspired
reverence and respect. The strict discipline followed
by Jain monks has astonished wise people worldwide.
Anyone who studies the conduct, daily routine, and
numerous restrictions with the scientific logic that
Jain monks observe will undoubtedly come to believe
that it is the supreme ascetic order in the world.
The purpose of mentioning this here is to highlight
that merely changing one's clothes due to an inability
to face the difficulties in the world doesn't serve any
real purpose. The path of asceticism is extremely
arduous. For those who become frightened by the
hardships of worldly life, it is worth questioning how
they can achieve progress by becoming ascetics.
Especially, friends who wish to follow the path of
renunciation should thoroughly analyze their inner
tendencies and the reasons behind their detachment
before embarking on this path.
For the most part, the belief that life is a hassle stems
