from an inability to face the turmoil and difficulties of
the world. This is due to a lack of strength and
steadiness. The inability to fulfill one's duties, the
challenges that interfere with daily worship, and the
262

Life is a hassle

mental strain caused by all these issues lead us to
thoughts in a different direction. This mindset often
includes an inclination to escape. If this is the case,
then it is not a virtue; it is a weakness of the soul.
However, if one turns to the path of renunciation
solely with the desire to live a completely sin-free life
in this human existence, then it is a true state of
knowledge.
Some people have grand dreams of achieving
material success in life, but they cannot fulfill these
dreams. They do not get what they want, and what
they do get, they do not like. It is no wonder that such
people find life to be a hassle.
To achieve success in life, the first important step is
to determine your position, circumstances, and the
limits of your abilities. After considering all these
factors, you should assess how far the available
resources and the limited availability of your strength
can take you. The creation of our dreams should be
within these boundaries.
A ticket from Ahmedabad to Mumbai cannot be
purchased with a five-rupee note. Similarly, our
dreams do not come to fruition without the necessary
resources and capabilities. When we create dreams,
the joy we derive from envisioning their fulfillment
proves to be an illusion if we cannot achieve the
desired goal. When we fail to reach our desired
objectives, our initial joy transforms into boundless
sorrow. Our actual dreams become the cause of our
unlimited suffering.
Therefore, when determining the goal of life, the first
thing we should establish is our own abilities and the
263

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

limitations of our resources. If there is no harmony
between our desires and our capabilities, there is no
need to be disheartened. In such circumstances, we
can determine two things.
Firstly, we should limit our goals to match our
capabilities. Secondly, we should strive to enhance
our abilities. As our capabilities increase, we can
gradually expand our goals. By adopting this
approach, if we determine the goal of our life and the
path to achieve it, life will not seem like a series of
troubles but will become a paradise of supreme joy
and delight. In shaping our life this way, Syādvāda can
be of great help to us. The essence of the word 'Syāt'
makes us aware of all the aspects of our life, our
strengths, and our limitations and shows us the
appropriate path to progress gradually. This is the
strength inherent in Syādvāda.
When suffering comes into life, we should not become
cowards and sit crying. First and foremost, we should
examine how many other joys we have in life despite
the suffering. This investigation will surely provide us
with strong reassurance. Next, we should use our
discernment and wisdom to understand what the
suffering is, its nature, its cause, and the remedy for
it. Thinking about the joys amidst the suffering will
calm and stabilize our minds. With a peaceful mind,
we can employ our discernment and begin to see
clearly that the shadow of the suffering can indeed be
dispelled. Joy stands right behind the suffering. By
contemplating in this manner, we will undoubtedly
understand the causes of the suffering. Then, we can
enthusiastically make efforts to alleviate it. All this
264

Life is a hassle

understanding and way of thinking can certainly be
achieved through Syādvāda.
Similarly, when extreme happiness comes into life,
we should also contemplate where this happiness has
come from, its source, its cause, how long its duration
will be, and what lies between its beginning and end.
Sometimes doubts arise as to why all this has
happened. It is necessary for us to contemplate all
these matters. This contemplation will lead us to two
possible conclusions.
Firstly, instead of blindly enjoying the found
happiness, we can enjoy it discriminately. Secondly,
after this happiness subsides, we can contemplate
how to defer sorrow or how to prevent it. We can
prepare our minds to endure the suffering that will
come after this happiness, or we can laugh and enjoy
it to its fullest. Furthermore, the attitude of sharing
our happiness with others is of the greatest benefit
that will be born within us. In this way, the path of
contemplation will also be rooted in us through
Syādvāda.
Through interactions with family members, friends,
and acquaintances, there are times when we
experience joy and also times when we feel sorrow.
At such times, we examine the differences between
our understanding and theirs, search for the causes of
these situations, and contemplate solutions to them.
Thus, we become liberated from mental anguish
arising from differences in perspectives, and the path
of such contemplation is also shown to us through
Syādvāda.

265

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The established meaning of the doctrine of Syādvāda
is that any object or subject can have more than one
aspect. The central point of Syādvāda's teaching is
that when contemplating any subject, one should not
be obstructed by seeing only one side of it.
In this way, if we use the principle of Syādvāda
thoughtfully, we will understand that all the
difficulties and troubles in life, and conversely, the
joys and pleasures that we experience, stem from
within us. These feelings arise from within us, not
from external sources. It is in our own hands to
generate the feelings according to our desires. This
understanding also elucidates the principle of
Syādvāda for us.
Following the principle of Syādvāda, as we gradually
gain control over our own reactions, both joy, and
sorrow, two seemingly opposing emotions, will
gradually come under our control. Feelings of
equality, empathy, and tolerance will emerge within
us. Slowly releasing ourselves from the grip of
transient happiness and sorrow, we can become
recipients of boundless joy. It is through this joy that
we will find the noble sentiment of wishing well for
ourselves and others. Without Syādvāda, such a state
of ultimate welfare cannot be achieved under any
circumstances.
The doctrine of Syādvāda does not teach us to tolerate
or condone injustice or unfairness. Wherever
necessary, we should not refrain from fighting against
it. However, in situations where we find ourselves
facing injustice or unfairness, it is often due to our
own perceptions being clouded by selfishness and
266

Life is a hassle

attachment. This clarity of thought is precisely what
Syādvāda aims to impart to us. Later, when the need
arises for a just fight, then, Syādvāda will guide us
towards adopting a method that is just, rational, and
empathetic. Through this approach, success and
victory are assured for us.
The complexities of karmas and the impurities that
affect the soul prevent us from embracing all the
virtues present in the world. When there is proximity
to noble thoughts and understanding, even though
wrong thoughts and unworthy deeds are committed
by us, not by choice but due to mere circumstantial
influence, we end up committing evil acts. This
condition is common to the entire world.
Similarly, some righteous deeds may happen against
our will, and we may perform all actions without true
understanding. Exiting the temple after seeing the
Tīrthaṅkara's idol, sometimes we give alms to the
beggar sitting on either side of the path. In some
cases, we may also scold a beggar encountered on the
street. Such instances are quite common.
We do not always have clear reasoning behind all our
actions. Habit, tradition, nature, circumstances, and
connections often influence how we behave. We do
not always consciously evaluate whether what we
have done is right or wrong, true or false. When we
encounter difficulty in contemplating a particular
action, we hesitate to think because we are stuck in
deciding how to proceed. Such hesitation often leads
to regret for the wrongdoing we have committed.
Mostly, remorse arises after the event has taken
place, making us ponder over how to handle the
267

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

situation that has arisen, how to escape from it, and
why it happened in the first place. We contemplate
these same issues. It is a matter of concern that we
engage in such contemplation, whether these
thoughts result in a way to escape from the
consequences of wrongdoing, or we simply end up
reverting to the same wrongdoings out of
desperation to escape. We choose paths to remove
the burdens fallen into our hands, and at the end of
these paths, the burdens that have fallen from our
hands back onto our shoulders. We were not aware of
this at all. All of this is apparent in this world.
If we find ourselves stuck in a difficult situation, and
someone randomly asks, "Hey Chandu, what did you
do?" At that moment, Chandu might say, "No, I didn't
do anything. The situation was like that, the
circumstances were such, he did this, he did that. Mr.
X was responsible for all this blunder, this and that
happened." After discussing many such points,
Chandu might then say, "For all these reasons, I am
not responsible." What Chandu talks about, he does
alone, is not the case here. For the most part, people
try to shift the responsibility onto others. The main
reason for many of the troubles experienced in life is
that we do not have a clear understanding of life, its
purpose, its goals, and the methods of living. If this
understanding comes to us, then life transcends mere
struggles. If we attain this knowledge, then life
becomes a paradise. This is something the Jain
philosophers have beautifully elucidated, and
understanding it can be quite enriching.
With the help of our 5 senses and the human brain,
we function properly. A body cannot be called 'intact'
268

Life is a hassle

if any of these senses do not work. These five are the
main organs of the body. Similarly, fives principles
have been shown for worldly beings:
i. Ahiṅsā (Non-violence)
ii. Satya (Truth)
iii. Asteya (Non-stealing)
iv. Brahmacarya (Chastity)
v. Aparigraha (Non-possession)
These five principles can be elaborated upon in five
separate texts, and these great, meaningful, and
supremely benevolent principles can bring ultimate
well-being.
These five principles are the true path to living a
righteous life. Just like when we sit in a vehicle, we
drive on the road. If we drive properly on the road,
there are very low chances of an accident; similarly,
when we walk on the road of these five principles to
live our lives, we do not encounter unnecessary
troubles. Once we step out, various obstacles emerge.
The meaning of the word ‘Ahiṅsā’ is not just refraining
from physical violence towards humans but also
refraining from harming any living being. If anyone
experiences suffering through our thoughts, words,
or actions, it is considered violence. Hurting or
causing pain to any gross or subtle living being is
violence. But even if someone's mind feels even a
slight discomfort, all actions, whether done through
the body, speech, or mind, become violent. Regarding
karma, it encompasses the threefold aspects of mind,
speech, and action.

269

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The word Ahiṅsā has become a universally
understood concept today. The first 23 Tīrthaṅkaras
and the 24th Tīrthaṅkara, Mahāvira Swāmi and
Buddha, emphasized the importance of Ahiṅsā for
political governance and societal life, making it a
paramount principle. Mahatma Gandhi captivated the
world with the word 'Ahiṅsā'.
The true essence of Ahiṅsā extends beyond humans to
encompass all beings, including animals, birds,
insects, etc. Refraining from causing harm to them is
essential because such acts harden and make our
hearts cruel and callous. Such hearts cannot generate
feelings of compassion and kindness.
Understanding Ahiṅsā in its true sense is a prevalent
subject today, but it remains largely incomplete.
While the concept of Para-hiṅsā (harm to others) is
widely understood, the true understanding of the
term Sva-hiṅsa (harm to self) is still lacking.
The actions we perform that cause harm to others
also bind us through those very actions. Due to a lack
of understanding of this fact, we have not understood
the nature of non-violence to self. Because of this, the
discussions of 'world welfare' that we engage in often
neglect our own genuine and spiritual well-being.
The essence of Ahiṅsā encompasses both sva (self)
and para (other) aspects. Considering both these
aspects together is essential for leading a righteous
life. One cannot think about oneself while excluding
others. Similarly, prioritizing others while separating
self is also detrimental in the long run.

270

Life is a hassle

Karma and the laws of the soul are just strict. The
consequences we face are the result of actions we
may have unknowingly committed. Consequences
may vary depending on existence, non-existence and
intensity of a cause. So, it is clear that when we engage
in wrongdoing or unwanted behavior, it ultimately
affects us.
A good book of stories falls on the road. Someone has

return it to its owner. If we don't, we are causing harm
to own-self. This relates closely to the concept of self
and others. This information can be found in Jain

Overall, we realize that the first step towards making

Another thing is that truth is related to non-violence.
Not contemplating or speaking untruths or unworthy
thoughts is essential. Even in untruth, there is the
element of violence. Speaking or acting untruthfully
can cause suffering to others. This is violence towards
others. Similarly, infliction of karmic bondage is selfviolence.
Next, the term 'asteya' means not stealing. Whatever
is not ours, whatever we do not rightfully own, should
not be accepted. Taking anything other than what
rightfully belongs to us is considered theft. Here, the
concept of violence and non-violence comes into play.
Taking something from others that causes them
suffering is violence. Similarly, when we engage in
271

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

actions that result in karmic bondage, it is selfviolence.
The term 'brahmacarya' has a vast meaning. It is not
entirely possible for worldly individuals to follow this
conduct. However, it has been practiced in two ways.
One is not to indulge in lustful thoughts towards
others. Because serving through thought can cause
harm.
Second is, while dealing with one's own partner,
maintaining the practice of abrahmacarya within
limits is necessary. In this Kali Yuga, where people
consider other women as equal, and maintain
boundaries in abrahma practice with their own
wives, they are indeed great souls, a kind of
brahmacāri. The mind, speech, and body are essential
to be restrained. Even if to entertain unworthy
thoughts about women, it is an expression of great
abrahmacarya. Here too, the concept of violence and
non-violence will come into play. It is a matter that
can be understood.
The fifth principle of non-possessiveness, which has
been prescribed as conduct, is a very significant and
understandable thing, and it must be practiced. The
direct and simple meaning of this is that 'we should
not accumulate special collections beyond our needs.'
In this, the concept of non-possessiveness as
explained by Jain scriptures is very understandable.
Today, in this physical world around us, behind the

Humanity desires happiness and peace, and the true

The concept of liberation of the soul is very
significant. Here, we are only talking about physical

vows for householders, which, unless observed, there
is no future for humanity or human existence either.
If these five vows are followed, then there will be no

Think deeply about this matter!
There are five fundamental principles for living.
These are interdependent. Leave one, and the other
principles will start to erode. Hold on to one, and the
others will follow. These five principles are
indivisible and one.
Starting from the top, 'Renounce violence.' Then
falsehood naturally falls away. If falsehood is
abandoned, theft follows suit. If theft is eradicated,
the base of abrahma will disappear. If abrahma goes
away, so does reliance on possessions.

275

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Starting from the bottom, 'Restrict possession.'
Through the clarity of simplicity, the practice of
abrahma diminishes. If the practice of abrahma is
relinquished, the inclination towards theft ceases. If
theft is eliminated, the need for falsehood disappears.
This leads to the manifestation of complete nonviolence.
Reverse the order, 'Violence.' After violence,
falsehood naturally arises. If falsehood emerges, so
does attachment to possessions, abrahma also does
appear on the surface. If attachment to possessions
returns, then theft resurfaces.
These developments lead to a vicious cycle, where
even non-violence and simplicity are lost.
Increasing possession leads to desire. From this, the

falsehood
never
leaves.

everything

Life is a hassle

emerges from this will only be flames; happiness and

Let us revisit our fundamental, essential discussion.
The effort here is to adhere to these five principles:

and non-possession. After ensuring this, a person

In this physical world, the goal should be to focus on

Everyone can't detach themselves from material

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

religious but fundamental and universal principles

for the entire humanity, this is the ultimate cause of
welfare science.
The Jain scriptures also beautifully analyse the

Jain philosophy does not consider this world to be
entirely illusory or deceptive. It is a reality. Only by
being vigilant, can a person clearly define their path
in life and find the treasure of internal happiness. The
complexities of this world cannot obstruct the
gradual development of the soul. Jain scriptures do

consider the role of the sarva-virati essential for
ultimate liberation. To create the base for sarva-virati,
practicing deṡa-virati dharma can be helpful.
Understanding the truth of this matter, Jain scholars
have created a comprehensive presentation of the
path that, intending to facilitate the development of
the soul, significantly contributes to the attainment of

Life is a hassle

the extraordinary heights of Gṛhastha Dharma; the

In the realms of physical and spiritual domains, even

beliefs, or practices, when considering spiritual goals,
the attainment of success, and other aspects from a
holistic perspective, there isn't any absolute
distinction. In big cities, certain long roads are
identified simply by one name but divided into
segments as North and South, like in Mumbai, there is

Road South' in the postal department. As far as the life
and development of a being is concerned, we can
assign the name 'Salvation Road South' and 'Salvation
Road North' to the material domain and spiritual
domain respectively.
Please do not mistake that the enemies of the soul are
not enemies of the body. The body functions until the
soul is connected to it. Therefore, until then, the soul
and the body, relatively speaking, are not separate.
This means that whatever actions the body performs
are the actions of the soul itself. Whatever is wrong in
thought cannot ever be good in action. Similarly, what
is good from a spiritual perspective cannot be
undesirable from a physical perspective.
By the same token, if the principles established in the
physical realm are not complementary to spiritual
development and assistance, then they cannot be
considered good in any physical sense either. If this is
confirmed, then it is the right path. Vyavahāra and the
achievement of the supreme state, i.e., Niṡcaya, are
279

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

parts of the same 'previous half and later half' path.
The goal of both is the same.
Keeping this subject in mind, the Jain philosophers
have described a special form of household duty that
shines brightly beyond the general household duty
for living life. It is a developmental path that Jain
philosophers have shown as a 'ladder of evolution' for
living life. This adds the colour of spirituality to the
material realm. This path gradually leads towards the
direction of the soul's complete development. The
steps of this developmental process have been
identified as fourteen stages. Following these steps,
keeping the time limits of one step after another,
relying on the five reasons, a person bases their own
pursuits. All these matters are comprehensible by

rational; there is nothing abstract in it.
Therefore, the central point of the path of living life is
Syādvāda. The utility of Syādvāda is unique in the
achievement of liberation. The 'path of liberation' is
beyond sensory perception. The conjunction and
disjunction of the soul and karma, along with various
causes, are all beyond sensory knowledge. Where
direct perception of metaphysical knowledge is not
possible, there humans may experience various kinds
of anxieties in their minds, which is natural.
Therefore, contemplating on the first stage of
Guṇasthānaka, the author has given this world the
name House of Perplexity. All these anxieties and
delusions cannot be comprehended intellectually and
are not acceptable to faith. Until then, there is no
progress in the developmental path toward reaching
the soul's ultimate goal.

Life is a hassle

Understanding Syādvāda can lead to the intellectual

Jain philosophers identify the 'Six Enemies' of the

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

battlefield of life. This is the primary goal of every
discerning individual. By raising this awareness, the

mind as our goal, the life we live becomes a 'pure
blissful abode of joy.' Without this aim, life is nothing
but chaos, great chaos.
To escape this great chaos, it is essential to take
refuge in the profound philosophical doctrines of
Syādvāda and Anekāntavāda.
So, we must be sure of this.
"This world is not just an illusion; it is real too. Life
isn't just chaos; it's also immense joy. In both i.e., in
the seemingly chaotic world around us and in our
lives, poison and nectar are both present. By
arranging for the real joy and nectar, we move
forward, and the day when the world appears to be
an illusion, at that time, that illusion will be dispelled
from us for miles and poison will turn into nectar."

CHAPTER 19

Confirmation and Refutation
The purpose of what is written in the following pages
is to provide a concise overview of the main practices
and ideas (principles) of Jainism and Jain philosophy.
Nothing has been written intending to refute one
view or promote another.
This is written solely to disseminate useful
information without the intention of engaging in or
inciting any debate.
The neutrality of Syādvāda is of a unique kind. It does
not involve a false or imaginary conciliatory attitude.
The core of the entire Syādvāda, its crux, the heart and
backbone is that truth should not be unjustly treated,
nor should falsehood be supported. There is no
animosity towards any differing beliefs or opinions.
In this regard, Śrī Haribhadrasūriśvarajī Mahārāja has
stated in verse:
"It is not appropriate to have animosity towards
other scriptures. One should diligently investigate
what they say, and whatever truthful words they
contain are not different from the teachings of the 12
scriptures of Jainism."
The depth or gravity of Syādvāda and its genuine
neutrality is underscored in the above statement.
According to Syādvāda, no statement is inherently
valid or invalid. Whether a statement comes from
283

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

one's own scriptures or another is, it becomes valid
or invalid only through thorough analysis and
examination of the subject matter.
The term confirmation and refutation means debate.
The main purpose of this debate is straightforward
and pure. Differences are an inherent aspect of this
world. According to the doctrine of Karma, just as
wealth
disparities
exist,
intelligence
and
understanding disparities are also present.
Therefore, doubts and disagreements are inevitable
occurrences. However, when two individuals engage
in a face-to-face discussion, the aim should be to
reach a mutual understanding rather than fostering
division. This agreement need not be unanimous;
differences can be acknowledged and negotiated.
Wise individuals have articulated a beautiful insight
on this topic: "When two people sit down to discuss
their differences of opinion, it often leads to
enlightening diversity in their perspectives and
understanding. However, if both approach the
discussion with the mindset of 'I am right, and you are
wrong,' it leads to degradation and decline."
In the West, when intelligent people engage in
discussions, and it becomes impossible to reach a
consensus, they part ways by agreeing on one point,
shaking hands, and wishing each other well. The point
on which they both agree is this:
'We agree to disagree.'
Hence, the purpose of this book is not to incite debate
or controversy. However, should any questions arise

284

Confirmation and Refutation

after reading this book, it is encouraged to address
them.
By exploring these questions and answers, one can
gain deeper insights and appreciation for the subject
matter.
We will address this explanation through a Q&A
format, which will make understanding the questions
and their answers enjoyable.
Question: According to Buddhist philosophy, things
are 'impermanent.' Vedanta philosophy considers
things to be 'eternal.' Jain philosophers have
combined these two views by stating that things are
both eternal and impermanent. What new insight
does this offer? Doesn't it just create confusion?
Answer: If we accept that things are only eternal or
only impermanent, our understanding of the subject
remains incomplete, and we miss out on other truths.
By assuming that something is solely eternal or solely
impermanent, we cannot reach the intended
understanding. Every entity encompasses different
and even contradictory attributes. If a complete
perspective is not attained, understanding remains
incomplete.
In scientific laboratories, microscopic analysis is
conducted to separate and examine things in detail.
Similarly, Jain philosophers have elucidated all the
qualities and attributes of things thoroughly. Nothing
is artificially combined; everything is presented
independently. On the contrary, it is not impossible
that other philosophies, which propose one-sided

285

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

views, have selectively borrowed individual aspects
from Jain philosophy that seemed favorable to them.
Considering a thing to be eternal, disregards its
Paryāyā (transitions), Avasthā (states), and Parimāṇa
(boundaries). Conversely, treating a thing as
impermanent overlooks its fundamental essence,
basic matter is neglected. Therefore, the dual
perspectives presented by Jain philosophers are not
Sanṡayātmaka (doubtful) but rather Niṡcayātmaka
(determinative).
A comprehensive grasp of these dual aspects of
phenomena is crucial for practical application and
ethical conduct. Buddhism and Vedānta, as singular
belief systems, each capture only a portion of the
truth, not its entirety. Denial of one part while
upholding another breeds falsehood. In contrast,
absolute truth is revealed in Anekāntavāda - a
paradigm marked by scientific rigor, self-consistency,
and logical coherence. It is essential to clarify that
concepts like Nityānitya (eternal and non-eternal) are
not mere aggregations of independent qualities but
distinct Anekāntavāda.
It was quite surprising when the followers of Vedānta
opposed this thought. According to them, the first
ever thing that was pure, genuine, and clean was
Brahma. Brahma firstly was conscious in nature. It is
said that from Brahma, Māyā was created. Both,
Brahma and Māyā are elements containing opposite
properties. Brahma is pure and Māyā is impure. If
Māyā has been created from Brahma then its apt
definition would mean that Māyā was Brahma and if
it was, then, in the pure Brahma there was an impure
286

Confirmation and Refutation

element. According to the imagination of Vedānta,
two opposing elements, pure and impure, were
together. However, they do not accept it. If they do,
then, they have to accept Jain philosophy's statement
that 'everything is full of contradictory properties.'
Jain philosophers say that if 'Brahma' is pure
according to Vedānta then, such an impure element of
Māyā, cannot arise from it, and that is why Brahma is
not entirely just a pure form. And if it was a pure form,
then, Māyā wouldn't be born out of it.' We will have to
understand both of these statements. In this way, how
Brahmavādī
vedantī
have
explained
the
interrelationship between Brahma and Māyā, with
pure logic and justice has been proved wrong.
As previously emphasized, employing a calm and
neutral mind will naturally dispel such doubts.
Question: According to Jain philosophers, the
qualities and characteristics of things are infinite.
Why then do they present only seven categories for
analytical understanding?
Answer: Clear comprehension of both Saptabhaṅgi
and Naya resolves this question. Saptabhaṅgi
delineates the nature of objects in terms of Dravya,
Kṣetra, Kāla, Bhāva while Naya dissects the
properties of objects, highlighting their multifaceted
nature.
Saptabhaṅgi exclusively scrutinizes the relative
attributes of each entity. Regarding Naya, it was
previously noted that the seven Nayas depicted are
the primary ones. Furthermore, the multitude of
perspectives matches the countless aspects of
287

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

existence. Skeptical inquiries find resolution through
the seven classifications of Saptabhaṅgi. For instance,
when examining a thing, Sangraha and Vyavahāra
Naya present distinct aspects viz. common and
uncommon. Out of these, Saptabhaṅgi allows analysis
on a particular aspect such as, "this is special and this
is not," and so forth.
Understanding the distinction between Naya and
Saptabhaṅgi dispels many uncertainties. As for the
question of why there are specifically seven
classifications, the answer lies in the absence of
evidence for an eighth category. Skepticism alone,
without substantial evidence, serves no purpose and
can be a negative hindrance. Rejecting propositions
solely for the sake of contention is futile and
counterproductive.
Question: It's understood that the same object can be
deemed good or bad, but how can it also be
considered small or large?
Answer: The classification of an object as small or
large is relative and dependent on comparison. For
example, arrange five fruits—cherry, lemon, orange,
coconut, and watermelon—side by side on a table. A
lemon might seem large compared to a cherry but
small next to an orange. Each fruit's size can be
characterized as small or large based on its placement
within this sequence.
Question: Is Jainism considered a branch of
Hinduism, and if not, what evidence supports this?
Answer: No, that assertion is incorrect. Just as a
father who has five sons would claim one of his sons
288

Confirmation and Refutation

as his own but would not say so for another's son. In
the same way, a tree's branches are integral parts of
the tree itself, not separate entities. No one can say
that these branches do not belong to this tree. If Jain
philosophy were merely a subset of Hindu
philosophy, Hindu scholars would regard it as such,
but this is not the case. Furthermore, labeling Jain
philosophy as 'wrong' denotes a fundamental
disagreement. The belief that Jainism is a branch of
Hinduism stems from a misunderstanding of the facts
and is wholly inaccurate. A logical examination
reveals that Jain philosophy stands as a distinct and
independent philosophical tradition.
A notable divergence between Jain philosophy and
other schools of thought is that while others may
denounce Jainism as incorrect, Jain philosophy
acknowledges partial truths within other systems
rather than dismissing them entirely.
The essence of each philosophy rests on its
perspective. Each philosophy establishes itself based
on a particular viewpoint (Naya) of the object.
Conversely, Jain philosophy's Anekāntavāda is
founded on a comprehensive framework comprising
seven classifications. This unique foundation
underscores that Jainism is distinct from Hinduism or
any other religion.
Similarly, suggesting that Anekāntavāda results from
amalgamating various philosophies is illogical and
inaccurate. While seven garments can be tailored
from a single piece of fabric, they cannot be
seamlessly combined back into one. In the analogy of
a father with seven sons, while they share a common
289

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

lineage, amalgamating them does not transform them
into a singular father. Therefore, it is a logical and just
conclusion that the Anekānta within Jain philosophy
has led to the formation of diverse philosophies with
partial truths that got extracted from it.
The remarkable neutrality of Jain philosophers lie in
their acknowledgment of partial truths within other
philosophical systems without denying their validity.
However, recognizing a part of the truth does not
equate to embracing the whole truth, which is why
Jain philosophers' critique for other systems is not
out of animosity but in pursuit of absolute truth.
Question: There's a notion that Jain philosophy
emerged from combining all religions, suggesting that
earlier visions preceded Jainism. How do we explain
this?
Answer: From a relative perspective, Syādvāda
represents the ultimate synthesis of various
philosophies. However, this does not imply that Jain
philosophy emerged by amalgamating these other
philosophies. Rather, it signifies that the partial
truths found in other philosophies has its own roots
in Anekāntavāda.
To illustrate, although all rivers ultimately flow into
the sea, it does not mean that they collectively form
the ocean. The sea predates the birth of these rivers,
which eventually converge into it. Each river retains
its individual identity even as they merge into the vast
sea.
The metaphor of 'getting absorbed in the sea'
illustrates how rivers merge into the vast expanse of
290

Confirmation and Refutation

the ocean. Similarly, Syādvāda embodies an oceanic
principle, which is neither created nor composed of
the diverse elements that converge within it. Instead,
Syādvāda dissects and delineates the various facets
and forms of existence, potentially serving as the
basis for other philosophical viewpoints derived from
its components. The author does not engage in
debates about whether Jain philosophy emerged
before or after other philosophies; rather, the focus is
on the inherent quality and profoundness of Jain
philosophy. Historical, cultural, and literary evidence
overwhelmingly supports the antiquity of Jain
philosophy. References to Srí Ṛ ṣabhadeva, the first
Tīrthaṅkara of Jains, in ancient texts of Vedanta
philosophy suggest that Srí Ṛ ṣabhadeva predates the
composition of these texts.
Nevertheless, it is a humble suggestion to focus not on
whether a philosophy is old or new, or which came
first and which later, but rather on discovering what
the complete truth is and where it lies. That is the true
path to understanding.
In ancient times, many philosophies that were once
singular have evolved with the development of
understanding, incorporating new elements. For
example, Vedanta and Buddhism have modified some
of their beliefs over time, moving closer to the idea of
Anekāntavāda. On the other hand, the Jain
philosophy, which has been inherently Anekāntavāda
since its ancient origins, has not needed any
modifications or additions. Some eminent scholars
from both Eastern and Western traditions have
acknowledged the originality of Jain philosophy,
291

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

whereas similar recognition is less evident for other
philosophies.
The principle of Anekāntavāda and its philosophical
exposition has been complete and unchanging from
the beginning. There is no necessity for any
modification in this. Given this reality, it can be said
that the complete truth is found solely in the
philosophy of Anekāntavāda in this world.

292

CHAPTER 20

Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra
Following a brief introduction to one of the world's
most esteemed philosophies, a profound gift
bestowed upon humanity by the founders of Jainism,
it would be incomplete to conclude this discourse
without acknowledging one of the religion's most
benevolent maṅtras (sacred verses). Omitting this
would be akin to placing an idol upon its throne
without its crown.
This desired maṅtra is known as the 'Namaskāra
Mahāmaṅtra'.
Since we have mentioned maṅtra here, a little
reflection about 'maṅtra' would not be considered
irrelevant.
Since time immemorial, humanity has constantly
struggled in search of distinct, unique and
remarkable happiness. The primary drive behind this
for an underdeveloped individual is to acquire
material and worldly comforts.
A loving wife, beloved children, a lively family, wealth,
and health as strong as a rock have been his primary
goals. In addition to these, the desire for one's name
to be known, to be respected and revered, to
accomplish something significant and glorious, and to
attain power and prestige also become part of a
person's pursuit of happiness. In pursuit of this goal,
293

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

humans have employed all available means.
Specifically, they have made relentless efforts to
discover and create new means that could facilitate
the attainment of happiness. Among all these various
methods, people have also been attempting to
achieve success through maṅtras since unknown
times. Humans have placed unwavering faith in
certain words or groups of words and their
methodical application, believing they could provide
miraculous success.
What precisely is this maṅtra? Do these maṅtras
possess the attributed power? Is there a plausible and
scientifically explicable force behind them, or do they
transcend human intellect and understanding?
To find answers to these questions, we need to
examine the three important components present in
any maṅtra. These components are as follows:
• The power of words.
• The meaning of the words and the strength of that
meaning.
• The determination power of the creator of the
maṅtra.
After experiencing the infinite strength lying within
the words, proponents who consider words as
eternal spirit, have revered words as 'Ṡabda Brahma.'
When we speak any word, a sound and an effect or
impression are produced. The airwaves capture the
word as it leaves our mouth, allowing it to travel not
only to the listener's ears but also throughout the
entire world.

294

Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra

The sound or vibration produced by the word creates
an impression or effect. Wherever the sound of the
word goes, its impact also follows. The listener's ears
receive the sound of the word, while their intellect
and heart absorb its effect and impression.
The impact and impression of a word depend
significantly on the strength and capability of the
speaker, as well as on the receptiveness of the
listener.
The deeper and more profound the meaning of a
word, the stronger its impact. Words can make people
laugh, cry, sleep, or awaken. The power of the word
itself, combined with the power of its meaning,
accomplishes many such effects.
Regarding the meaning of the words, especially in the
context of a maṅtra, one important aspect is the
power of the deity invoked by the maṅtra. The name
of the entity for whom the maṅtra is composed is
always present in the words of the maṅtra. Therefore,
the capability of the one whose name is mentioned
also plays a significant role.
Following this, the third power of the maṅtra is the
power of determination, of the creator of the maṅtra
i.e., the compiler who prepares the maṅtra. The
maṅtra creator prepares the maṅtra by empowering
the words with their determination. This power of
determination is the most significant element of any
maṅtra.
A maṅtra prepared in this manner occupies a unique
position in the realm of words, akin to a specific kind
of medicine. Different types of maṅtras have emerged
295

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

for various achievements. Its form is scientifically and
logically complete. The accounts of certain results
achieved through maṅtras that we read about are not
mere fabrications. They may involve exaggerations
inherent in human nature, but they are not entirely
unfounded. Even today, many maṅtras can be found
in the texts of maṅtra science.
However, the results of a maṅtra are not achieved
merely through its existence. Even if a person
repeatedly chants a maṅtra, it does not necessarily
become effective. Just as there are three important
factors in the origin of a maṅtra, there are specific
conditions required for its success. The main
conditions are:
i. Faith
ii. Concentration
iii. Perseverance
iv. Procedure
v. Purpose
The foremost requirement among these is faith,
which is not to be misunderstood as blind faith. Jain
philosophers emphasize the equal importance of both
intelligence and faith. They assert that "Intelligence
without faith is promiscuous, and faith without
intelligence is barren." This highlights the notion that
intelligence unguided by faith can lead to harm, while
faith lacking intelligence yields no fruitful results.
The proper balance between faith and intelligence is
essential and well-understood within Jain
philosophy, renowned as one of the most rational
philosophies globally.
296

Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra

To fully grasp the profundity of this remarkable
philosophy, one must commence with faith.
Nevertheless, Jain philosophers, who possess a deep
understanding of their philosophy, advocate: "Come
with faith, and bring along wisdom." They do not
advocate abandoning intellect.
When an individual believes in something through
faith, they become deeply attached to it; however,
conveying this conviction to others solely through
faith can be challenging. When a person gains
intellectual
understanding,
they
not
only
comprehend it themselves but are also capable of
explaining it to others.
Amidst the numerous peculiarities observed in this
world, faith stands out as an exceptionally
outstanding concept. All endeavours worldwide find
success when they pivot around the axis of faith. Any
undertaking devoid of a foundation of faith will fail to
bear fruit or achieve success.
If we were to inquire among a group of individuals, it
would become apparent that most people consider
trust as faith. However, few truly understand the
profound meaning of faith.
In general, people equate faith with 'belief or trust.'
Yet, if you were to ask them about the deeper essence
of faith, many would promptly respond, "No, brother,
there is no one trustworthy in this world." Despite
this skepticism towards the concept of faith, the
entire world's transactions are supported or held up
by faith.

297

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

However, the meaning of faith is deep and profound.
Simply believing and saying, "I have faith in this," is a
very simple, small, and common statement. A person
who merely claims to have faith is not truly
considered faithful. The real test of one's faith is when
it is challenged, and the person is ready to face and
overcome that challenge. A person can only be called
truly faithful if they are willing to sacrifice everything,
even their life, for what they believe in. Mere verbal
expressions of faith, without this deep commitment,
are like a flock of sheep that can be led in any
direction.
So, when it comes to the matter of achieving success
with a maṅtra, the first condition must be of this kind
of complete and unwavering faith. Only faith that is
established and fortified through intellectual
understanding bears fruit. Any other type of faith
does not yield results. Faith that is merely inherited
or followed blindly without questioning - like "this is
how it has always been done" - is not true faith. It is
not true in the sense that such people, when put to the
test, tend to dissolve like bubbles in water.
Another crucial aspect of mastery over the maṅtra is
the concentration of mind. The concentration of the
mind is a formidable force essential for progress in
maṅtra practice. Without it, advancement is
unattainable. Concentration cannot flourish in a mind
prone to constant distractions and electrified various
mental activities. To cultivate the necessary focus,
significant effort is required to establish a definite
and disciplined state of mind. Without this
foundation, achieving mastery over the maṅtra
remains elusive.
298

Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra

The third condition is perseverance, which is a
powerful force in itself. Perseverance is the strength
to stand firm in the face of challenges, adversities, and
hardships. When difficulties arise, when unexpected
obstacles occur, or when the journey takes much
longer than anticipated, one must remain steadfast.
Intellectual
faith,
combined
with
focused
concentration, naturally leads to determination.
However, it is only those who possess the strength to
persist unwaveringly, no matter what happens, and
who refuse to give up on their efforts, that ultimately
achieve success.
The fourth condition pertains to procedure or
methodical practice. This discipline is another potent
strength required for gaining mastery over the
maṅtra. It involves meticulously following the
prescribed procedures of spiritual practice as taught
by knowledgeable mentors. It is vital to recognize
that maṅtras, whether from written sources or
imparted by others, remain dormant until ritually
received from a qualified guru. Only then does the
maṅtra manifest consciousness and its full potential.
Lastly, the fifth condition concerns the purity of
intention in maṅtra practice. How does the intention
behind one's pursuit of mastery the maṅtra influence
its outcome? This question naturally arises when
considering the fusion of faith, intelligence,
concentrated effort, and methodical practice. It is a
fundamental principle that actions devoid of pure
intentions fail to yield lasting fulfillment in this world.
When a person engages in maṅtra practice, the intent
behind it - whether noble and pure or ignoble and
299

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

impure - holds significant importance. If the intent is
impure or malevolent, even if the practice is carried
out with faith, concentration, and perseverance, it
might yield results, but those results are short-lived.
Moreover, such achievements ultimately harm the
practitioner, leading them into a deeper pit of
degradation. On the other hand, when maṅtra
practice is done with a noble and pure intent, it not
only continues to empower the practitioner but also
guides them steadily on a path of continuous wellbeing and spiritual progress.
Conversely, engaging in maṅtra practice for averting
calamities, sustaining one's family, or meeting
material needs is neither auspicious nor categorically
inauspicious. The distinction between 'auspicious'
and 'not inauspicious' intentions holds significant
meaning. However, motives aligned with personal
welfare, devoid of the intent to inflict pain or harm
upon others, cannot be labeled as 'impure.'
Furthermore, contemplating the concepts of
'inauspicious' versus 'not inauspicious' intentions
prompts reflection on what qualifies as auspicious or
pure.
A new perspective emerges as we gradually assess
life's purposes or goals based on utility. By shifting
focus away from transient pursuits, our
contemplation converges towards the eternal
essence - the soul. Through such contemplation,
examining the historical and philosophical
complexities that surround us, we arrive at the
realization that life's ultimate objective transcends

300

Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra

mere physical existence and wealth; it centers on the
liberation of the soul.
Here, when we discuss intentions that are unvirtuous
versus not unvirtuous, it's important to also reflect on
what we consider virtuous or pure. As we gradually
contemplate the goals or purposes of life from a
utilitarian perspective, we will uncover new insights.
Through this reflection, by examining the
impermanent phenomena and focusing on the
eternal, we will eventually direct our thoughts
toward the eternal essence of the soul, which is
unchanging and enduring.
As we continue this process, analysing the historical
events of the world and delving into the philosophical
exploration of the continuous stream of occurrences
around us, we will ultimately arrive at the conclusion
that the ultimate goal of life is not the body or wealth,
but the soul and its liberation. Wise and
understanding individuals think beyond short-term
benefits, considering the long-term implications of
their actions. A plan or thought process lacking
foresight may provide immediate benefits, but it is
unlikely to be truly beneficial in the long run.
Therefore, whenever we set our goal, we should plan
our lives with the final emancipation of the soul at the
center. The aspiration for the welfare of the entire
world is intrinsically linked to the welfare of the soul.
Thus, any maṅtra practice performed for the
liberation of the soul, for the ultimate release of the
soul, and in alignment with the welfare of others, is
considered pure and virtuous, as it is devoid of any
materialistic desires or selfish motives.
301

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

Now, let's continue our exploration of maṅtra
practice.
By embracing the five conditions mentioned earlier
i.e., beginning with pure intention, receiving guidance
from a knowledgeable mentor, demonstrating
unwavering
determination,
maintaining
full
concentration, and exercising faith along with
intelligence, a person can achieve mastery over the
maṅtra as prescribed in the scriptures.
However, it's important not to assume that
possessing these five elements alone guarantees
fruitful mastery. An essential question arises here i.e.,
the selection of the maṅtra. As discussed earlier, the
pursuit of a materialistic life devoid of spiritual
progress does not lead to true happiness; instead, it
often breeds various sorrows. Similarly, maṅtras
crafted solely to attain worldly happiness often yield
unintended consequences.
In the previous pages, we have observed that any
materialistic life path that does not lead us toward
spiritual progress is not a true life-path. Such a path
does not bring genuine happiness because the
pleasures derived from it often give rise to various
other sorrows, both small and large.
From this, it is clear that while selecting a maṅtra, one
should use complete discernment and wisdom. The
maṅtra should not only be 'bestower of all
achievements' but should also possess a specific
power to manifest and strengthen the practitioner's
mental and spiritual emotions.
The maṅtra should possess the power to
automatically remove the practitioner's deficiencies,
302

Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra

purify the impurities of the practice itself, and also
purify and make the goal auspicious. It should be a
maṅtra that, like a philosopher's stone, transforms
the base into gold upon contact and can control the
practitioner, the practice, and the goal through its
inherent power alone, being self-achieved and selfregulating.
Among such suitable maṅtras, if there is any that
stands out as the best in this world, it is the
'Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra.' This great maṅtra is
honoured with the title of King of maṅtras.
Though this maṅtra, with its simple words and
straightforward meaning, may not initially seem very
impressive or extraordinary to the ordinary person,
it carries profound significance.
When a small child takes their first steps in school,
they start by learning the basic letters. Once these
basics are mastered, who remembers them in youth
or adulthood? Yet, who doesn't know that these
fundamental letters form the foundation of
extraordinary knowledge?
The religious education for Jain children begins with
the 'Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra.' This maṅtra possesses
a power that far exceeds the basic letters of the
physical world by millions of times. This realization
comes from thoughtful reflection and guidance from
a knowledgeable guru.
This maṅtra is a self-acclaimed supreme maṅtra. It
has the power to transform the practitioner, purify
the means, and grant inherent knowledge of the goal.
However, when does this occur?
303

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

The initial condition is to get ready with an
understanding of the unparalleled potential
embedded within the maṅtra.
Now, let's analyse the wording of this maṅtra:
"Namo Arihaṅtāṇam
Namo Siddhāṇam
Namo Āyariyāṇam
Namo Uvajjhāyāṇam
Namo Loe Savva Sāhuṇam
Eso Paṇca Namukkāro
Savva Pāvappaṇāsaṇo
Mangalāṇam Ca Savvesim
Padhamam Havai Mangalam"
At first glance, do these sentences appear unusual? In
the first five lines (padas), we offer salutations
(namo) to the five Parmesṭhī (noblest elements) viz.
Arihaṅta, Siddha, Ācārya, Upādhyāya, and Sarva
Sādhu. The subsequent four lines (padas)
demonstrate the fruits and effects of these
salutations, encapsulated within the 'Namaskāra
Mahāmaṅtra' itself.
The straightforward meaning of these nine verses is
as follows:
1. Salutations to the Arihaṅta (the supreme souls and
the promoters of the true path)
2. Salutations to the Siddha (the purified souls)
3. Salutations to the Ā cārya (the directors or
governors of the true path)
304

Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra

4. Salutations to the Upādhyāya (the teachers of texts
related to the true path)
5. Salutations to all the Sādhu (the monks and nuns
walking on the true path)
6. Salutations to these five
7. Are the destroyer of all sins
8. And among all auspicious things
9. this is the first auspicious thing.
It is not possible to have an idea about how serious
this maṅtra, which looks quite simple at the first
glance. However, before considering the deep and
subtle significance it contains, let us see what those
who have had a partial taste of this Mahāmaṅtra have
to say on the subject. Start with faith. We will later
embellish that faith with intelligence.
"This Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra is the epitome of all
scriptures. This maṅtra, is amazingly impressive. It
fulfills all desires. This maṅtra removes all ailments.
All kinds of mishaps and calamities are destroyed by
the effect of this maṅtra. This maṅtra is auspicious in
the ocean-like world, more wish-fulfilling than the
Cintāmaṇi Ratna, Kalpavṛkṣa and Kāmadhenu, and the
creator of something from nothing. All kinds of sins
are destroyed by chanting this maṅtra. This maṅtra
brings happiness to this world hereafter and also in
next life. Nirjarā of Karma is possible by this maṅtra.
The sins of our endless lives are washed away by this
maṅtra, our soul is saved from the grievous pains of
terrible plight. A soul that has become pure and holy
after being freed from the bonds of karma, manifests
its pure form and attains pure knowledge only
through the influence of this maṅtra."
305

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

"Remembrance in the morning makes the day
supremely happy. If this maṅtra is recited at the time
of birth to a newborn child, his entire life will be filled
with supreme success and success. If it is recited at
the time of death, the soul then attains better state."
"This maṅtra gives a required amount of happiness to
all. This maṅtra, self-conceived, has the high and
excellent power of bestowing all kinds of
achievements. It is the all-conquering maṅtra."
The powers and achievements of this 'Namaskāra
Mahāmaṅtra' have been explained since time
immemorial.
The esteemed experienced scholars have written
extensively about the powers encapsulated within
each letter of this maṅtra so much so that the life of
even hundred years won't seem sufficient to fully
comprehend it.
What does this maṅtra contain?
How do I begin to describe it?
Attempting to encapsulate even a fraction of the vast
ocean of wisdom contained within this is a challenge
beyond words. Primarily, it requires firsthand
experience. To gain even a glimpse of its essence,
individuals must seek the company of monks who
have delved into its depths. Seek out the existing Jain
Ācāryas or monks, approach them with faith and
curiosity, and you will not be disappointed.
The word 'namo' within this Mahāmaṅtra demands
extraordinary qualification from us to truly embody
its meaning. It is not merely a salutation. Bowing is
only meaningful if I truly believe myself worthy of
306

Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra

saluting. This self-assuredness is a unique and
elevated qualification. Until we completely shed the
ego that resides within us - whether knowingly or
unknowingly, at all times and in all places - true and
pure salutation cannot manifest within us.
When will this realization occur?
It will happen when our understanding aligns with
the virtues and influences of those whom we
contemplate saluting. This spirit will then reveal
itself. So, whom are we saluting in this maṅtra? By
addressing the Arihaṅta, Siddha, Ā cārya, Upādhyāya,
and all the Sādhus, we pay homage to the supreme
essence.
We bow down to Arihaṅta, who has defeated all
adversities like attachments and aversions.
We extend our salutations to all the Siddha who have
achieved purity by eradicating all karmas, to the
esteemed Ācāryas who impart true knowledge of this
path, and to the Upādhyāya whose teachings have
illuminated our minds on this journey, and to the
Sādhu who have been walking on the path of
salvation.
Given the constraints of space, time, and energy to
fully express the unique qualities of these five
supreme elements, here, the author humbly suggests
that engaging in conversation with Jain Ācāryas can
provide significant information, more enlightenment
on this.
However, it is crucial to remember that this
'Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra' stands alone in this world,
purifying and elevating all desires of the human body,
307

Anekāntavāda: The Heart of Jainism

mind, and wealth. By focusing solely on this
magnificent jewel of a maṅtra, it takes upon itself the
responsibility for the entirety of the practitioner's
life.
For those who surrender to the Namaskāra
Mahāmaṅtra, it serves as the Father, the Guru, the
God, the Dharma, the Redeemer, and the Guiding Star.
It consciously fulfills all material desires, wards off all
dangers, and safeguards not only the seeker's body
and soul but also their family, leading them to
complete and eternal happiness. You stand on the
banks of a Lake of Nectar, with nectar visible right
before your eyes. It lovingly beckons you to partake
and receive blessings.
What is the benefit of merely standing by?
Plunge your hand into it, taste a few drops on your
tongue, and experience the sheer joy of what unfolds
next!
Before we part with auspicious words like 'see you
soon,' let us take a moment to meditate on our desired
maṅtra by closing our eyes for two minutes.
------------------------After two minutes of silence, let us hold in our hearts
two profound sentiments:
Khāmemi savva jíve, savve jíva khamaṅtu me
Mitti me savva bhūesu, veram majjam na keṇai
I forgive all living beings, may all living beings forgive
me. I have friendship with all and enmity with none.
308

Namaskāra Mahāmaṅtra

Ṡivamastu sarvajagatah,
parahitaniratā bhavaṅtu bhūtaganah
Doṣāh prayāṅtu nāṡam,
sarvatra sukhibhavatu lokah
May there be peace in the entire universe, may all
beings be engaged in each other's welfare. May faults
be eradicated, may everyone be happy everywhere.
See you soon!
